THE RED PAGE
The Innocence of Father Brown —G. K.
Chesterton ( Sydney Book Club). The
fat little priest of these stories is some-
thing new in the detective line. Though
Chesterton does not play the unravel-
ling game any more fairly than Maurice
Leblanc in some of the Arsbne Lupin
series, this book is full of good ideas
and worth reading. For the first time, de-
tective stories are used as a medium for
religious propaganda.
The Missing Finger —-Albert Boissi&re
(Dodd, Mead and Co., per Angus and
Robertson). A clever mystery stoi-y,
although it depends upon the good old
twins who cannot be distinguished
apart.
The Skipper and the Skipped— Holman Day
(E. W. Cole). There is an abundance
of American humor in this “shore log of
Cap’n Aaron Sproul.”
The Land of Veiled Women —J. Foster
Fraser (Cassell and Co.). The pictur-
esque aspects of life in Algeria, Tunisia
and Morocco have been skimmed in ex-
cellent fashion for the casual l-eader,
to whom accuracy is of little moment.
F.F. is a smart journalist, and knows
how to hit the public with bright stuff
at the -right time.
THE QUEST.
I have followed her ever—
From boyhood’s tangled wilderness
Or fairy tale and wondrous story,
Where I touched the hem of her dress,
Once—when the white-winged glory
Of a great star hung a-quiver
O’er the midnight river—
O’er a blurred white face in the river,
And I felt the silence shiver
As she passed me —whispering.
I followed her onward ever
To the realm of madness and fire,
To the kingdom of parched desire,
Where I was king.
And I heard the silence sing
When my lips—tor a moment —were swept
by her hair,
Blown like sunshine everywhere
About an awe-struck world ;
But, alas! for longing my heart was dumb,
For very passion my arms were numb;
And she glanced—and left me there.
But I followed her still for ever,
Through manhood’s thronging years
Of phantom loves and unwept tears,
And the anguished scarlet of lips defiled,
That could not pray—yet smiled,
Till I found a trace
Once more of her grace
In the beautiful face
Of my child.
I shall follow her onward for ever
Through the great white gates of death —-
To her luminous palace of death ;
And, when I find her,
Mv passion shall mnd her,
Heart to heart—breath to breath—
Fierce kisses shall blind her
Blue eyes to all ruth
For the fatherless child, and (h. wiuowed
wife,
And the wasted life,
That was wasted—for Truth.
N.S.W.
D.
Hans Heysen: A Great Water-
Color Painter
Hans Heysen is in more than one respect
the most remarkable landscape painter in
Australia. -He is probably the only artist on
this great continent who neither lives by
journalism nor by teaching. It seems to
me, something astounding that an artist
should live by his art in Australia, should
paint exactly what he likes —-and Sell it.
Yet Heysen has two years’ commissions
ahead of him. ,
It is a pleasure to think that merit can
be so equitably rewarded. The only reasons
I caii adduce for the phenomenon are that
his art is noble, sincere and intimately Aus-
tralian, that Melbourne possesses art-
patrons of taste and discrimination, and
that he is one of the most brilliant water-
colorists living.
* * * *
Heysen was born in Hamburg, Germany,
in 1878, and came to Australia with his
parents at the age of seven. His father took
up a selection outside Adelaide, and knew all
the ups and downs —particularly the downs
—connected with “the land,” and the boy, to
assist his parents financially, drove a cart,
and worked for some time in an ironmongery
store. He was 15 when his first interest in
art awakened. A lithograph of some white
swans throwing their reflection into very
blue water seemed to him both beautiful and
delectable, and, his mother assisting, he con-
structed a first palette of Prussian blue,
chrome yellow, brown and white. The re-
sult can be imagined easily. From thence
the fascination of color possessed the boy.
His only opportunities were on Sundays, and
he was always up before dawn, with tucker
enough for the day, and away to the hills.
His work was careful, a kind of indiscrim-
inate pre-raphaelitism, but it gradually im-
proved under the tuition of Mr. Gill, at the
School of Design, where he worked at night.
Some sketches offered modestly for sale at a
dealer’s shop sold at once, and the young
painter established a market. He was soon
doing so well that four Adelaide gentlemen
thought him likely to spoil his hand, so they
put their purses together and sent him to
Europe, with the proviso that he was to re-
tain his sketches, but that his finished work
should go to them. Heysen put in four
years abroad, studying hard. He worked
first at Julien’s, Paris, under Jean Paul
Laurens and Benjamin Constant, after-
wards at Beaux Arts under Bonnat. His
evenings were spent at Callarrossi’s, Where
Prenet, Colin and Tournes criticised. He
spent all this time drawing from the figure,
and only painted in the summer months,
which were spent in Scotland, Holland, Eng-
land and Italy. Of all the provincial
Italian art centres where he worked, Heysen
found Florence the most interesting. To-
wards the end of 1903 he returned to Aus-
tralia. The Sydney, Melbourne and Ade-
laide Galleries acquired pictures by him, and
he established himself at Ilahndorf, the little
German village near Adelaide, where he finds
the life simple, and the big gums his truest
source of inspiration. His main support
has come from Melbourne, and his chief big
“imperial” sized watercolors have been ac-
quired by Sir Thomas Gibson Carmichael,
Professor Baldwin Spencer, Mr. Walter
Schmidt and Madame Melba.
Since lie returned from Europe, Heysen
has been fascinated with the painting of
Australian light. In all his letters to me
the subject recurs. Referring to a large
water-color he was recently at work on. he
writes: “I am trying to get into it all the
HANS HEYSEN.
light and heat I can, raising it some degrees
higher in key than my Sydney water-color.
But to give expression fo that intangible
stuff, light and atmosphere, is indeed a
problem, yet I feel these to be the essence
of Australian Landscape! And they are
what make nature so various and fascinat-
ing here. Sunshine above all is the essence
of life —and air and atmosphere close on its
heels. To give these with an arrangement of
beautiful and truthful forms, should make
beautiful pictures. Ah, well, we can only
have a try, but it’s fine to dream about it.”
On seeing his water-color in the Mel-
bourne Gallery last November, he wrote:
“I was very disappointed—-in fact, I could
hardly believe my eyes—the whole drawing
seemed so devoid of light, the very thing I
had always thought it gave an impi-ession
of. I hope I have got past this stage for
ever.”
Writing of another work —“Timber Haul-
ing’’—which was recently acquired by the
Sydney Gallery, he says : “I think it nqw
contains more serial color, at the same time
retaining a certain bigness of form. I find
the great problem is to keep, the same bal-
ance between these two. To aim at the
vibi’ation of light generally means destroying
the dignity of a picture. You may take
nearly any one of even the biggest impres-
sionists —Sisley, Monet, Pissarro, and in
isolated pictures only have they retained
nature’s dignity or the deeper character of
forms. Judging by reproductions of Monet’s
latest works, I seem to feel he is coming
back to the above point of view. In looking
at a picture one wants to see and feel
nature first —not method It requires
more than a correct portrayal of forms to
paint a country. The heart must be there.
Without feeling how can a work be con-
vincing?”
I have collated these passages from Hey-
sen’s letters carefully, because they contain
the whole of his aesthetic. He has swallowed
tradition wholesale—Poussin, Millet, Turner.
He has served an apprenticeship to the ana-
lytical methods of the impressionists-—he
has an extraordinary knowledge of the re-
action of colors, and upon this double foun-
dation his art has developed. His fine
powers of observation and love of nature
have freed him frpm both eclecticism and
science, and his work is to-day characteris-
tic and original, as sincere and dignified as
the man himself. To have seen the beautiful
set of water-colors recently exhibited by
Heysen at the R.A.S., Sydney, was to know
that though Manet was right when he said
that the principal person in a picture was
the light, yet that without emotional com-
munication nothing counts. These “Hymns
tq the Sun” —offerings of a devout Apol-
lonian —were charged with emotional beauty,
the splendor of the technique had but accen-
tuated the splendor of the painter’s vision.
For Hans Heysen is not only master of a
very difficult medium, he is, in my opinion,
a very great artist, and to know the man,
straightforward and sincere, content with
his bread and his art, is to account—if ever
genius can be accounted for—in part at
least for the nobility and \sweetness of his
work.
LIONEL LINSAY.
Short Circuit
From the wide and open marble upper ter-
race of the Hotel of the Stairs of Iron
they looked down upon the hotel garden—
once the ramparts of a fort which dominated
the Bay of Cagliari ere the Mediterranean
retreated from the Capital of Sardinia and
left the fort as a steep rock marooned half a
mile inland. And on the dock a century
ago they builded the Albergo della Scali di
Ferro.
The scents of the garden ascended to the
man and woman oh the terrace 50 feet above
the trees—magnolia, gardenia, oleander,
frangipanni; the moon rose out of Italy
saturating the air with light, falling in a
shower upon the statue of the Verrochio boy
clasping the struggling dolphin that spouted
a stream of tinkling silver to the fountain
basin. And south there were but the rid-
ing lights of' ships and the place where the
tunny fishers go, and the quiet breathing of
the sea that was like moon-blur and shadow,
and lastly, and invisible, the coast of Africa.
It was all beautiful and quiet and elo-
quent of scented rest; that quietness that
asks only Love —even the fraudulent imita-
tion of Love—to make it electric.
The woman had the face of a pretty and
clever child—small-nosed, small-mouthed,
large-eyed, full with laughter; skin as if she
had been to honey and roses for tints.
The man doesn’t matter—he was ordinary
type: Say, Long Primer, Elephant Expanded
—by which a printer' would understand that
he was fairly tall and more than a little
over-stout.
A white peacock came spreading his tail
and holding hign his crest —saw that the
chairs Were very* close together—and van-
ished around the corner of the loggia again,
and became a ghost in the shadow.
Sex seemed to become more and more an
accumulator of the electric currents of the
night; the aggregation of the noises of in-
sects, of distant music and, of the tinkling
fountain basin made the air give out the
humming sounds of a great dynamo.
“You know quite a deal about poetry,”
murmured the woman, as if more than mere
poetry obsessed her and made her almost
tongue-tied.
“I know more about electrics,” laughed
the* man, more at his ease, but yet affected
by the fulness of the night and the proximity
of the woman,
“There has to be imagination in that,
too, hasn’t there?”
He laughed. “Yes! I suppose that even
a bricklayer of imagination would lay bricks
better than a dull man of the same trade.”
“But laws govern that more than they do
poetry.”
"No, I think not —isn’t it an axiom that
poetry is the exquisite expression of an ex-
quisite impression? And that’s a law.”
“And what is the base of the law of
bricklaying?”
“I don’t know —to lay the brick squarely
and not be sloppy with the mortar, I sup-
pose. And of electrics? Well, I suppose C
equals E over R.”
What does that cryptic sentence mean?”
“It means that Current equals electro
motor force divided by the Resistance.”
“You’re beyond my depth, now. By the
way, isn’t is strange that nobody ever ad-
dresses you as Nox-man Strong, Esq., or Mr.
Strong, but just Norman Strong?”
“A habit not worth speculating on, is it?”
“Of course, these Italians address you as
Ingegnei’e Illustrissimo.”
Illustrious Engineer! But I don’t think
they mean it.”
“I’m sure they do. You are illustrious.”
“I would like you to think me so.” She
laughed nervously.
“Now, you mustn’t hold my hand. . . .
Look at the beauty of the night—the air
saturated with light.”
“The moon-rays like a sea drowning the
air.”
“It seems too beautiful to breathe.”
“Yes. ... What are they playing on the
Marina? Chopin’s Mai-che Funebre? Beau-
tiful.”
“I don’t care a great deal for it. The
first movement of it is natural, but aboriginal
—aboriginal enough to be Irish, and the
second is almost.-machine-made.”
“Yes —a little trivial! I can’t disagree
with anything you say to-night. ... Oh!
really don’t hold my hand. I meant to cool
it on the balustrade. . . . How cold marble
is.”
“But marble is only a form of limestone,
and in all limestone there is fire.”
“How?”
“Burn it—mix it with water, and it con-
sumes itself with heat.”
“Now we’re becoming too chemical.”
“All processes are matters of chemistry
—even love.”
“Love ? . . . Oh! let us get back to poetry.
.. . Now! Please! O^i! don’t hold my
hand. . . . Do you like Swinburne?”
“Yes.”
“I’m so glad. So do I. What do yon
most like of his?”
“So we kissed beneath the pale, cold moon.
Ah, God! Ah, God! that day should come
so soon.”
“I don’t like that so much—it seems too
gross, too fleshy.”
"Call not anything I have created common
or unclean.” ,
“Oh! I know I can’t argue with you. I’m
not clever enough.”
“There’s a little curl fallen on your neck.”
“Thank you ; I’ll put it up.”
“No, no, don’t; I tike it so.”
“Why? For what?”
“For that.”
“Oh —-please-—someone might have seen
you kiss my neck.”
“No —only the peacock.”
“He’s gone away shocked. You know
you’re right about Chopin. He is trivial.”
“For dignity in grief, majesty in sorrow,
there are only Beethoven and Handel.”
Yes, “Ombra mai fu’ is a heartbreak, but
dignified.”
“You can’t imagine Handel cxying himself
into a red nose even if all his family died.
But a,s to Swinburne.”
“Oh ! The Spring Song; you know it?”
“I know I love you.”
“Oh ! S-s-h. Ah ! don’t —don’t kiss me
so. . . . Ah, don’t make me afraid of you.
. . . You know the beautiful Spring Song?
Now. Ah! Don’t kiss me. I thought you
were a gentleman. ... Oh! Do behave!
I’ll hold your hands'... Yes, dear I know,
but cruel to be kind Listen, dear boy-
listen —The Spring Song.”
“And a bird overhead sang ‘follow.’ ”
"Ah! Don’t kiss me so. lam faint.”
“... .And a bird to the right sang ‘here .’”
“All! Do not torture me.”
“And the arch of the leaves was hollow.”
“Oh! Be good, be nice.”
“And the meaning of Spring was clear.”
“Ah! My God! Norman you must be
mad.” .. j ,
The moonlight seemed to crash upon them
in billows of making and breaking light;
the white peacock blushed in the shadow of
the tamarisks.
Current equals electro motor force di*
yided by the resistance.
RANDOPH BEDFORD.
E. Dyson : It has come. Ever since I
gulped a full dose of O. Henry, which hap-
pened only a little time before the death of
the excellent American, I have been waiting
for the accusation, and “J.E.” gets it in—B..
Sept. 14. I am a disciple of the grower of
“Cabhages and Kings.” The fact dawned
upon me when I first read O. Henry, that,
for 15 years at least, I had been imitating
him by a process of unconscious cerebration.
My tricks, wiles and devices were all cribbed
from 0. Henry some years before his first
public appearance. I recognised them at
every turn in “The Four Million.” “Factory
’Ands” and “Benno” are fill of proofs of
crime such as my pleasant critic has picked
from “An Ornament of the Bar.” As for
Rex Beach, I have never read him, and shall
do so with the utmost apprehension, dreading
to have to further incriminate myself as one
in possession of literary property reasonably
supposed to have been stolen.
* * * *
The Concise Oxford Dictionary of Cur-
rent English, adapted by H. W. and F. G.
Fowler from the great Oxford Dictionary 90
far as the latter is completed, is the best bf
small dictionaries yet issued. Common words
receive more space than usual, their shades
of meaning being given with illustrative sen-
tences ; and the spelling is consistent with
usage. Colloquial terms have been admitted,
and a few words of Australian origin such
as “larrikin” (which has passed into general
use, and appeared recently in Belloc’s The
Girondin ), “coo-ee” and “gunyah.” In a
few cases, sueh as “squatter,” the special
Australian definition is given. It could not
be expected that slang and local usages
would be comprehensively treated in a book
of this kind. There is still room for a re-
vised edition of Morris’s Austral-English at
the price of this Concise Oxford Dictionary,
vis.,' 4s.
* * * *
In the notice of Alfred Searcy’s By Flood
and Field (Red Page, 14/9/T1) an error
was made in the price quoted.. The book is
sold at 55., not 3s. 6d.
They still work off these gags against the
“hot understood novelist”
After reading one of Henry James’s latest
stories for a while, what a relief it is to turn
to the lucid simplicity of The Critique of
Pure Reason, by Immanuel Kant. — Boston
Globe.

Testimony received from Capt. H. E. U. BURKE, late
3rd King’s African Rifles, British E. Africa, and
Assistant Master, Sydney Grammar School, College
Street, Sydney. States his opinion of what Clements
Tonic can do in cases of Fever, Wasting Illness, Loss
of Sleep or Appetite, Indigestion, or Convalescence
after severe illness.
“ The Cottage,” 250 Ocean Street,
Paddington, 17/1/Tl.
CLEMENTS TONIC LTD.
“ I have benefited much by your medicine, principa ly in Africa
where it was first given to me by an Australian, after a severe attack of
malafial fever. I was taken to Nairobi, the centre of Govt, in British
E. Africa, in a state of collapse. The greatest care had to be taken
during mv convalescence.
“My Australian friend gave me three bottles. It improved my
appetite, creating desire for food, the beginning of a general restoration
to health, transforming a condition of extreme debility to one of
splendid strength.
“ I was so impressed with the medicine that I recommended it to
my brother officers. I have cause to be very grateful to this excellent
medicine, and, as I am now in Sydney, I am only fulfilling a duty in
testifying to its w r orth. You may use this letter as you like.”
(Signed) H. E. U. BURKE (Capt.).

VARIUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Psalm of Life.
With a book, and a pipe, and a willow tree,
And a breeze to ripple the pages,
I feel I am great, I know I am free,
The king of the marching ages.
My pipe may be empty, but Cissy knows
Exactly the way to fill it,
And if in these pastures a microbe grows
The sweet blue smoke will kill it.
Her hair is rippling round her face
In exquisite, rich disorder,
And the dancing leaves on her white frock trace
A delicate lace-work border.
Is there any lack of a bottle of wine?
Though Omar might deride us,
The billy we boiled, with lid ashine,
Stands there on the grass beside us.
Instead of a simple loaf of bread,
Or a rocky chunk of damper,
Cissy and I have richly fed
On sandwiches from the hamper.
Little we reck of bale or bliss,
Or the problems that lick the sages,
If the sun kept warm, we could lie like this
And be glad for a dozen ages.
The statesman furrows his brow with care,
And the poet sweats at his riming,
And some idiot tries to patrol the air,
Up the stairs of the. breezes climbing.
And another is mad upon raising spooks,
And gathers his proofs in bunches,
While some, with dishevelled and downcast looks,
Are hunting for counter-lunches.
We hold that the world is undoubtedly queer,
But we never ask its meaning,
For my world is the sunshine around me clear,
And the girl against me leaning.
I wouldn’t take on the job to make
A better earth than this is,
With the sun ashine, and the right to take
A sufficient number of kisses.
For Cissy is really a wondrous girl,
And I bless the day I met her,
I wouldn’t exchange her smallest curl
For all that the saints hold better.
N.S.W.
D.M.W.
Heroes.
In the Streets of Little Ease
There were shouting, laughter, cheers,
In a back-room on her knees
Wailed a woman wan with tears.
Bands were playing in the street,
“See the Conquering Hero Comes!”
Urchins sped with naked feet
At-the rumble of the drums. -
Soldiers, splendid in their pride,
Thrilled with conscious majesty,
Triumph in their measured stride,
Marched, a glittering panoply.
In the richest trappings, dressed,
As befitting one that bore
Many medals on his breast,
Rode the demi-god of war.
He, astride a chestnut cob,
With a light hand on the rein,
Never smiled, withal the mob
Shouted, cheered and cheered again.
Through the streets of Little Ease
Eager people followed them; .
Lone, the woman on her knees,
Wept a lonely requiem.
AVhen the splendid pageant passed,
Lo! a dark procession came,
Like a gloomy thought that fast
Followed in the wake of Fame.
Half a dozen wretched folk
Trailed behind the sable coach
Sullenly, and never spoke,
But their eyes were a reproach.
He that held the reins was named
Death, and hasting slowly, he,
And the mutes looked half ashamed
Of such motley company.
He within had fought for years,
Nameless, an heroic past....
Wondering to hear the cheers,
Gloomily they followed fast.
N.s.w.
W. M. WHITNEY.
A Dream.
I had a dream. I stood alone
It seemed, before the Great White Throne.
The Judge upon me bent Ilis look ;
The knees of my poor spirit shook.
An Angel vast stood near by me
To read out my biography.
Great awe and terror on me fell
Before he spake a syllable.
I knew the black and dreadful screed
That in a moment he would read.
“O Lord,” I cried, “let me explain”—
But these excuses were in vain.
The Judge said —“What was his chief crime?”
“He wrote, O Lord, a book of rhyme.”
The Judge, while Gabriel looked grim,
Said —“Much may be forgiven him.
“When I, who made the Cosmos grand,
And you and him, took first in hand
“Creation as a steady thing,
I made the stars together sing.
“You see, we always so begin—
It is a very venial sin—■
“In fact, a sin of no amount;
So, Gabriel, cross out his account.”
And Gabriel did, and I was free
Of Heaven for Eternity.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to Tiie Bulletin would oblige the
paper (and, to an extent, themselves) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. “The Editor, The Bulletin," is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official always is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be re-
fused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
part of the contents of this number is hereby ex-
pressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where prac-
ticable, acknowledge in the “Correspondence” col-
umn all contributions submitted, whether in the
form of Political, Social or other Articles, Verse,
Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially accept-
able), Paragraphs, Letters or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS.
when the address is specified and stamps are en-
closed. But no MS. will be returned, or preserved,
unless these conditions are observed. When MS. is
forwarded without stamps for return it will be un-
derstood that the copy is to be destroyed if not ac-
ceptable. Personal applications for return of MS.
cannot receive attention. The Bulletin desires,
further, those sending MS. to distinctly understand
that it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

THURSDAY, SEPTEMBER 28, 1911.
THE DEPARTURE (THANK THE LORD!)
OF FOREIGN CAPITAL.
The Australian Corporation of Public Ac-
countants sat in solemn conclave a few days
ago at Adelaide, and the president, one T.
Brentnall, from Melbourne, heaved him-
self up to speak words of warning into the
bowl-like ear of this day and generation.
These are some of the words: —
It is too soon yet to estimate the full effect of
the Federal land-tax, but it is a significant fact that
since its imposition a large number of valuable pro-
perties have* been *sold, and in most cases the money
has gone out of the country, and will not be re-
invested here ... In these days of abounding pros-
perity one hardly dares to sound a pessimistic note,
yet those who recall the crisis of 1893 are somewhat
sharply reminded of many similar conditions.
It is well to have a few pessimists in the
community lest Jeshurun should wax fat
and kick out of his turn, yet T. Brentnall
seems hardly the right man for the job. It
is quite true, as he remarks, that there are
many points of resemblance between the cir-
cumstances of 1911 and those of the years
just before 1893. There is the same joyous
confidence, the same vast expansion of trade
including an absurd increase of imports, the
same hilarious whooping-up of the price of
land, the same great and notable rise in
bank deposits, and, most unfortunately, the
same mania for encouraging the Public Debt.
So far, the resemblance is complete, but
after that it begins to break down. There
isn’t the same crop of shoddy land banks—
not yet, at all events. The cash reserves of
the legitimate banks are very much larger,
in proportion to the call liabilities, than
they were just before the bottom fell out of
all creation, hereabouts, 18 years ago. Above
all things, there is now the holy and blessed
and remembersome circumstance pointed out
by T. Bkentnall and other Tories, that
capital—the capital of the absentee investor,
that is—is leaving the country, and has been
doing so for a number of years. So many
Conservatives who are honorable men —the
same as Bkentnall and Brutus —have at-
tested to this departure of capital that it is
impossible to doubt it. The exit has not
been sudden but continuous, yet instead of
Australian financial institutions closing up
through inability to meet the demand, they
have paid all lawful claims quite cheerfully,
and instead of the drain reducing their re-
sources their casli assets have increased.
The latest recorded flight is the capital of
the awful Van Diemen’s Land Co., whose
wasted acres are now passing to an Austra-
lian syndicate; and a good deal of the
Coopek wealth is also departing now that
these chronic stayers-away are driven to sell
their property to small local holders. Thank
the Lord, foreign capital is leaving the
country! That is to say, a great deal of the
principal is leaving, wherefore the constant
departure of the interest is coming to an
end. So. (again thank the Lord!) Austra-
lian capital is beginning to stay in the
country for the first time in many years.
It was quite different just before the
downfall of 1893. In those days foreign
capital wasn’t leaving the country. It was
streaming in as the blessed Tory thinks it
should do now, to develop the great local
resources. Taking Australia and Maoriland
together, it is estimated by Statistician
Cogiilan that the money of absentees
poured into Australia during a terrace of
strenuous years was as shown hereunder: —
Time of Peace.
Public Investments (less Net influx of
Borrowings withdrawals/ Absentee
Abroad. by Absentees. Capital.
IS7I-75., £20,310,000 Less than Nothing £12,625,000
Boom.
1876-80.. 31,902,000 £4,041,000 35,943,000
1881-85.. 46,004,000 26,597,000 72,661,000
1886-90.. 52,586,000 38,812,000 91,428,000
Slump.
1391-2 .. 18,786,000 2,857,(00 21,643,0C0
Burst.
£234,300,000
It was a grand time for absentee invest-
ment —a time when no beastly democrats
were discouraging the influx of money or
driving foreign capital out of the country
with a graduated land tax. But the trouble
was that during the 22 years included in the
above list no less than £205,514,000 left
Australasia as interest, dividends and profits
to the absentee investor, so that though
£234,300,000 of absentees’ capital came in
only a trifle of £28,786,000 remained in.
And when the British investor struck a
frightful snag in his own country at the
time of the Baring bank collapse, and sud-
denly stopped the supply, and even wanted
his money back and refused to be pacified
with notes as the Australian depositor was
in New South Wales and Queensland, it was
impossible to pay £234,300,000 out of
£28,786,000, and almost equally impossible
for the country which had been drunk on
absentee capital for 20 years or so to get sober
immediately, so Australia and Maoriland
had to ask for time from their creditors, and
get down to desperately hard work and short
rations in order to move the dead horse off
the premises.
* * * *
Since then (thanks to Providence and
better Government!) a great deal has been
done in the way of driving absentee capital
out of the country. The rising democratic
party hasmade many grievous blunders, but
its persistence in hunting foreign capital off
the face of this continent covers a multitude
of sins. Though the population has in-
creased since 1893 there is every reason to
believe that the outflow of money in the way
of interest, dividends and profits to the ab-
sentee person has greatly diminished. As
foreign capital has been driven out —which
is another way of saying that foreign in-
terest has been driven in—the cash reserves
of Australian banks have expanded from
less than £20,000,000 which these institu-
tions were able to show in 1892, after the
sudden influx of some £200,000,000 of
foreign capital, to something well over
£33,000,000. And though the lamentable bor-
rowing policy still continues, Australia does
things now (with foreign capital being
driven out) in the way of financing its own
undertakings which seemed 10 different kinds
of impossible in those fine old Tory days
when the absentees’ money was rushing in.
Last financial year, for instance, out of the
£6,325,216 borrowed by the wildly lavish
N.S.W. Government, £6,324,637 was ob-
tained in Sydney, and only £579 was cadged
in England. In the last five years no less
than £16,410,167 has been borrowed in Syd-
ney, and part of that went to pay off old
British loans. Since the floating of local
loans began, which was only after the great
slump in the importation-of-foreign-capital
business, N.S.W. has depended upon its own
money market, for two-thirds of its accom-
modation, and has struck the rather sur-
prising fact that it is a much better market
than the great Bull metropolis. For instance,
the lowest interest rate at which the Ma
State has raised the wind in London since the
bank-breakage year has been £3 Is. 84d. per
cent, against £3 os. s|d. per cent, in Sydney,
which is Is. 2|d. per cent, in favor of Sydney.
And the highest price paid for accommoda-
tion in London has been £4 10s. 2|d. per
cent, against £4 ss. 10|d. per cent, in Syd-
ney, which shows an advantage of 4s. 4d.
per cent, in favor of Sydney. Considering
all these circumstances it is difficult to be a
complete pessimist. No doubt there will be
great financial disasters in the future as there
have been in the past, but so long as foreign
capital is steadily driven out of the country
it is difficult to believe that Australia will
ever again see, in a time of profound peace,
splendid seasons and good prices, such a
hideous downfall as that of 18 years ago.
Certainly there is no evidence of it just
now. Many of the signs are the same as in
the late ’Bo’s of last century, but the one
utterly hopeless and disastrous sign—the one
plague spot which there is no mistaking—
the great influx of the absentee person’s
capital for investment, is not visible. That
kind of capital—may the heavens be praised!
—is still being driven out.
THE BOOK OF THE LAMENTATIONS
OF THE PROPHET POCKLEY.
The facts before them, and the more immediate
conclusions to be drawn from them, were of concern
in regard to the colonisation of northern Australia.
Here there was a tropical country, unique in having
no native colored races sufficiently numerous or will-
ing or able to help in developing the country. Unless
science came to their aid in providing some means
by which they could resist tropical influences, above
and beyond protozoal diseases, and if they were to
believe in history and natural selection, it would
appear to be inevitable that if they were to con-
tinue to hold this fine country, they must either
let it remain unnroductive, or comparatively so, or
they must evolve some scheme by which they could
develop it, by using colored labor. (Applause.)—
Dr. Pockley, in his Presidential address to the
Medical Congress.
A permanent reaching-out for nigger labor
is one of the earmarks of the old Tory. He
craves for nigger labor, apparently, for three
reasons: (1) Because he thinks it is cheap;
(2) because he thinks it is humble and
docile, and his conviction that the worker
should be very humble and very docile is in-
curable ; (3) because he thinks it doesn’t
want a vote, and his conviction that nobody
save himself should have a vote is also in-
curable. Starting from this basis he has
evolved, on no evidence whatsoever, or rather
in the face of all evidence, a theory that
without nigger labor the case of Northern
Australia is quite hopeless. The Bulletin
doesn’t know whether Hr. Pockley has ever
been in the North or not; certainly in his
address to the Medical Congress he made no
claim to first-hand knowledge of that
country. In that respect he is behind the
writer of this article, who does claim to have
lived, worked and travelled in tropical Aus-
tralia. But in the Book of the Lamenta-
tions of the Prophet Pockley it is set down
that if the white man can live and work
and prosper in tropical Australia he will do
so in violation of all precedent. And this
attitude sets one wondering where Pockley
found his precedents.
As a matter of fact, the white influx into
the tropical parts of this Commonwealth is
an event which stands alone. There are no
precedents. Many times before, in the stormy
history of this flying speck which we cail
Earth, the white man has gone into the
tropics. There he found, in every previous
case, hostile colored forces, and great, or at
least considerable, colored peoples. He found
the colored man’s germs, and the colored
man’s dirty, undrained, unwholesome towns
and cities. Also, he found a large and often
shambling and inefficient colored working
population, which he either enslaved or hired
at low wages to do the jobs which, in his
capacity as a soldier and a conqueror, he
didn’t want to do himself. As a result, two
kinds of tropical white man have gradually
developed—one better and one worse. There
is the permanent white settler of Mexico,
Central America, Colombia, Venezuela,
Guiana, Ecuador, Peru, Bolivia, most of
Brazil, portions of Chili, Argentina and
Paraguay, and of the Northern Transvaal.
Pie has dwelt for centuries in the tropics,
and has multiplied there, and has shown
himself, in most cases, a sturdy fighter and
a person of no small possibilities. Yet, he
has. always suffered from the fact that,
having a colored population to dig and hoe
for him, he hasn’t got right down to the soil,
and he has always had to contend with the
colored man’s diseases and the colored man’s
influences. Then there is the temporary
white man of India, Egypt, and such-like
places—the mere hotel dweller, who never
dreams of attempting actual settlement, and
who simply goes out for a few years as a
soldier, official or merchant. The British
hotel-dweller in crowded India has not
amounted to much as' a settler; no hotel-
dweller is expected to do so. But the perman-
ent Spanish and Portuguese colonist of the
tropics has been a success where he has
kept his white blood pure, and a modified or
complete failure in proportion as he mixed
it with the blood of negroes or aborigines.
These experiments have nothing to do
with the case or Northern Australia. That
is a business which flies by itself. There is
no other instance in all the world or in all
the world’s history where a white race has
found a tropical region with no colored
population worth mentioning, no special
native diseases, no dirty native cities or
towns, and no cheap docile local nigger to
do the hard work. Northern Australia is
the one tropical spot on record where the
white man has been his own laborer, where
he has inherited no ancient black, brown or
yellow problems or traditions, where he has
taken off his coat and proceeded to get up
an honest and manly perspiration—where,
in short, he has started from scratch. And,
so far, the results have been satisfactory
enough. Settlement happened to start at
the south end of Australia, because in the
days of sailing ships Torres Straits, and the
island-studded Timor Sea, and the treacher-
ous waters inside the Barrier Reef were not
easy places to navigate, and the original
settlers who tried to make the first begin-
nings of tnis country up there were wrecked.
And for lack of transcontinental railways,
settlement, having begun at the south, had to
crawl laboriously northward along the coast.
It has crawled to remarkably good purpose,
seeing that there are still living men who
were born before the first white township
began in Queensland. Inside the tropics
there are promising centres such as Rock-
hampton, Townsville, Cairns, Charters
Towers, Cloncurry, Lopgreach, Hughenden,
Winton and very many more. On the other
side of the continent population is also
moving northwards at a steady rate. The
young children on the coast of North Queens-
land now represent, in not a few cases, the
third generation of settlers. Thev are a
sturdy and hopeful race. The local birth-rate
is higher than that of England ; the death-
rate is not only about 40 per cent, lower
than that of England, but it is, bar the
death-rates of South Australia and Maori-
land, the lowest in the world. Also, it gets
lower as the north fills up. These tropical
settlers have penetrated to the farthest
north of Australia —farther than the most
northern part of the Territory—and, so far,
nothing has gone wrong with them. In
fact, the experiment they have made is the
most remarkable and almost the most pro-
mising one that the world has on hand just
now, though the virulent Tory wants to
abandon it as a failure. Possibly, some day,
the birth-rate in the north may drop 50 per
cent., and the death-rate rise 100 per cent..
and the white man may begin to wilt and
the population to diminish. But there are
no such signs at present, and until they
arrive the Book of the Lamentations of the
I rophet Pockley is an unsaleable volume
* * * *
Last week there was presented to the
House ot Representatives the report of the
expedition which recently visited the
Northern Territory under the leadership of
Professors Spencer and Gilbuth. This was
the tale of eminent scientific men who had
been there, not the loose theories of an
elderly city eye and ear doctor, living hun-
dreds of miles away from the scene of
action. A few extracts from the newspaper
summary of the report are as follows:
In the Territory the European’s health was re-
markably good. They were told that children should
be sent away at the age of 10 or 12, but they de-
cline to say that the practice is necessary for health.
Tropical diseases, such as malaria and dysentery
were not endemic, except in mining townships, and
need not be anticipated so long as hygienic pre-
cautions were observed. The school children’s
standard of intelligence was equal to that of southern
settlements, and the physique and energy of the
men, who had spent 30 or 40 years in the Territory,
compared favorably with men elsewhere. The
healthiest and strongest men and women were those
who took regular exercise even in the heat.
Life in the back country is reported on very
favorably. It is decidedly healthy, winter low tem-
peratures—often 40 deg.—affording recuperation,
from the excessive damp heat of the summers. The
scientists saw heavy dews, fogs and even ice.
“Though this part of Australia is well within the
tropics, it is not really tropical in its nature...”
“The stock were in excellent health... The general
condition of the horses was good. Sheep culture had
scarcely been attempted, but goats are healthy,
prolific and valuable lor milk and meat. Pigs are
scarce, but in good condition.”
That doesn’t read like the description of a
land which should be thrown as a dead and
gone proposition to the first nigger who
comes along. Of course there is always the
plea that the white man has “failed to settle
the Territory.” And certainly, for the few
brief years of the man’s ownership, he
has failed. In the same way the white man
failed for nearly three centuries to settle the
centre and west of Canada and the United
States. Even England failed for some mil-
lions of years to accumulate a decent popu-
lation. And Prussia, 450 years ago, had just
about as many people as Sydney, yet it
didn’t despair and give itself away to China-
men. It is always unwise to jump to the
conclusion that a child’s throat should be cut
because it doesn’t grow up in a day. Or that
two young married people should give their
spare house room over permanently to
Soudanese on the ground that it will
never be wanted by their own family,
just because twins haven’t been born
within three months after the wedding day.
Or that Dr. Pockley should be disqualified
from the practice of his profession because
he hasn’t, in one brief lifetime, found in-
fallible remedies for cancer, leprosy and
tuberculosis, and that his business and his
brass plate should be given over to a Solo-
mon Islander. The doctrine of crazy haste
can be overdone.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Triumph of Anarchy.
When times are stormy, and the public
temper grows perilously short, agitators,
outrage-fomenters and such-like dangerous
insects, generally crawl from beneath
the nearest flat stone and get busy. Their
office is to persuade as big a section of
the public as they can get to listen to them,
that conditions are so intolerable that riot
is justifiable, not to say admirable. The
Law has an ugly name for this sort of thing
and, where it is done crudely, leaps on the
offender with no uncertain hoof. The Syd-
ney morning dailies have been inciting poli-
ticians and others to riot and bloodshed for
about a month now in connection with the
N.S.W. Speakership business, and they
haven’t even the excuse of principle to jus-
tify their disorder-breeding. As recently as
August 18, the S. M. Herald took this
eminently-reasonable view of Willis’s ac-
ceptance of the billet about which all the
disturbance has arisen :
The Government is, we think, doing the constitu-
tionally right thing in endeavoring to carry on in-
stead of forcing a dissolution at the present moment.
Apparently Labor intends to hold office only so long
as is necessary to get through the Electoral Bill, the
Redistribution of Seats Bill, the Estimates, and some
other pressing matters that do not specifically imply
its special programme. This is a very proper way of
looking at the situation, and as we have already
said, it should have the assistance, if possible the
cordial assistance, of the Opposition. To hold an
election on obsolete rolls and obsolete electoral boun-
daries is repugnant to common sense, and there does
not seem to be any outstanding reason why Parlia-
ment should not get its electoral machinery in good
order before attempting another shuffle of the cards.
Suddenly the ukase went forth from the.Pitt-
street headquarters of “Liberalism” that the
man who had dared to put the interests of
N. S. Wales before those of C. G. Wade was
to be crucified. On August 21 the Herald
came out with the following:—
Of all the tempests that have swept through the
legislative halls of this State, none will equal the
one Mr. Willis will face when he presents himself
for election to the Speakership on Wednesday. If
the Liberals meet the House i-n the temper they were
in at the end of last week, this new knight of the
political arena will be up against a hurricane.
The Telegraph gave the office to ,the
bravoes of “Liberalism” in somewhat simi-
lar terms. The next morning both papers
informed their readers that every “Liberal,”
worthy the name, was thirsting for Willis’s
blood ; that he was a traitor and a fool; and
that if an enraged Opposition seized the
earliest opportunity to fall upon him,
it would be no more than he deserved.
On .the 23rd Willis, scenting trouble, said
in the Telegraph :
The press has suggested the possibility of Par-
liament being turned into a brawl when the election
of Speaker is proposed. That is impossible in an
assembly of gentlemen, and improbable in this Par-
liament.
But he was wrong. The rank and file of
Parliamentary “Liberalism” had not studied
their party newspapers in vain. For 20 hours
this man, who had done no worse than help
to accomplish that which, six days pre-
viously, the Herald had argued was “the
constitutionally right thing to do,” lay back
patiently and listened to a stream of
“Liberal” abuse and Billingsgate that,
in many Assemblies, would have led to gun-
practice and bloodshed. And, even then,
the party of disorder hadn’t finished with the
man whose worst crime was that he had
prevented an election being held “on obsolete
rolls and obsolete electoral boundaries.
Willis announced that he would explain the
situation in his constituency. As plainly as
any Anarchist orator ever urged his audience
to loot and violence the dailies told the
Anti-Labor electors of the Upper Hunter to
close Wn.T.TR’s mouth. For over a month
now, the “Liberals” in Parliament have de-


liberately held up the business of the N. S.
Wales Assembly and rendered it a byword
and an object of scorn from one end of Aus-
tralia to the other. During that time the
smaller or gutter papers of “'Liberalism”
have contained enough scurrilities concerning
the Speaker, his family, his intellect and his
character, to provoke him and his friends to
every conceivable breach of the peace, from
plain assault to daylight murder. The
climax was reached wtien various Par-
liamentary “Liberals” hurled books and
similar weighty missiles at the Speak-
er’s head—blue metal not being avai-
lable. This tne Telegraph, claimed
(20/9/’ll) was “humanly inevitable,” and
tbe Herald reasoned on the same morning
that it constituted a sound reason for imme-
diately holding that election (“on obsolete
rolls and obsolete electoral boundaries”),
which, four weeks before, it had damned as
an idea “repugnant to common-sense.” Nine-
tenths of the credit for this criminal busi-
ness unquestionably rests with the papers
which have egged on the perpetrators of out-
rage. It is a cheerful reflection for journals
which have hitherto had a certain claim to
be on the side of Law and order.

A Wholly Hypothetical Hoskins.
Dear Bulletin, —I see in the press re-
peated clamors by certain Labor bodies that
there should be no compulsory military
training in Australia, and that this country
should have neither an efficient army nor a
useful navy. Also, there are numerous roars
to the effect that it should look forward help-
lessly, but hopefully, to a time when all in-
ternational disputes will be settled by an
Arbitration Court, whose awards, though
backed by no force whatsoever and capable
of being disregarded with impunity, will be
frankly and fully accepted by all parties con-
cerned. At the same time, I seem to keep
running across the tale of a frantic and long-
continued strike at Lithgow (N.S.W.) —a
strike which goes on in defiance of arbitra-
tion awards, though the N.S.W. Arbitration
Court has far more force betiind it than the
Hague tribunal possesses. Now, why, if
these “anti-militarists” have such profound
faith in the acceptance, by conflicting na-
tions, of the awards of a prospective court
on which all manner of colored and hostile
and cheap labor and long hours and non-
union peoples will necessarily he represented
• —why don’t they live up to their own creed
by always peacefully accepting the awards
of their own Arbitration CourtV It would be
an easier job than the other. The local in-
stitution is a much better one to get on with
than any possible international court will
be, especially when the Jap and the Chinese
and Siamese and Hindu members thereof sit
to consider the appeal of Nippon or Tim-
buctoo against Australia’s theory that
colored races are unfit to come into this
country. Why (to resume the questioning
attitude) do the Anti-Defence Laborites de-
clare arbitration in their own small case a
plain, unmitigated failure, and yet urge the
country to trust to it over questions which
may involve the wiping out of our race and
color and all our institutions, the Trades Hall
included? Why do they throw bricks (for
some bricks certainly have been thrown),
and yet say that peace is at hand and can
be relied upon ? Peace is not bricks any more
than a dog is a serpent, nor does the go-
hanna live as a regular thing in the Equit-
able Building. Sydney. Why do they keep
their own industrial army organised, ready
tor a struggle to the death whenever arbi-
tration makes a bad failure of things, and
yet urge the country to remain unarmed and
unorganised, on the ground that the blessed
thing can’t be a failure? Why do they ask
Australia to trust the Mikado and Kaiser
William when they themselves won’t trust
Hoskins, of Lithgow? The present atti-
tude of a certain section of Labor is that
arbitration has completely broken down, and
at the same time that the said object will
never break down. Let me ask the Anti-
Australian party among the Laborites to
more fully consider Hoskins, of Lithgow.
Let him be viewed as an almost absolute
Hos Kins, with all his bad qualities, what-
ever they may be, multiplied by the insane
pride of high station and royal descent. Let
him be a Hoskins brought up in the doc-
trine of divine right, and urged on by his
own ambition and the ambition of a con-
siderable section of his subjects. Suppose
him to be, for all practical purposes, a
200,000 or a r 2,000,000 times bigger Hoskins
than he is. Let him be occasionally a black
or yellow or brown Hoskins, but never a
striped Hoskins. And then let a solitary,
unarmed, unorganised worker with no sup-
port behind him ask this Hoskins to arbi-
trate — a Hoskins who would be about two
miles high and wild in proportion ; to come
down to that solitary worker’s level; to sub-
mit all disputes between himself and that
unarmed and unorganised worker to a court
that had no single shred of power wherewith
to enforce its decisions—a court half the
size, for real fighting purposes, of theatrical
manager Jimmy McMahon —and to swear
that he -would abide by its decisions, quite
regardless of the fact that the solitary
worker was himself no great hand at abid-
ing by similar decisions when he found them
disagreeable and had the power to reject
them. Let him ask Hoskins, in fact, to
give away the game when he has it in his
hand. And then let the Anti-Defence section of
the Labor party consider the probable roar
of this Hoskins —this wholly hypothetical
Hoskins, two miles high and wild in pro-
portion —and the way his snort of fury
would blow the crops off the surface of 16
parishes. If tjie said section doesn’t like
that orospect, then it can’t blame me for
not liking the prospect its anti-defence pro-
posals open out before me. Still, I am a
reasonable person, and will make a fair
offer. If there are no strikes in Australia
for 20 years ; if for 20 years every award of
the Arbitration Court is cheerfully accepted
and fully obeyed; if Labor finds that it can
safely disband (and does disband) its com-
pulsory service industrial army, which is to
say its trades unions—then I will join the
Peace Brigade and vote for keeping Aus-
tralia unarmed and unorganised. And I will
recognise that the great unarmed millennium
has begun when Labor shakes hands with
Hoskins (I admit that I never shook hands
with him myself), and asks him to din-
ner. and sings a rousing chorus to the effect
that he is a jolly good Hoskins. —Yours,
etc., Henry Hqrsecollar.

An Appeal for Plain Speech.
Dr. Ham read a paper on venereal disease
before the Medical Congress, last week, and
the S. M. Herald came boldly out with a
leader on the subject. Some extracts: —
Dr. Ham dealt with a matter of the utmost import-
ance to any community, and he was fully justified in
calling for the sympathy and support of all those
who are in a position to influence public opinion.. ..
The facts of life are there to be faced in whatever
way they may arise, and it is the most mawkish of
morality to refuse to face them....A great deal of
nonsense is talked as to the inadvisability of refer-
ring to these things at all. How terrible is the price
that we pay for that concession to a false sentiment!
... It is almost impossible to get attention drawn to
the evil in any effective ' way. A conventional
hypocrisy blocks the most elementary steps of reforni.
. .. We do most heartily emphasise the author’s claim
for frankness and freedom in the discussion of one of
the gravest of social problems.
The Herald helped to “drag the facts”
about syphilis, as it put it, “from the fester-
ing night into the light of day” (1) by re-
fraining from mentioning even the name
of the horror once in the whole
course of its article: (2) by omit-
ting to publish an intelligible sentence of
Hr. Hails paper; (3) by telling the pub-
lic nothing whatever as to how to avoid or
cure this calamity, which, as it remarked,
afflicts "not merely the contracting parties,
but generations—innocent generations —yet
unborn.” Since the other big Sydney daily
acted similarly—with the exception that it
didn’t even waste space on a blankly mysteri-
ous leader concerning Ham’s effort —the
readers of these two delicate rags know as
much about syphilis now as thev did before
the Medical Congress met, which is to say
practically nothing. Joiin Bunyan termed
tuberculosis “the captain of the men of
death and in the 17th century it had a
fair claim to the title. To-day it is a mere
corporal in the army, and there is every
likelihood of its being drummed out of the
regiment altogether in the near future. As
a result of the desperate war waged by the
medical profession on this disease ; the lavish
financial help given in the cause by the civi-
lised nations (the British Government cheer-
fully spent £76,000 on a Royal Commission
on Tuberculosis, the other day) ; and the
educational campaign that has been con-
ducted in the newspapers, by pamphlets and
on platforms, it has declined over 50 per
cent, in the past 50 years. The president of
the Glasgow Local Government Board, in his
opening address at the recent tuberculosis
conference, said :
In 25 or 30 years tuberculosis should be a memory
of the past, like typhus, leprosy and plague.
Meanwhile syphilis—an even more dreadful
and far-reaching complaint—increases apace,
and, to all intents and purposes, unchecked.
This is particularly so in most parts of Aus-
tralia. Mr. R. M. Me. Anderson, speaking
at St Stephen’s Church, PhilJip-streef, Syd-
ney, a few weeks back, quoted a report pre-
sented to the House of Commons (1907)
with regard to the health of the Australian
Squadron as follows : —-
With the exception of venereal diseases the ratios
in reference to all the important diseases are satis-
factory. In this respect, Sydney has the unenviable
distinction of figuring easily highest, the percentage
of crews suffering from venereal disease, after sojourn
in Port Jackson, being 214.75 per thousand* or more
than one man in five.
Mr. Anderson continued
While Sydney is the worst place in the world, Bris-
bane, the next port of call, is practically free of the
scourge, and they ascribe it to the Contagious
Diseases Act of Queensland, which is worked satisfac-
torily, but against which, periodically, the Churches
used to arise in their wrath to demand its repeal.
In 1908 the Wade Government introduced a Conta-
gious Diseases Act, but I have to say with deep
regret that the convenor of committee of the religious
and public morals of our own Church actually for-
warded a letter objecting to the passing of this most
beneficent Act.
The chief reason for this hideous state of
things is the craven dread the local daily
press has always had of plain speech on the
matter. Once instructed on the subject of
this disease, which is responsible for more
insanity, crippled and deformed children,
and all-round ill health and misery, than
any other thing on earth, and the public
would see to it that the State took the same
drastic steps to wipe it out as it has taken
to eradicate plavue and smell-pox. But so
long as the question is burked by the papers,
or mentioned only, as in the case above-men-
tioned, in such a wealth of genteel peri-
phrasis as to render it incomprehensible to
all but those who don’t need information,
syphilis must continue to blast the national
health as heretoiore.

The Wowser on a Good Wicket.
Wowserism, for once in a way, has taken
up an unassailable position in Victoria.
Wowserism is waving the flag of Fair Play
for Workers. It snorts at the sacred
rights of Proputty. It fires from be-
hind a barrier of Common Sense.
Melbourne Social Reform League lately
waited on burly Frank Madden, the State
Speaker, to ask him to take charge of a
petition praying Parliament to extend the
Early Closing Act to bars, and put the
workers therein on the game level as the
employees of other shops. The petition
carried the signatures of hotel employees,
which gave it a sufficiently democratic char-
acter. Poop Frank Madden had to pro-
mise that the document would be duly pre-
sented to the House, and then he began to
unpack his soul: —
He asked whether the petitioners had any scheme
to deal fairly with Vested Interests. Hotelkeepers
had entered into leases under existing conditions.
They had to pav premiums and high rents under the
leases. Would the petitioners do the publicans the
injustice of reducing their hours of trading without
compensation? etc., etc.
And he asked them to “consider a fair
scheme for getting their prayer carried into
effect, and not to ask for what would be
ridiculed.” Now, every sort of established
shop business was sold in the dark old days,
prior to Early Closing legislation, under the
conditions that then existed, and never a
word was said about compensation to the
owners when their hours of business were
reduced in number. It was reasonably
pleaded that the public would buy all the
goods it required within the legalised shop-
ping limit. It can be just as reasonably
argued that the public can buy all the liquor
it requires within the hours appointed for
purchasing bread, ironmongery, bonnets,
etc. Frank Madden unblushinglv suggests
that common or grog-shop victuallers have
Vested Interests in supplying the public
with more beer than is required, and that,
inasmuch as the injurious superfluity would
be reduced in volume by extending butchers’
shop regulation to the beer-shopkeeper, the
latter would be entitled to compensation for
loss of trade. The drunks would _be
counted, presumably, and for any diminu-
tion or falling-off (not falling down) the
aggregate publican would be recompensed
at so much per drunk. It would be a rough
and ready process, for some drunks hold
more than .others, but there would be a cer-
tain crude justice about it —as the Frank
Madden clan understands justice.
A Conflict that Doesn’t Rage Worth
Tuppence.
Certain Federal and Cabbage Garden poli-
ticians are much disturbed over what they
call a conflict of policies. When the Superior
Legislature drew up its list of articles pro-
hibited of import it put prison-made goods
among them. Since then, it has_ offered a
bonus on the production of wire-netting
made in Australia from imported wire. Now
the Murray State employs some of its
prisoners in Pentridge at wire-netting
making, and these wiseacres ask what is the
use of trying to encourage an industry by
bonus if a State Government manufactures
what are really prohibited gqfcds? Also,
they ask, will the State Government apply
for a share of the bounty? There is no
reason why it shouldn’t; neither is there
any anomaly about the position. Foreign
prison-made goods are prohibited, not be-
cause they are made in gaols—Jliey are not
extra bad or over-adulterated on that ac-
count —but because they are often sold at a
rate with which private enterprise can’t
compete. They are cheap because the
prisoner often works long hours, gets little
or no wages, and has not to maintain either
himself or his wife and family. The policy
of prohibition should apply with equal force
to other goods that are the product of
cheap-labor and long-hour countries —jute
materials, for instance. The goods are pro-
duced primarily because it is better that
prisoners should be fully employed than
kept in idleness, even if the goods they pro-
duce have to be given away, but Australia
has no yearning to assist other countries to
solve their penal problems; also, it wants
all the manufactures it can get—hence the
prohibition and the bonus. But that policy
doesn’t apply to its own prisoners. They
must be maintained, therefore —and for
other reasons—they must work; and it is
better they should toil to maintain them-
selves than that, as is the case to-day, free
men should toil to maintain them. The fact
that they are where they are tends to prove
that they need a course of hard toil to in-
culcate habits of industry. Having then pro-
duced their goods—wire netting, f’rinstance
—these must be sold, and so long as the pro-
duct is sold at its market value no harm is
done. So far, there is no accusation that the
Weary State is selling below market value.
If there were so many prisoners that one
had to be maintained by every five “out-
siders.” instead of by every 600 or so as at
present, it would speedily dawn on these
objectors to prison labor that there are no
points about keeping confinees in idleness,
and that the harder they work the more they
produce, and the higher the price their pro-
ducts realise the better for everybody. Also,
when a prisoner has to do hard labor on
sensible lines —until he has earned enough
to cover the loss or injury he has caused —-
he will be as anxious as anybody else that
his products aren’t sold below market value.
A Free Country.
In answer to a voluminous correspondent; —
The Bulletin has been afflicted this week with a
gigantic communication of 21 closely-written pages
from an excited person residing near Hyde Park,
Sydney, which person demands —in 21 pages—that
this country should remain unarmed and defenceless,
and trust to Providence. Amid a great deal of talk
about the armies of Russia and other despotic coun-
tries, there is just one sentence which calls for a
certain amount of consideration:—-
“Does The Bulletin really believe that men,
trained and drilled under a compulsory military
system, can ever make a free people?”
After considering the cases of Switzerland, Den-
mark and Norway, generally considered about the
most liberal countries in Europe, the evidence is that
men trained and drilled under a compulsory system
can be free. In two of these States both Houses of
Parliament, and in the other State the Lower House
and four-fifths of the Upper, are elected by universal
suffrage. The people carry no loads except such as
they think best, which is also the position of the
people of Australia. The same definition applies to
France, Chili, Brazil and other spots. In all these
pl’ces a free nation has resolved that all-round mili-
tary education is a good thing, and has freely
adopted it. On the other hand, China, Persia, Turkey,
Morocco, San Domingo, Ilaita, Korea, and otljer States
have escaped universal compulsory military training,
and their joyous, advanced, progressive peoples are
a rather doubtful set of circumstances.
As it seems to be the custom to dig up aged and
r-'mote historical evidences, it may be mentioned that
old Poland, in its latter days—Poland where the
people were divided into slaves and nobles —had no
compulsory military service ; more than that, it dis-
liked “militarism” so much that, towards the end, it
had no army at all. There was no compulsory service
in the Roman or Greek empires in their last days, nor
in the slave States of Uncle Sam's country. Nor Is
there in Britain, with its hereditary Upper House, and
its Lower House elected mostly on a property fran-
chise. Whether all these countries rank or rank"'!
among the “free” is a matter of opinion. It all
depends on the definition of the adjective. In The
Bulletin's view a country is free when its burdens,
even though they be heavy, are imposed and main-
tained by the deliberate will of the adult population,
and it isn’t free if its burdens, even though they he
light, are imposed by a Government which is beyond
the control of the people. On that basis compul-
sory military training no more makes nor unmakes
freedom than compulsory education, or compulsory
drainage, or compulsory water supply. But if it is
held that all-round military training is the real test
of freedom, then we arrive at the result that Switzer-
land is now practically the most enslaved country in
the world, and that one of the most free and liberal
States going at present is Morocco. That may be the
case, but there is an air of improbability about the
matter somehow.

When the Progressive Land Tax was before
the Australian Parliament there was a
curious division of opinion amongst its Tory
opponents. One group predicted with great
fluency that the tax would have practically
no effect upon land values, and that land
would be as dear as ever. The other group
cursed the Bill as a “confiscatory measure,’’
and predicted that it would cause a disas-
trous slump in land values. The tax has
been in operation now for over a year, and
both groups are. apparently, no nearer an
agreement than before. During the speech
on the Address-in-Reply the following re-
marks were made in the Senate: —
Senator W. Russell: It would appear that the
progressive land tax is going to have the effect that,
instead of a few men getting a living from this
land. . .there will be 70 or 80 persons settled there.
Senator Millen: On paying a higher price than
was paid for the land a few years ago.
Senator W. Russell: I have said that the price
has gone up.
Senator Millen: So that the land tax has not pro-
cured cheap land for the people.
Next door, however, in the House of Reps.,
this sort of thing was being said:—-
Mr. Fisher: It is singular that, notwithstand-
ing this tax, land values in the aggregate have not
declined.
Mr. R. Foster: That is not correct.
Mr. Fisher: I say that, in the aggregate, land
values in Australia have not declined.
Mr. R. Foster: I say they have.
Mr. Hans Irvine : And in every State.
It would be pleasanter if Millen and Fos-
ter were to have a friendly conference, just
to decide which line they should follow, as
the present system is embarrassing. Foster
and Irvine both seem to overlook the fact
that the ultimate value of land is what it
will produce, clear of taxes and general ex-
penses. As the Federal tax does not hit
the average farm, and the value of land-
products is, if anything, on the up grade,, it
is only natural that land values should still
tend upward. The only possible interfer-
ence with this tendency would be the throw-
ing on the market of huge areas of previ-
ously locked-up and suitable land. As it
happens, while the land tax js sending-land
steadily into the market, the demand for it
by new settlers (including farmers’ sons! is
more than sufficient to maintain prices. The
position is simple enough, but the Irvines
and Fosters still wag their chins fatuously
and repeat their old gag—though they can-
not pick up a newspaper without seeing
their pet contention biffed in the eye, so to
speak.

Dog is eating dog on the Yarra wharves in
Melbourne. The importers, exasperated by
the jumble and the Gehenna and the chaos
of things under the management of the
Stevedoring companies, want to wipe that
bit of Private Enterprise out of existence.
Importers say that their goods are flung on
the wharves anyhow from the ships; they
are badly sorted and badly stacked, and may
be plunked in the middle of the road for
hours and days; whereupon an army of per-
sons called Pilferers get to work. They say
that shed space is wasted, that immoral
preferences are shown, and that the fear-
some charges they have to pay are making
Melbourne scandalously expensive as a port.
All this is what they suffer under Private
Enterprise, which gets hold of merchandise
as it drops out of the ship’s slings, and
tenaciously keeps its teeth in it until a
large enough bite has been taken. So in
their just wrath the importers say, “Damn
the Stevedores!” —-and they appeal to the
Harbor Trust to take over the whole of the
wharfinger business. The Harbor Trust! —
a Socialistic institution under public
management, and created by a Socialistic
Act of Parliament in the Socialistic year
1576 ! Next week, the same importers will
howl against some scheme of State Enter-
prise because it isn’t designed for their
special profit. At present, in fact, they have
just finished howling against Pearce,
Minister for Defence, because he intends to
build military factories for Australia. But
when they want system and organisation
for their own advantage, they turn at once
to Socialistic management. Which proves
what a damnable and carnivorous spirit is
in this same Socialism. It even makes dog
eat dog—and plain raw dog at that, not
boiled dog,.
Who Owns the Riverina?
I heard two Fat Men shouting in a fami-
liar key—•
Like twin volcanoes spouting their language
seemed to me!
They stood each side the Border (we still
have “borders” here)
And yelled in fierce disorder across the sham
frontier.
The noise they made was awful —whilst
cuss-words flew like rain ;
Each s" ore his claims were lawful, and
scoffed in loud disdain!
They raged with heat volcanic—yea, each
with frantic mien
Exclaimed ‘midst leaps galvanic: “/ own
the Riverine!”
Said New South Wales: “I’m certain this
land belongs to Me.”
Vic. yelled : “Go, lift the curtain of bygone
historee !
When What’s-His-Name reported in
Eighteen-blessed-two”-
“Yah ! Yah!” the Ma State snofted, “that
ancient yarn won’t do!
When Thingumbob surveyed it”—then Vic.
made swift reply :
“You’ll tell me next you made it! Oh, what
a flaring lie!”
’Twas thus those Fat Men wrangled—they
turned the climate green
Anent that question tangled: “Who owns
the Riverine?”
Then, 10, I heard a Stranger up-raise his
voice as well:
“Get out of this here manger ; get out and
go to h-11!”
Then both the Fat Men trembled, like Tories
brave and true;
Whilst each, in wrath dissembled, cried,
“Who on earth are you?”
Tne Stranger said: “My shingle hangs out
from Cairns to Perth.
I am Owner single of this here bit of
earth!
I am the whole great Nation,” remarked the
Stranger lean.
“The Younger Generation will boss the
Riverine!”
Went on the Stranger, slowly: “You Two
are fat of girth ;
In bondage vile, unholy, you hold Australia’s
earth !
Each side your bogus Border the untilled
acres lie —-
Fat is our Prison-warder. With Greed’s un-
sleeping eye!
Ye watch the people seeking for land to
plough and till —
I hear no waggons creaking witn wheat for-
ma rt and mill!
Ye wait the harvest golden,” proclaimed the
Stranger keen.
“This was your watchword olden: ‘Fat
owns the Riverine!’
“You Two,” observed the Stranger, “for
many galling years,
Like dogs wituin a manger, have held these
sham frontiers!
This Land, by Fat divided, has waited over-
long—
You Two have long denied it a Manhood
brave and strong!
This Empty Land is calling; it calls for
virile men;
Be off!” —his foot sent sprawling each vasty
Abdomen !
“Clear out!” exclaimed the Stranger, “you
spoil the pleasant scene ;
You are Australia’s danger—/ own the
Riverine!”

Aberdeen—the Hot Place Where the White
Man Can’t Live.
The coldest supporter White Australia has
got is Sydney Telegraph. That large and
misplaced organ seldom loses a chance to re-
mark how much it wants to keep this
country wiiite, and how broken-up with
grief it is because the thing is almost or
quite impossible. The Medical Congress, in
Sydney, brought forth two doctors in parti-
cular —one who declared that the north of
Australia was an ideal place for the white
settler, and another who had doubts. Where-
upon the Telegraph wept bitterly as fol-
lows :
It is highly desirable that competent investigators
should collect and analyse evidence on the spot in
this matter. The investigation to be conclusive
would have to extend, of course, over a wide range.
In fact, it would have to include practically the
whole of the very sparse white population of tropical
Australia. Dr. Breiul has apparently approached
the subject from the standpoint of an expert in tropi-
cal diseases. An enthusiast in that branch of re-
search might be expected to take the view that
when once the parasitic germs which he has to fight
have been fairly defeated, the white man can come
in and live in the country. But it may be pointed
out that the' white man has hitherto failed to make
good his foothold in the trop.eal regions of other
countries. The almost complete disappearance of the
pure white stock planted by the Spaniards and Portu-
guese in Central and South America is a significant
fact that cannot be ignored. The white populations
of South America to-day are found exclusively in the
temperate regions—in Argentina, for instance —and
even there they are only maintained by a strong and
steady flood of immigration.
It seems as if, in his haste, the Telegraph’s
ignoramus bad sworn a little too hard. He
says, for instance, that the white popula-
tions of South and Central America have
“completely disappeared” except in the tem-
perate regions such as Argentina. The
statement is a silly and bumptious and glar-
ing invention. The really white population
of tropical Spanish America is certainly not
large; it never was large, for Spain had very
little population or its own when it con-
quered these countries, and could only send
out a handful of men. It used to invade a
bm native State with 100 soldiers or so, and
a large proportion of these married brown
women for lack of sufficient white ones. Yet
there are something over 230,000 whites in
little, obscure, out-or-the-way Bolivia, which
corresponds with the region between Papua
and Central Queensland. Brazil, as
far back as 1872, had 3,787,000 white
inhabitants, of whom 3.543,000 were native
born; and that was before the great immi-
gration began. Yet Brazil is mostly tropical,
and extends right up to the equator; also,
it has grown a city of 230,000 people in
the same latitude as Port Darwin. Costa
Rica, in Central America, is mostly in-
habited by pure whites. Over 2,000,000 of
the population of Mexico is white. The
Chilians are mostly white, and there are
over 3,000,000 of them. In fact, the
original handful of white settlers, in-
stead of dying out, seems to have multi-
plied wonderfully. These facts can hardly
be unknown to the Telegraph ; but that
paper’s insane craving for nigger labor leads
it to ignore or flatly deny them. But it
makes a specially silly exhibition of itself
when it describes Argentina as a place where
the white race can only be “maintained by
a strong and steady flood of immigration.”
The south end of Argentina extends much
farther towards the Antarctic than the
south extremity of Maoriland (in fact, it
answers to the north of Scotland or the
south of Norway in the other hemisphere).
The republic's great centre of population is
in the same latitude as Sydney, and its
winter is colder. It is a wild, mad Tory
paper which represents Argentina as a place
where the white race can’t survive unless
helped out by immigration. Apparently the
'Graph is capable of declaring that the
white man is being killed off by the extreme
heat in the tronics of Aberdeen, and that
nigger labor must be procured instantly.
