France and Germany in Morocco.
The Old Soldier’s views:—
The nation which Britain is backing in the present
Franco-German trouble isn’t by any means clean-
handed as regards Morocco. The French representa-
tive at Fez is Colonel Mangin, of the Algerian Rifles.
He combines with his representative character that
of Commandant of the Sultan’s mercenaries, much as
if the Sirdar of the Egyptian army were to be also
British Consul-General. Colonel Mangin had his
instructions from Paris to secure the payment by
the usurping Sultan Mulai Hafid of the advances
France had made to him, and he had also to use his
mercenaries to collect the Sultan’s revenue from the
tribes, who have never paid anyone except on com-
pulsion. That stirred up the tribes to resistance,
and Fez was besieged, and relieved by a French force
under General Moinier. Says a writer in Black-
wood for August:—
“It is not to be believed that France entered
lightly into the stupendous expense of these difficult
operations simply to save the lives of the few Euro-
peans who might easily have left Fez under safe
conduct to the coast, by consent of the invading
rebels. On the day that the investment of Fez was
raised, Dr. Vassal, the German Consul-General, had
Colonel Mangin. head of the French Military Mission,
‘stone dead.’ By that is meant that Mangin, with
his head inflated by the personal adulation that the
Parisian press had bestowed upon him, permitted the
French-trained. French-officered troops of the Magh-
zen to descend upon the peaceful homesteads of the
Uled Jama tribe, and to burn, ravish and destroy
as to them seemed sufficient. As a result of this
atrocious licence, much property of British- and
Germah-protected Moors was destroyed, arid children
of tender years were torn from their homes, and after
being ravished, were sold in the streets of Fez into
life-long slavery for a few silver coins. When
officially remonstrated with by the representatives of
friendly European Powers, the leader of the French
Mission is said to have replied: ‘THERE ARE NO
RIGHTS OF PRIVATE LIFE AND PROPERTY IN
A REBEL COUNTRY.’ ”
The cableman was careful to tell us nothing about
that last little touch of Col. Mangin’s quality, nor
has he told us of the. little plan that has been, since
the ’Bo’s of the last century, before the French Par-
liamentary Committee, for flooding Europe with a
French-trained, armed and equipped army of African
savages, to be raised and drilled in the negro coun-
tries south of the Sahara. In the War Budget
Committee’s Report for 1909, the former proposal
was recited, with this significant addition: —
“As it is obvious that the sanction of Parliament
to this increase of our black force may arouse dis-
trust among our own people, as well as others, it
has been decided to authorise publication (unoffi-
cially) of a work compiled by Lieut.-Col. Mangin,
which is here submitted. The committee will notice
that the scheme is presented as a temporary one for
bridging the interval required for restoring the
birthrate, b- means therein set forth, and it is
hoped that the very able arguments in favor of this
policy will allay public suspicion for the time
being.” *
The idea is “10 years hence,” that is in 1919, to
transport 100,000 Arabs and 40,000 negroes to
France. The committee adds; —
“We are fully confident that the allies of France
would hold the Atlantic open for the transport of
her black legions, and that the French fleet would
be strong enough to hold the Mediterranean.”
The “Allies of France” can only be the British.
In these days, when the Australian daily press is
doing its best, by cooking its cables, to work up an
enthusiasm for the French as against the Germans,
it is just as well that the facts of the case should be
plainlv stated, viz., that, according to all the avail-
able evidence, the French Republic stands for the
colored man and tv. very, and the German Empire for
white men and liberty.
“Button-stick” : To the Hogg person’s de-
mand of the M.L. Commandant, referred to
in The Bulletin (7/9/T1), as to “whether
any scheme of Compulsory service possible
of adoption by the M.L. Government can be
considered adequate compared with the forces
that might be arrayed against it.” I’d like to
yell loud enough to be. heard from here to
Maoriland an emphatic. “Yes.” If all the
material to hand—every able-bodied man
between 18 and 40 —was. well trained, had a
rifle per man and a plentiful supply of am-
munition, Maoriland would be a tough
enough mouthful to make any force think
twice before taking a bite. I know the
country fairly well, and I don’t think I’ve
ever seen one that offered better chances of
defence by a numerically inferior force. It
is a natural fortress from end to end. Any
of the troops who went through the kopje-
climb-and-scrap between Colenso and Lady-
smith know the difficulty of forcing a way
through such country. Wellington, Auck-
land and Christchurch all offer excellent
chances of resisting attack from sea. I don’t
profess to know much about sea-figliting, but
I know that all these places are snugly
planted in positions where a few properly-
constructed forts and modern long-range
guns could give considerable of a Hot Time
to any ships that came near. Certainly the
railways are not too well placed for the
rapid massing of troops to resist a landing
at various points, but neither are they too
good from the. strategical point of view of
the invader, and any he could use have so
many bridges, culverts, etc., that they could
easily be put out of action by the retreating
defenders. There are sheep and cattle
enough in the country to last for an in-
definite time, and these could be driven into
the hills at the back of the defenders, aud
bare grass left for the attackers to feed on.
The chief towns might be occupied by the
enemy for a time, but as long as the Maori-
landers took care not to allow their armies
to be inside when they fell, and left no big
stores of arms or ammunition in them, their
loss would affq’ct the war even less than the
loss of Bloemfontein and Pretoria affected
the Boers.

A branch of the People's party, at Strath-
merton (Vic.) recently' had a shot at for-
mulating a scheme of.'wages and conditions
for farm hands, 'in opposition to the Rural
Workers’ Union schedule. The branch did
very well, so far as the farmer is concerned:
Ordinary farm hands, 20s. per week, and found.
Ploughmen and drill-drivers, 255., and found.
Haymaking hands, 305,, and found.
Binder drivers, 365., and found.
Hours of work: 7 a.m. to 7 p.m., or sunset. The
paddock to be left at 7 p.m. or sunset (whichever is
latest, apparently). Horse drivers to attend to
their horses in their own time.
In the summer, therefore, the driver of a
binder will have to get up at 5 a.m. to feed
his horses and himself, and it will be at
least 8.30 p.m. when he finishes. Allowing 1J
hours for meals, this leaves him 14 hours
work per day—for 65., or slightly less than
sd. per hour. There will probably be a wild
rush of farm-hands to Strathmerton this
harvest-season —if only to hang somebody.
Newcastle’s bubbly old friend, Tommy
Walker, who sat in the N.S.W. Parliament
for years, is among the 10 Laborites to get
into the new Westralian Assembly without
having to fight for it. The Wilson Govern-
ment could muster up pluck enough to go
for only one of the Goldfields seats ; and it
wouldn’t have shown its nose there only it
couldn’t save the political skin of one of its
Ministers any other way. Nowadays, there
are 50 seats in the ’Groper Assembly; and a
crowd that has 10 of them safely in its
pocket can’t complain of the start it gets,
anyway.

The first production of “Carmen” by the
Melba Opera Co., at Her Majesty’s (Syd-
ney), was a brilliantly colored picture of
the tempestuous career of the Spanish
gipsy with a volcano in her temper. Bizet’s
music is a cyclonic mixture of a storm, a
passionate love song, and the tinkle of
guitars. It is a drama m itself, and a per-
fect setting for the whirlwindy “Carmen.”
Eleanora de Cisneros, all thunder and light-
ning, sends Don Jos6-Zieni to blazes in one
of the finest performances of the present
opera season. Vocally, Eleanora-Carmen’s
first warble on Saturday night was not
flawless in its tempo, and Angelini strained
like a greyhound in the leash to get the
voice and the orchestra into a mutual under-
standing. Yet, it was only a small spot on
the brilliant disc of Eleanora’s superb sing-
ing. Zeni, as Don Jose, was rather heavy
in his first scenes with Carmen. His
struggles were the obvious kicks of a fly on
the edge of a treacle tin or a precipice.
After the first act, however, Zeni-Don Jose
not only went to the devil in magnificent
voice, but with a hurricane of dramatic
craft. Eileen Castles, the gentle Micaela of
Saturday’s performance, in spite of nervous-
ness, sang like a bird with a sweet, small
and absolutely true voice that was right on
the spot in every note. Her warble in the
aria (third act) was, vocally, almost fault-
less in its purity of tone. Again, The Bul-
letin hands a posy to Rosina Buckman,
the Mercedes of Carmen. Edmund Burke
was a wooden Escamillo, and sang the
riotous music of the Toreador’s song in a
rich, round voice with the expression of a
lay preacher looking for a job.

To-morrow (Friday) night, “The Waltz
Dream” sways gracefully, melodiously out
of Sydney Royal, giving place to “The
Speckled Band.” The new production is a
dramatic version of one of the Sherlock
Holmes hair-lifters, and the cast will include
Miss Dorothy Dix (a recent import), Wil-
liam Desmond, Cyril Mackay and a live
snake.

“Lady Frederick,” the new show at the
Criterion, is not the work of art that the
writer had been led to expect. It is impos-
sible to accept it as a serious picture of life
anywhere, much less in the Empire’s Smart
Set, which, by all accounts, is not a remark-
ably brainy set, however well-dressed and
sprightly and good-looking and sport-loving
it may be. There are characters in “Lady
Frederick” who get off more bright things
in the course of a couple of days than the
average wit of the iSth century ? (when talf
.mg was an Art) achieved in a year. The
modern society man who, like" Paradine
It ouldes, garnished his every remark with
epigram and paradox and polysyllabic gener-
alisations about late, delivered, a.- it were
from a vast intellectual height, would pro-
bably be expelled from the Smart Set as an
intolerable nuisance and bore, and advised
to get down to his proper level with G L
Shaw, Wells and the members of the Fabian
“The Chocolate Soldier ” at Mel- LESLIE
bourne Royal. GAZE
ALBERT KAVANAGH as as
CASIMIR POPOFF. BUMERLI.
Society. Lady Frederick Berolles is de-
lightful but quite incredible. Her astound-
ing successes in the matter of pulling
people’s legs are only explicable on the
assumption that her dupes are half-witted.
Consider, for example, the case of the low
comedy dressmaker to whom Lady Frederick
owes £750 odd, and who is abased to the
earth by a little gross flattery and a misty
sort of invitation to lunch with an Arch-
duchess. Assuming this tradeswoman is
completely devoid of intelligence, she
wouldn’t be in the immense way of business
she seemingly is; on the other hand, unless
she is insane she would see right through
Lady Frederick’s audacious blarney, in about
three minutes, and resent it accordingly.
The same objection applies to the scene
where Lord Mereston is cured of his in-
fatuation by the painful spectacle of his
lady-love beautifying herself. Recalling the
frantic efforts of his mother, to estrange him
from Lady Frederick, and other things, the
nobleman would be idiotic, indeed if be
failed to grasp the inner meaning of the
gratuitous show. David Garrick’s disil-
lusioning of his adorer was a convincing bit
of self-degradation, inasmuch as, 150 years
ago, it was customary for men to get up-
roariously drunk in society. But tne day
has not dawned, nor is ever likely to, when
women will, with no ulterior object, reveal
the subterranean mysteries of the toilet to
their admirers. The name-part is palpably
written up with an eye to Miss Irving or
someone like her. The comic underplot has
no more relation to tne play proper than if
the latter were a melodrama. The Jew mil-
lionaire is too maladroit to be true. Yet,
with all its defects, “Lady Frederick” sup-
plies a glorious evening’s entertainment.
One explanation is that it provides Miss
Ethel Irving with an opportunity to run
the entire gamut of those essentially femin-
ine emotions whicn she has a'peculiar genius
for portraying. She is alternately gay, de-
jected, pleased, disgusted, lachrymose, tender,
exasperated, yielding, defiant; and each
phase is expressed with the utmost charm
and the most restrained and natural art.
Stephen Ewart makes the stupendous Para-
dine Fouldes almost human, a fine feat con-
sidering the artificiality of the character.
Halliwell Hobbes, by studious underplay,
tones down the crude speeches which the
author has put into the mouth of Captain
Montgomery. Warburton Gamble plays the
difficult part of Lord Mereston with ease
and skill: and the portrait of his mother
(except that the matron doesn’t look an
hour over 30) is a credit to Miss Violet
Ley. The subsidiary roles are as well
handled as they deserve, though the blatapcy
of the dressmaker is over-stressed by J|iss
Nelson, and Gilbert Porteous’s boisterous
stage-admiral (in a make-up which more
suggests a poodle than a sea-dog) is some-
what out of the reposeful picture.

** * *
At the National Amphitheatre, Maude
Mortimer, the massive lady with the massive
voice, continues to sing, and to be sung at
by a small Eton-suited lad in the audience,
amidst the warm approval of all present.
Queen and Le Brun persist in their Hunting
Sketch, and Johnson and Wells (colored
comedians) remain unmassacred. “Les
Petits Totos” have retired to the First Part,
but otherwise things are as they were.
** * *
“The Fatal Wedding,” resurrected at the
Adelphi, is having just about as good a recep-
tion as. it had when it put Meynell and Gunn
on the ladder that led up to a partnership
with J.C.W. C. R. Stanford is the only
member of the original company in the cast,
and he still carries a heavy bundle as Peter
Schwartz, Dutch janitor and henpecked hus-
band. The Tin Can Band is there in all its
noisiness. Vera Spaull makes a delightfully
natural Little Mother. Cecil Mannering and
Roland Riley do pretty well in the struggle
to give life to Howard Wilson, the wealthy
clubman, and Robert Curtis, the man about
town, who takes to drink and gives the plans
of the bad woman away. Ada Guildford, the
bad woman who likes wrecking happy homes,
and Ida Gresham, the wronged wife of
Howard whose home is wrecked, also get one
good mark each.

The picture show may be strong enough to
pull the crowd in single harness, but it
seems significant that one after another of
the “palaces” are ;offering the horse a lift
at the wheel. Spencer’s (Sydney) are the
latest to add music to the films, their attrac-
tion being the Windsor Choristers, including
James lies, a boy soprano. The interesting
little crowd croons such sweet things as
“When They Ask You What Your Name
Is” and “Love Me and the World is Mine,”
along with Jude’s old “Home, Boys, Home,”
which sounds like a cabby’s midnight instruc-
tions, but isn’t. There is a good programme
of pictures, some .of which, however, were
delayed by the sluggish arrival of this week’s
English mail, and started to whiz a day
late.
** * *
The Tivoli last week acquired 'lie Kas-
racs, a mixed team of four acrobats who
throw*’ one another about without injury.
They should be hired to instruct some mem-
bers of the Ma State Assembly and the
Sergeant-at-Arms how to transform a dull,
scrambling and objectionable removal from
the Chamber into a bright and entertaining
spectacle. As soon as Stage Manager Willis
gave the cue, “Sergeant, remove the Honor-
able Member for Blither!” both performers
would spring out of their seats, bow grace-
fully, and, after whirling each other round
several times, would clasp arms affection-
ately and roll rapidly across the carpet and
out through the swing doors, in the manner
of a whirling Chinese puzzle. Clark and
Hamilton, purveyors of gay nonsense, Com-
edian Fred Bluett and McLallen and Carson,
the roller-skating pair, are other valuable
prograihme assets. Harry Allister, the
serious character impersonator, has added
the picturesque ’Enry Parkes to his portrait
gallery, and the eminent whiskers of the Past
■wave boldly across the footlights like the
banners of old Romance —or words to that
effect.
** * *
Clement Wragge has packed up his ex-
traordinary collection of stars, sun-spots,
moons, etc., folded up the milky way, and
left the Sydney Y.M.C.A. Hall. On Satur-
day night Joseph Blascheck and Mildred
Wrighton, the really humorous entertainers
who have been amusing the suburban popu-
lation for a few weeks, will return for a
farewell season.
** * *
After a remarkably successful revival of
“The Arcadians,” at the Adelaide Royal,
Tom Walls (who has done a little real race-
riding in the intervals of being Peter
Doody), Gertrude Gilliam, and the rest,
have assumed the voluminous trousers and
petticoats of Holland, where the Hooks come
from. The Tivoli has a strong bill, with the
accent on the musical side: Lamberti, Alex.
Prince, Albert Rees, who “entertains,” and
Margaret Ismay and her girls; also, Spissell
commits assault and battery on Mack, and
Jerry, the image, is disrespectful to Carlton
Max". The King’s nas introduced a quaint
act called “Whizzing the Whirl.” It is a
sort of pillow-fight, and the audience can
join in. Toby Claude is singing her rather
heavily-peppered songs, and Walker and
Scott do high-wire balancing.
** * *
“The Chocolate Soldier,” at Melbourne
Royal, is as it was in the beginning. The
competent understudies are resting on their
laurels, whilst Miss O’Connor and Gaze are
back-in their places. Noel Flemming con-
tinues to spread himself on his announce-
ments' of the things he never does, and the
I’opoffs and Mascha join as usual in the
social disturbance which excites the Bul-
garian chorus to tuneful fury.
“The Lyons Mail,” at Her Majesty’s (Mel-
bourne), is a mediocre play, with some ex-
cellent melodramatic properties, and one
large opportunity. H. B. Irving having
made a one-part play of “Hamlet,” it was
to be expected that he would absorb public
attention in this elderly French device, the
motive of which is essentially the conception
of “Jekyll and Hyde.” The fact that Ste-
phenson crowds his opposing personalities
into one hide, does not alter the dramatic
case. It is merely a matter of closer settle-
ment. LI. B. Irving is somewhat inconse-
quential as Lesurques, and interesting as
Dubose, without giving anything more than
a surface impression of a dangerous maniac
with the rum habit. Whatever Irving’s
Hamlet may have been, there’s no doubt his
Dubose is insane. The impression created
is not such as would be inspired by the con-
duct of an unscrupulous sc-’mp with a sar-
donic vein and a certain cut-throat humor,
but is the effect one might get from watching
the caperings of a humorous homicidal idiot
through asylum bars. The two low-comedy
blackguards, Choppard and Foumard, are
workman-like exhibits, for which Arthur
Whitby and Tom Reynolds are responsible.
The smaller and grimier villain is particu-
larly bright. Courriol is passably done, but
a bunch of Irving’s young men are nervous
and fidgety, and cannot occupy stage space
ia a scene without damaging the general
effect. Miss Irene Browne is satisfactory
as Julie, which, however, is merely a nice-
looking part. Dorothea Baird re-introduces
her Botticelli effects as Jeannette, but more
markedly as Mary Cave, in the curtain-
raiser, “A Maid of Honor,” a part which
she plays with an obvious and persistent
nerve strain. “The Lyons Mail” is for one
week only. On Saturday next, “The Bells”
will ring furiously.
KA UFMANN (Basso) and ZEN I (Tenor)
of the Melba Opera Co. Drawn by Zeni.
The failure of “The Lady of the Pluck
Up,” at the Princess’s, on its all-important
first night, proves nothing against the author
except that he still lacks a sense of propor-
tion. Randolph Bedford, ere this, should
have been hailed as the bald-headed father of
Australian .literary melodrama, and the dis-
tinction remains open to him. The “Pluck
Up,” with a story running on broad, safe
lines, shows his improved sense of how to
make a combination of • characters strong-
enough to hold the attention of the audience,
whilst carrying a slender plot to a common-
place finale. Says the programme :
The action of the play is placed at Pluckup Silver
Mining Camp, Barrier Ranges' (N.S.W.), and extends
over three months. Period. 1884.
The discovery of silver at Broken Hill is the
cause ; the characters are the effect. When
the effect is painfully weak, the fault is
almost invariably in the performance. In
the casting of the women parts, and in the
construction of one of them, Randolph Bed-
ford reveais the weak spot in his otherwise
sturdy mental equipment. Not having culti-
vated the faculty of humorously estimating
the relative values of things, he cannot see
at a glance the difference between foolishness
that is passable on the stage, and absurdity
that isn’t.
The story depends for sentimental interest
—and no mining melodrama can live without
that —on the personality of the name-part
player. The leading )ndv must be youthfully
charming in face, figure and style, tenderly
sympathetic in speech, and quite exotic and
spiritual as compared with (say) Tittell
Brune’s “Sunday,” although equally able to
strike an attitude of indignation. Miss Mary
Herries is none of these things, nor can she
act well enough to create an illusion. Yet
the part, in the hands of a graceful, soft-
spoken young novice, such as Mrs. Maesmore
Morris was of yore., would sufficiently play
itself. Miss Le Croisette, in the more diffi-
cult character of an inconsistent tow-haired
Maudie of the bar, burlesques it out of all
semblance of humanity, even when she might
be easily true to life. Against these draw-
backs, the acting of the mqn availeth not.
Kenneth Brampton is an earnest, manly
hero. Bryant, Greig, Harry Neville and
others are convincing types of “Pluck Up”
civilisation, and Diver is only a little too
intensely melodramatic as the one bad man
in the crowd. The mild old innocent who
is saved from selling a 14th part of Broken
Hill for £5O might be improved upon, but
he is not a conspicuous blot on the land-
scape. The scenery, by Grist, is a pub most
of the time, but thei’e is an effective land-
scape in the third act. Also, an occasional
coach and four adds local color to this ill-
used drama of real Australian material and
manufacture.

At the King’s, there is no slackening of
popularity in “Struck Oil,” so John Stofel’s
mortgage paper will be disinterred from un-
der the hearthstone for another week.
** * *
Nicola, a wizard from the land of the
Beef Trust and Ella Wheeler Wilcox, trans-
pired at the Athenaeum Hall, on Saturday
last. The Yankee has a nonchalant man-
ner, a certain crude, dry humor, and a slick,
clean style in getting through his tricks;
making up in skill wliat he lacks in origin-
ality. A number of old, and a few new, de-
ceptions are perpetrated with the aid of
several cabinets, divers hats, “a pack of
ordinary playing cards,” two girls, some
public-spirited citizens from the front stalls,
and the other ancient prons which have been
the stock-in-trade of princes, kings and blokes
of magic since time immemorial. At a late
stage of the proceedings Nicola permits him-
self to be fastened up with tangible pieces of
ironmongery, and retires to a cabinet what
time the orchestra makes noises remindful
of Mary Gibbs. After a space Nick emerges
with the handcuffs, etc., in his hand, and a
broad smile on his physiognomy. A clown
who pervades the whole of the programme
is rather less amusing than Mr. Bruce Smith.
Fern and Mack, billed as “comic gymnasts,”
perform some fakes of strength, with the aid
of a wire which is not nearly as invisible as
it might be. For the rest, there are Nadolny,
a juggler, and two nice-looking girls who as-
sist the “star,” and about whom this paper
does not propose to say anything unkind.

“Her Sacrifice,” running at WesPs
(Melb.), on Saturday last, looked like a bit
of “Camille” photographed in Mexican
scenerv. In plot, it was only a poor rela-
tion of Dumas’s drama; but it was bright-
ened by plenty of eye and dagger play. A
surprising film was “Forgiven In Death,”
which began with a scene of rural peace, and
ended by dotting the screen with human re-
mains.
* * * * ’
Appearing at the P’ran Lyric (Vic.), last
week, was “The Gunfighter,” a drama packed
with Injuns, cowboys, and disorderly con-
duct. Most of the trouble is caused by a
desperado carrying gun-practice to the verge
of ostentation. Of other films shown, the
most amazing was “The Ends of The
Earth.” Herein a hubby is driven, annoyed
by his wife’s indifference, to the Frozen
North, whither the remorseful matron chases
him. Their reunion in the snow occurs as a
matter of course.

The very latest comers at the Opera House
are a lady, described as the Dapper Dandy
Bov, who has to be taken serio-comically,
and an unaccountable burlesque acrobat,
clothed in conventional pants and _ shirt-
sleeves, whose nom-de-frivol is Ridiculous
Recco. Recco claims to have had the honor
of appearing ridiculous four _ times before
the King and Queen of Spain, in 1910, since
when popular feeling against the monarchy
lias been rising higher. Sam Mayo hap-
pened at the Opera House in the beginning
of last week, and is now a comparatively
old attraction. It must be grudgingly ad-
mitted that Samuel is amusing in a way. He
wears a dismal dressing-gown, and a doleful
expression, and he crouches like a child suf-
fering from colic whilst he croaks . verses
to the congregation. If he can sing or
dance, or both, he is concealing those ac-
complishments at present. He merely croaks
in a dressing-gown, and is said to have been
preceded in this country by an imitator who
was not so funny. May the Lord forgive
that imitator! Binns and Binns are firmly
established on the bill,' and the beauty ot
Alcide Capitaine’s gymnastic exercises is a
joy until further notice.

at their Melbourne Hippodrome on- Caulfield Cup
night, there are, x see, “the 11 Bastrens, equestrian
acrobats, from the Circus Schumann, Berlin. Ihe
line sets the memory groning back in the sawdust.
This Schumann visited Australia as Husband of \ ic-
toria Cooke, the equestrienne, about whom there was
a precious row in Calcutta, after the proclamation of
Queen Victoria as Empress of India. For that great
Delhi Durbar, at which Titheradge read the procla-
mation, Vicerov Lvtton had a number of medals
struck off. After all the Indian potentates had got
one apiece, there were some .left over. Lytton, won-
dering' what to do with them, was seized with a urii-
liant idea. On one of them he had “Victoria, Em*
press of India” obliterated, and “Victoria Cooke,
Empress of the Arena,” inscribed instead. She was
at the time the star of Wilson’s circus, then perform-
ing on the Calcutta Maidan, before it came on to
Sydney. \ iceroy Lvtton descended into the ring one
night and presented the precious medal along with
a glowing speech. The indignation among the Indian
princes was hot enough to make the Ganges boil, and
some of them threatened to fling away their baubles.
Yates of the London World, got hold 1 of the affair,
and held Lytton up to ridicule, and his star set
right then.
AT POVERTY POINT.
The lunch-hour organ recitals at Melbourne Town
Hall, which happen twice a week, supply a want-
free of charge—to city clerks who are off the chain
between 1.15 and 1.45. Also, they advertise the
fact that the Town Hall organ, reconstructed a few
years ago, is a highly respectable instrument, and
that Dr. Price can get some fine effects in popular
classical music from it. The audiences are partly
permanent and partly shifting. Many of the young
fellows who drop in for 10 minutes or so before
getting back to the desk or the shop, had no pre-
vious knowledge of a modern organ’s resources, nor
wore they aware of their capacity for applauding a
Wagner item. Thus, Price, with his organ recitals,
is something of a recruiting sergeant for the little
army of music-lovers. The recruits are raw, but
they look likely to be licked into shape.
“Cockaigne”: Miss Eastlake, just dead at 56,
was Barrett’s leading lady in “The Lights o’ Lon-
don,” “Romany Rye,” “Silver King,” “Claudian,”
and other productions, with Willard as the villain.
The quartette of Wilson Barrett, Willard, George
Barrett, and Eastlake could never be equalled by
Barrett’s rival Terriss with his support. Yet, Terriss
was better than Barrett; and George Rignold was
better than either.
Adelaide is to have a new theatre. Harold
Ashton, on behalf of J.C.W., Ltd., has bought a
block of land at the corner of North Terrace and
Victoria-street, opposite Parliament House and the
railway station, and thereon the co. is going to
build the customary “best theatre in Australia,” to
seat 3000 people.
Miss Eastlake, who had been insane for nearly 20
years, died the other week, aged 56. She was the
luckiest melodrama actress of her time, playing
leading lady parts with Wilson Barrett whilst he
was exploiting “Lights o’ London” and subsequent
money-making plays. Miss Eastlake “created” the
character of Nellie Denver in “Silver King,” and did
it very well in her angular, emotional way.
From Edwin Geach: “Cassandra” (B. 21/9/’ll)
is surely in real life either the president of the Ice
Carters’ Association or the Pessimists’ Club. Already
he is preparing to hold a post-mortem on the picture-
show business, the decay of which he asserts has
already begun. As a matter of fact, nothing could
be wider of the mark. Before the introduction of
the picture-show it was estimated that, roughly, only
25 per cent, of the population attended entertain-
ments of any kind ; some kept away on account of
religious scruples, and others because they could not
bring the youngsters with them. To-day it is esti-
mated that fully 95 per cent, seek rational enjoy-
ment in theatres and picture-halls. Cut out the
latter, and what is to happen to those who have a
prejudice against the music-hall and the home of the
drama? Will they be content with the Optimists’
Club, or will they, like “Cassandra,” who has taken
to betting, indulge in more harmful pastimes? It
may be taken for granted that as yet the picture-
show is in its infancy. [The Bulletin isn’t taking
any hand in this controversy. It is merely reminded
of something. Last week it saw a shabby old man
doddering along the street. His face looked familiar,
and presently this paper recollected. He was a once
hefty speculator, who declared, in 1891, that the
land boom was only in its infancy. There is no
more connection between this event and the picture-
show business than there is between a giraffe and
the Norman Conquest. The item is merely put in to
fill up space.]
“Sr ”: The lamentable First Part has been struck
in the stomach by the axe of Progress at Hobart —-
the very last place where one would expect to find
the axe of Progress striking any stomach. Brennan’s
show used to include all the old stand-byes—the four
blackened minstrels, each with a fruity voice and
the Jenolan Cave for a mouth; the evening-dressed
and well-fed interlocutor; the serio-chronic; the
dashing soubrette in a short frock who sings about
Bertie; the stout tenor; the basso tremendioso;
and the rest. They have all been obliterated into
outer darkness. The First Part is now part of the
Second Part, and the Second Part is that part of
the Second Part which hasn’t become part of the
First Part. And the change appears to work all
right.
Pathfi’s “Australia at Work” picture-show has
just completed a remarkable tour in Victoria. M.
P. Adams ran it for more than 300 consecutive
nights without a pause, the 300-odd displays being
distributed among 250 towns and townships, after
Melbourne and suburbs had been exploited. A
similar tour, with a new assortment of industries,
is being arranged for N.S.W.
Taits have got the Australian rights of the
Kinema color-process in picture work. They will
introduce it at Melbourne at the Glaciarium when
they resume possession in October, and thereby
settle the vexed question whether Kinema color films
now faithfully reproduce the actual appearance of
things, or continue to confuse the tints as they did
when the process was in its infancy.

Sailor.” It is under weigh now.
STAGGERGRAMS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The popularity of moving pictures has greatly
diminished the number of theatrical companies visit-
ing the smaller centres of the Dominion —Press item.
Oh, for the rant of a bellowdrama,
For the virtuous tears of the injured wife,
For the villain dark, who fain would damn a
Hero’s joy with a carving knife,
For the rolling R’s, and the good stage thunder,
And the hansom cab, and the baby’s pram.,
And the glitter and gore and shouts and wonder
That have yielded place to the flickergram.
They are seen no more, and we miss them badly,
While the endles pictures come and go,
And the sprinting corpses chase us sadly
Through the flickering streets of the endless show.
Up and down and around they stagger,
And we wonder what it is all about,
While we sigh for the roar and rant and swagger
Of the good old mimes that are all knocked out.
Concert companies still can find us,
Or a fiddler fiddles the livelong night;
Or raging amateur shows remind us
Of the stirring hours of our lost delight.
But the pictures come, and the pictures flicker,
And the gaff that we loved no more is seen,
And it seems that the world has all got shicker
From the staggergram on the jiggering screen.
MAORILAND.
D.M.W.

ANOTHER STORY.

“Brass Button” : The N.S.W. North Coast
steamer Rosedale takes up the cry of the
Yongala for an Act compelling all pas-
senger ships to carry “wireless.” It is sinful
that good lives should be lost for the lack of
such a cheap and easily rigged arrangement.
The “wifeless” might not save the ship; but
even if the vessel sunk before assistance
could reach it, it is long odds that some of
the passengers and crew would be floating
round on gratings and fittings for hours
after the final plunge. If a call for help
were “wirelessed,” search boats would be able
to steam straight to the scene, and might
rescue some of the floaters before cold and
exposure chilled thepi into throwing up the
sponge. And in the regular traffic-lanes
round the Coast, there is a good chance of a
message being caught by a steamer near
enough to make a sprint and get in even be-
fore the dike, or very soon after it. Which
is another strong argument in favor of com-
pulsory sparks where voluntary ones are
hanging fire.
The 34-year-old coasting steamer, Rose-
dale, which disappeared with all hands in
the southerly gale which raced up the
N.S.W. coast at the end of last week, has been
affectionately compared, by a former com-
mander, to a faithful horse. The chief differ-
ence between a horse and a ship is that,
when a horse gets old, there is no way of
repairing him and sending him out to face
the same big fences that he flew gaily in his
youth. An old ship, on the other hand, may
have an artificial youth or second child-
hood. Its steel muscles, sinews and skin can
be renewed in dock. Still, ships will persist
in aging despite the shipwrights. The Rose-
dale had been steeplechasing for a long, long
time, and had suffered a couple of bad falls.
The last accident happened early this year,
when the "patriarchal craft got a tremendous
pounding at the Bellinger River. The sal-
vage job by which it was preserved for the
final tragedy was reckoned to be one of the
finest ever done in these parts. It was
dragged from its resting-place, repaired and
entered for all open sea events as if it were
a fresh young salt colt. The Rosedale ran
well and faithfully until the disturbance of
a few days ago. Then the straggling field
of coasters Weighed in one short. No sea-
faring person seems to have a satisfactory
explanation of the disaster about him. The
only outstanding facts appear to be that the
Rosedale was a venerable steamer, that the
marine fences are as high and as stiff as
they were in the days of Noah, and that
David Jones is as young and boisterous as
ever. Jones never seems to lose his punch.

The S.A. Labor party is wroth with the Verran
Government tor proposing to bring out immigrants,
either men or women. Yet domestic help is practi-
cally unget.table in Adelaide, except at famine prices,

without meals, while girls hire themselves out there
for ss. a week.

“Te Babarantiko’s” tribute to his shirt-
tail crew
We were working the
coast of India, collecting
cargoes for a Parsee mer-
chant, with about 50 nigger
lumpers—men, women and
children. We never ex-
pected much from them or
the spindle-shanked crew, and
the first emergency did not
disappoint us. One thick,
rainy night, just before eight
bells, as, according to cus-
tum, I gave a pull on the
braces, there was a roar, and
over the hooker went, taken
flat aback in a squall. We
were under easy sail, show-
ing nothing above the ’gal-
lant crosstrees; and the flying
jib was stowed. By the ac-
companying flash of light-
ning I saw the jibboom
snap off, and come up on
what had been the weather
side with two jibs stand-
ing; also I saw the two
lookout men petrified with
horror. Then the darkness
shut them out. Just pre-
viously the decks had
been filled with shirt-tails,
yet in that moment, every-
one had disappeared. A
short but energetic search
failed to locate any in
the dark, and loving en-
treaties to them to come
out were equally futile.
Fortunately the squall was
short; nevertheless we three
whites had a pretty strenuous
half-hour getting the vessel’s
head up before we could
respond to the “old man’s’’
soul-stirring apepal to “Get
some of the rice-eating black
swine out if you have to
kick their backbones through
their skulls! ” In the ’tween
decks a pair of lean shanks
showed from behind a beef-
cask, and a hearty yank
dislodged the chief serang.
Finding he was not dead,
biit likely to die soon
if he failed to get a
move on, he gradually routed out the other rats, and
we belted them into clearing ship. Something of
that sort has been my experience on every similar
occasion, and thg blasphemy of other British officers
in relating their experiences satisfies me that the
complaint is general. That is why I shall be able
to read the news of the awful doings aboard a big
P. and 0. or British-India liner any day without
being unduly surprised.

“J.G.” (from Victoria) Quite agree with
The Bulletin (7/9/T1) as to the great
urgency of tossing our whole political and
municipal fabric into the melting-pot, and
building a big bonfire under it. If’instance,
I live in the parish of Wallan Wallan. My
mails are posted and received at the Wallan
East post office, and the railway station op-
posite the post office is the Wallan station.
My name is on the ratepayers’ list of the
Shire of Merriang ; upon the State electoral
roll for the electorate of Bulla; and on the
Federal roll for Mernda. I am in the police
district of Wallan, but have to go to Kil-
more to get fined the usual 5s. or 24 hours,
when recovering from a jag. If I want to
rent a pub. I have to apply to the Broad-
meadows Clerk of Courts for the necessary
license. If I want to go mining I have to
apply to the Bendigo mining district for a
miner’s right. I am in the Broadmeadows
division of Bulla, in the County of Bourke,
and in the Seymour division of the Federal
electorate of Mernda. * My kids have to go
to the Upper Plenty State School, whilst the
boys will have to attend military drill in
the Moonee Ponds area. If I want water
laid on to my humpy, I have to apply to the
Kilmore Waterworks Trust. I am a few
other districts and divisions of various sorts,
but the above list is enough to go on with.

“Field-Marshal”: The aeroplane is going
to make the battle of the future a short,
straight-ahead function. During the recent
German manoeuvres, the birds of petrol are
said to have collected in 35 minutes, infor-
mation which would have taken a strong
force of cavalry four hours to obtain. The
Lancers, Dragoons and Hussars, who have
hitherto had the job of locating the enemy,
are overbearing persons in hysterical clothes,
with spikes on their heels and the upholstery
of bears on their heads. The Life Guards'-
men, who occasionally assist, are even worse,
for they are partly canned, and, when on
parade, bear a strong family resemblance to
a consignment of tinned beer before it is
labelled. Ft is generally understood that the
cavalrymen have been privileged to look
down on the soberly-garbed multitude be-
cause they did all the scouting and rode into
the frequently toothless jaws of Death in
search of information. If the sputtering sky
machine helps to moderate the swagger of
the overdressed horse chauffeurs, I will shout
the first ’plane I meet a pint of foaming
gasolene.
The N. S. Wales Health Department has
found the “guaranteed pure” swindle too big
to overcome, for the present, anyhow. Up
till recently, the department had sought to
make it a sine qua non that the label,
“guaranteed pure,” should carry an actual
guarantee under the State Pure Food Act as
to the quality of the article to which it was
attached. But thereabouts it was discovered,
as usual, that many imported goods bearing
the legend “guaranteed pure” were not up
to the standards required by the local de-
partment ; in fact, that some of them ought
to be labelled, “guaranteed cronk.' Never-
theless, when the department learned how
expensive it would be to the poor oppressed
importer to obliterate the words, “guaran-
teed pure,” it gave way, and now the guar-
antee which is not a guarantee at all but a
plain, common or unscriptural take-down,
may roam at large and do its worst. And
about the only way to avoid the worst is to
insist on getting home-manufactured goods
all the time and every time.
Colonel Roth, who with a sword in his
hand and a wall to his back, could hold off
probably any dozen other doctors in Sydney,
has been pricking another blister on Aus-
tralia’s reputation. The lean, lank Austra-
lian, in the raw state, hasn’t much of a
“chest,” and the “expert” who reckons him
up by European text-book standards rushes
off, as a rule, to some medical congress or
Tory conference, and pours out a jeremiad
about “the decadence of the Australian.’’
Roth, however, who knows what the long
but narrow-chested Australian can do,
bulges out his own front until it shows an
expansion of 6in., and then says “Pooh!” to
the performance, and declares it mere knack.
“I’ll put an inch on any man in a month.”
he says, and infers that the man wouldn’t
be any the better for it. Roth says the aver-
age chest expansion of the Sydney fire-
brigade men isn’t more than 3in.; and yet
there are few finer bodies on this small
planet.

“J.F.H.” : The Anglican Bishop of Perth
has a letter on the usual subject in a recent
issue of the London Times. Here;is a slab
of humiliation :• —
I want to create a bishopric for the Goldfields.
£10,000 is required for an endowment. I have
been promised £6600. Will any of your readers
help towards the balance?
Why England should be asked to subscribe
some £3000 odd for the supply of religion to
the place where the “richest square mile in
the world” resides, is baffling to the non-
religious mind. By the way, Canada has
already leapt heavily on this discreditable
practice of cadging for church purposes in
foreign parts, as though those cadged for
were cannibal islanders. Is the Australian
any less self-respecting than the Canuck.''
THE “LIBERAL” PUSHITE STATES THE CASE
FOR AN IMMEDIATE DISSOLUTION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
But the sinister thing about Mr. McGowen’s state-
ment was the affirmation, in effect, that the House
had not become demoralised. The unparalleled scene
of Tuesday evening was not due to disorderliness
among members, so we are told, but to the disorderly
conduct of a few members who were ejected. Thus
it appears that an Opposition of fO members in a
House of 90. which has in a body made protest
against the Speaker’s efforts at suppression, is to
count for nothing. — S. M. Herald, on the recent
Parliamentary riot.
Is there no Justice in ther blanky world?
’Ave orl our ’owls and eursin’s bin in vain?
Does six weeks’ steady riot count jer nixf
I use the words in bitter grief and pain.
’Ere wuz our crowd, fair lustin’ fer the fray ;
Defective rolls, electorates unchanged—
On bang into ther “Lib’ral” party’s ’ands,
As though by some wise Providence arranged.
Wuz it ther game ter wait; ter form fresh rolls,
And redistribute seats? My oath! Not much.
’Oo ever ’eard o’ sharpers ’oldin’ back,
Wot ’ad a seemin’ sitter in their clutch?
One Thing alone (I spurn to call him Man),
I mean, of course, this blighter Willis, stood
Between the Law Abiders and their aims.
Wot wonder that we clamored fer ’is blood!
Our only ’ope, with Willis Speaker, wuz
By threats, disorder, moans, yells, stoush and whines
Ter force the bloomin’ dissolution, wot
We couldn’t ’ope to get on legal lines.
And ’ow we worked ! I’ve outed referees,
Blue-metalled umpires, rubbed ’em in the grime,
And jobbed and kicked orl sorts of arbiters,
Bight down ter billiard-markers, in my time.
Vet never ’ave I battled with the stern,
tin tirin’ frenzy of the parst few days,
Nor ’eaved so many insults, nor blasphemed
So loud and free, and in so many ways.
Is all that toil to go unrecompensed?
Ah, then, ’ave Truth, Fair Play and Honor fled
Farewell the Right and Pure!... ’Ark ter that
scream,
Where good old British Justice falls down dead.

Professor William Salmond, of the Otago
(M.L.) University, who recently stated in
the Loanly Isles that Prohibition was a
quack remedy, and that intemperance would
never be cured by drastic compulsory legis-
lation. has since replied to the infuriated
howls that descended upon his argument.
Asked why he and others like him were op-
posed to the schemes of the Raspberry Fizz
cranks, he remarked, amongst other things:
We do not wish to see the country flooded with
falsehood, perjury, moral cowardice and duplicity,
such as are inevitably called forth by laws which
are too repressive and run a-tilt against common
human nature, and convert innocent acts into crime.
We fear the creation of a moral pestilence walking
in darkness, in truth, worse than the destruction that
wasteth at noonday. We shrink with becoming dis-
like from the introduction of a regime that will in-
flict, a feeling of humiliation on the greater part of
the manhood of the land, by bringing them under a
moral tutelage fit only for children and savages.
To this the anti-liquor party has replied that
there is no proof that “emotional” legisla-
tion has always failed and always will fail in
M.L. Which leads The Bulletin to sug-
gest that, if the cranks will despatch a real,
hard-doer into the drv districts of their coun-
try, and pay all expenses, he will return in
10 days with a collection of horn-back centi-
pedes and illuminated snakes that will defin-
itely close the argument.
If circumstances don’t make a brick for
the S. M. Herald to throw at Australia,
that malignant anti-Australian goes into the
brick-making industry itself. One day last
week its London correspondent sent an
article on the great strike in England. In-
cidentally, he remarked: —
It is highly interesting to an Australian to see the
workers in England being led, in this case undoubt-
edly with success, by men whom Australian workers
have rejected. The reason why Australia rejected
Mr. Tom Mann was that Australian workers have so
important a share in political control that they can
carry through their objects without recourse to such
a hopelessly ponderous and wasteful method as a
general strike. The English working man does not
yet possess political control; and such measures of
reform as the Radiea_ Government has undertaken
on its behalf have habitually been emasculated or re-
jected by the House of Lords.
But while the London correspondent threw
the,brick at the House of Lords, the dread-
ful anti-Australian Herald headed the
article, “The Great Strike: Mobs and Ball
Cartridge. Australian Agitators in the
Field”—the “Australian agitators” being
the English Tom Mann whom, on the Her-
ald’s own showing, “the Australian workers
had rejected.”
A CORONATION
VICTIM.
MISS SOFFGOODS :
“Mothaw has
been pwesented
to the Queen, Bwidget.”
BRIDGET : “Th' saints presarve us! Will the
mashter be allowed to marry agin?"
“Billo” —his belated protest, with an eye
to next Coronation :
The decorating habit has grown on Australia to
such an extent that the country now requires next
to no provocation to hang flags along the principal
thoroughfares, erect triumphal arches, and obscure its
statues by sticking flagpoles round them. The evil
dates from the rags-and-tatters orgy that flapped
round George of York and his consort May in the early
days of the Commonwealth. Previous to that time,
public rejoicings were marked by a decent restraint
in the matter of bunting; when a man wisneu 10
THE SOPORIFIC PREACHER
SHE: “The Rev. Wilkins is something of a faith-healer, you know; and lam thinking of
attending his church for my rheumatism."
HE: “I can recommend him for insomnia."
indicate that he was loyal, or drunk, or getting
married, or feeling sorry that someone was dead, he
ran up a flag on the pole over his warehouse or shop,
and let it go at that. In those times, a thorough-
fare like Collins-street, in Melbourne, made a pretty
and dignified show. The long, irregular lines of
flags seen against a blue sky were just sufficient to
relieve the sombreness of the buildings, and give the
boulevard a holiday appearance. Nowadays, the
fashion is to break the expensive wood blocks, and
erect Venetian masts; to blot out statuary with
greenery, flags, and poles ; to wrap buildings in
strips of violently-colored rags, and to put up trium-
phal marble arches made of plaster. These are the
four main sins- of an Australian celebration, and the
plaster arch insult is about the most rancid of the
quartette. Other outrages against common sense and
art are devised for private individuals and firms—
but more particularly by ingenious drapers and ware-
housemen to whom rags are no object. The faded
decoration or drape usually serves some base use
when the days of mourning or rejoicing are over.
When Queen Victoria died, Melbourne tied crape
and other black substances all over itself and
mourned hard for about one calendar week. Then
the pictures of “our late beloved Queen” were taken
out of 10,000 shop windows, and the shop boys used
the outward signs of grief wherewith to dust the
counter, polish the stock and clean the front win-
dow. Present scribe was in a bicycle shop in those
days, and has a distinct recollection of seeing a new
racing machine, wrapped in crape, being led down
the street towards the railway station by a grimy
small boy. The owner of the thing wasn’t dead, and
the small boy didn’t constitute a unit in a funeral
procession; it was merely the last of our national
mourning being used up.
While the wrangle goes on in the Medical
Congress and elsewhere concerning the rela-
tion of the State to the hospitals, the
Sydney institutions are rapidly near-
ing the point where they can go on
no farther unless carried bodily by
the Government. Recently the di-
rectors of Prince Alfred Hospital
waited on Acting Chief Secretary
Flowers and presented him with the
choice of handing the institution a
cheque for £5OOO or taking the blame
of closing 100 occupied beds and
throwing out 100 patients. Mean-
time, Sydney Hospital is getting
ready to celebrate its 100th birth-
day (October 30) with a big art
union, and hopes to dredge sufficient
from the gambling instincts of the
public to kick the wolf backwards as
far as the front gate. Unfortu-
nately, the animal will not remain
there, let alone go away and chew
his head off, under the present sys-
tem of precarious upkeep by cadging
round with the hat. The sooner the
Government places the hospitals in
the State under the management of
the State the quicker will Socialism
once more show the way to Private Muddle-
ism.
A sale of slum —or, at least, slummy—
property was held last week in Melbourne,
when some of the oldest lane and alley resi-
dences adjoining Latrobe, Little Lonsdale
and Exhibition streets were auctioned. At
that sale all local Asia seemed to have
gathered—Chow, Hindu, Syrian, Arab and
miscellaneous. Asia had dwelt, for many
years, in these crowded back lanes, and loved
them; and when the property came into the
market he rolled up to buy each man his own
blessed hovel so that he might be safe
against disturbance. The fear that they
might be driven out —that their, cherished
smells might be dug up and shifted, and
their aged mouldiness scattered to the four
winds—maddened the aliens, and , they paid
wonderful prices. It was a most successful
sale, and 12 different kinds of undesirable
were said to be among the purchasers.
Those who form their estimate of the
N.S.W. Assembly by what they read in the
Sydney morning papers are not to blame
if they regard Speaker Willis as a demented
person who habitually bites an inoffensive
Opposition out of sheer wantoness. Wood
(in whom, as a result of years of license,
insulting Parliamentary behavior has be-
come a chronic habit, like beer-snatching
or drug-taking) was bumped along the usual
path the other night, and the Herald and
Telegraph supported his contention that he
had created no disorder, insulted neither the
Speaker nor the House, and generally com-
ported himself like a model Parliamentarian
and gentleman. However, the Evening
News came out, 12 hours later, with this: —
Mr. Beeby had moved that the consideration of the
Bill be an order of the day for the morrow.
Mr. Wood rose and proceeded to make a speech.
The Speaker asked him to resume his seat, the
motion being purely formal.
Mr. Wood: Everything’s formal in this House now.
Mr. Willis again called Mr. Wood to order, re-
marking that it was the second time he had had to
speak to the member for Bega.
Mr. Wood: “I don’t care if it is the fourth,” and
continuing to make asides in spite of the Speaker’s
injunctions, the latter ordered the sergeant to remove
him.
Mr. Wood, not exactly resisting, but leaning back
to delay his departure till he had had his say, said,
as the sergeant propelled him gently but firmly,
“I’m glad to be put out by a man like you” (ap-
parently addressing the Speaker)—“you’ll get tired
of putting me out.”
Looking angrily across at the Government Benches,
the member for Bega said, “Oh, you can lookl” He
was still speaking as he disappeared from the sight
of the gallery.
In short, either the Herald and Telegraph
deceived their readers, as regards Wood’s in-
offensiveness, or the News cruelly libelled
one of the leaders of the party it supports.
THE SURVIVAL OF THE FATTEST.
How wise Australia was in refusing to
accept a mad Belgian from the Finnish sail-
ing ship Finland, last January, appears from
the sequel. He looked all right when at
Port Victor (S.A.), but the captain—who
presumably knew him best —kept him in
irons, and the Commonwealth would have
none of him. Bristol (England) papers now
complacently report that he has been
“brought to Bristol and handed over to the
local asylum authorities,” after terrorising
the whole ship on the voyage back. Poor old
Mother seems to be the dumping-ground for
all kinds of foreign rubbish nowadays.
Four seamen were charged in a Sydney
police court, the other day, with deserting
the Britisn ship Lauderdale. Their defence
was that the vessel left Port Huon (Tas-
mania) in an unseaworthy condition, inas-
much as, having no tarpaulins over its
hatches, Neptune would be able to empty
slops into the holds ad lib. After hearing
evidence, the magistrate agreed with the sea-
men that the vessel they had bolted from in
affright was not seaworthy, dismissed the
charges against them with costs, and ordered
their arrears of wages to be paid. The Tas-
manian Marine Board, which allowed the
Lauderdale to go to sea in the state com-
plained of, was fined two months hard labor,
or, rather, that is what it appears to de-
serve.
The Barrack-street, Sydney, Savings Bank
which is uproariously resenting amalgama-
tion with the Government Savings Bank, got
a bad advt. from the disclosures in some re-
cent bankruptcy proceedings concerning an
ex-employee. This individual was “manager”
of the Scone Bank, with a salary of £5O a
year. The branch office was open only for
an hour on Wednesdays and Saturdays, the
“manager” being a lawyer’s clerk at other
times on £9O a year. Except for an annual
visit of the auditor, the bank that isn’t under
State control gave its country manager a
free hand, which he exercised for seven
years to the tune of £6OOO odd. In its
efforts to evade State management the
Savings Bank of New South Wales will un-
doubtedly have the support of every de-
faulter on its list.
The comprehensiveness of some people!
According to the Age :
An extraordinary case occurred at the Hobart
(Tas.) police court recently. During the hearing
of a maintenance case, counsel for complainant said
the husband had made most sensational statements,
accusing his wife of misconduct not only with
counsel engaged on both sides, but also with the
Police Magistrate himself. In this dilemma the hear-
ing of the dispute was adjourned, pending another
Bench being constituted.
Nowadays, ’tis said, when a Tasmanian
J.P. is observed to bound enragedly from the
bench, there is no anxiety. The janitors and
others know he has not been stung—only
accused of human frailty by a litigant.
* * * *
So far, the chief result of the Melbourne
“missing woman” case —it was a “murder
case” until the law told the papers that 'the
fine would be £2O0 —has . been to provide
certain plump policemen with a little needed
physical exercise. After the arrest of Dr.
Peacock four of the law’s limbs went to his
farm at Carrum and investigated. First
they panned off the ashes of a fire, and were
rewarded by finding a few buttons and other
trifles. Then they came across a place
where a largish lump of soil had been lately
disturbed. The caretaker assured them he
had buried a dead horse there; but it was
necessary to be certain, so they bogged in.
The day was warm, and the beast had been
buried deep; also, he was very dead. The
worst of it was that after they had exhumed
the beast they had to cover him up again—
and the nearest pub was at Mordialloc, four
miles off.
* * * *
“Trotter”: Sydney’s housing problem is
paralleled in Old Rome. The worker of the
Eternal City is prejudiced by the local wor-
ship of Ruins. In Italy there
is a kind of expert called the
Regulatore, or Regulator. He
is a City Planner, like that
Burnham, of the U.S., who
has done so well in San Fran-
cisco, Chicago and Manila.
The Regulatore has done good
work in Naples, Milan and
Turin, but Rome thwarts him.
He is confronted at every
turn by superb sites for
modern dwellings, monopolised
b l 7 superb churches for modern
tourists. He can only house
the local worker by commit-
ting vandalism right and left;
and anyhow the public won’t
let him vandalise, since the
churches attract the Inglese
and other foreigners, and the
foreigners mean trade. Mean-
while, the Dago poor, being
unable to go out on the mias-
matic Campagna, herd to-
gether like rats, and the dirt
and insanitariness of them and
their surroundings is enough
to make the dome of St.
Peter’s sob.
The In Memoriam pome is a poor old sub-
ject for a grin, but the following from a
Melbourne daily invites amazement:
There is a grave in the Coburg Cemetery,
A grave of covered clay,
And underneath that mossy bank
My darling father lays.
A suitable damp covering to clay can become
mossy. But what is father laying all this
time? The verse seems to suggest that father
took lessons from Mossy Isaacs, and is lay-
ing a bit under the odds.
LINES TO A MONSTER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The following resolution has been passed by the
■Ohatswood branch of the Women’s Liberal League:
“That this League lias viewed with intense indigna-
nation and disgust the betrayal of his party and the
unjust treatment of their leader by Mr. Willis in his
capacity as Speaker ; and consider that thereby Mr.
Willis has forfeited the confidence and respect of all
true Liberals, and we sincerely trust that he will
never again enter the Parliament of New South
Wales as a member. —Sydney Telegraph.
Begone, false wretch! In Chatswood’s groves
Dare not to come in wig and gown !
Avaunt! With base-born blokes and coves
May you, henceforth, be written down!
Serpent, we scorn you 1 Glide away!
Would that Papa were with us now,
Your carcase vile to nobly slay,
With thunder on his marble brow.
Hence, Monster, hence, or we shall swoon 1
Instanter cease your horrid tricks 1
Ingrate 1 We passed this afternoon
A motion on your politics!
Retire, base wretch, to lands afar!
The Fair henceforth your name will shun 1
(To-night we’ll ask our dear Papa
To tell us what it is you’ve done.)

ABORIGIN-
ALITIES
“Gip”: Ever feel that you want to hug
the blue haze on the hills, the song of the birds,
the scent of the wattles and the air—the air most
of all? No! . Well, spend a winter on Monaro
(N.S.W.) and watch Spring come graciously towards
you. Only the other day, snow; now, masses of
trailing clematis; white, pink, mauve Michael-
mas daisies, and the Golden Glory, swaying and
nodding on every hillside. Not yet, but soon, your
every step will crush a blossom. Purple violets and
mauve and white daisies, tall blue-bells and pink
convolvulus, buttercups, white cowslips and brazen
dandelions are everywhere. But it’s the blue hills,
with the massed snow behind them, that get right
into your heart, and make you love the place—they,
and the glorious air, so pure and crisp and sweet.
In Spring-time the birds are gladder here than they
are anywhere else in all the world. Just listen to the
goldfinches and the wee blue wrens, the bronze and
green honey-suckers and the sweet grey thrushes.
On my leafy bed I blush for two shameless lovers in
the scrub close by. “Sweet Winnie, sweet Winnie, I
want you,” he calls anxiously, and she, pleased,
pauses in her effort to carry away* a whole blade of
dry grass, and lisps back, “I . . .’ll come and kiss
you, and kiss you.” “Get work! Get work!” ad-
vises a soldier-bird, disgustedly, and a jackass roars
laughing at the whole lot of them. Oh, who’d live
in a town!
“Gip”: Somebody asked in this column,
not so long ago, why dogs rolled in unpleasantness.
I ca-n tell him why one dog did. Ballyhooley and
Paddy were both Irish terriers, though in point of
breeding Ballyhooley was much the finer gentleman.
Moreover, he had a smart stump of a tail and an
accomplishment. When requested to he smiled fran-
tically, showing every tooth in his head, in the best
post-card manner. Paddy, on the other hand, was
extremely surly, and the only person he had any re-
gard for at all was the hunter of rabbits. Also, his
tail was long. Paddy’s one aim in life was to make
Bally look small. He treated him with the utmost
contempt, and though Bally could have licked his
head off if he’d liked, he stood it all with meekness.
When robbed of his share of supper, or kicked from
his warm bed into the night, he took it philosophi-
cally, but out in the paddocks, when the hunter was
abroad, his heart would nearly break. No matter
how cleverly he caught a rabbit, Paddy always took
it from him, and, with tail wagging triumphantly,
would carry it off to the hunter. It was Paddy who
was told he was a clever dog, Paddy who got the
pat on the head, and was ordered pleasantly to go
get more. Bally stood in the background wriggling
his body and grinning idiotically. Once, to the
escaped rabbit’s great joy, he turned, and, but for
the hunter’s intervention, would have torn Paddy in
pieces. For this he got the father of a hiding. One
morning Paddy was missing, and for six weeks no-
thing was heard of him. Bally grew young again;
he caught more rabbits than he’d ever caught in his
life before, and he brought them in triumph to the
hunter as Paddy had done, and the pat on the head
and the “clever dog” came his way now. Then one
Sunday, as they walked out together, they stumbled
over poisoned Paddy. He was quite recognisable, and
lying as though asleep. The grieved hunter hardly
dared gaze on his dead favorite, but Bally, after one
long, satisfying look, turned and grinned broadly.
“Hooroo!” he shouted. “It’s himself all right,” and
capered delirously. Then, flinging himself on Paddy’s
body he deliberately rolled. Bally’s triumph was
complete.
* * * *
“Left Hand”: Robert Salmon, of Bool-
coomafta station (S.A.), who advocates the breeding
of kangaroos for dingo meat, and says he practises
the dodge .on his place, and thereby succeeds in keep-
ing dogs off his sheep, is a person of large discourse,
looking before and after. But the cloven hoof of
Discrepancy sticks out beneath the coat-tail of his
argument. A dingo will never tackle an animal that
turns on him, and the ’too does this with rage in his
countenance every time. There are stacks of
seasoned old wethers and ewes that will face a dog,
and they owe their sere and yellow existence to the
fact. The wild dog prefers lamb because it runs
from him, and when lamb is “off” he makes a meal
of young sheep, because it nearly always does the
same as live lamb.
“L.L.” : If it comes to an endurance test
in the matter of burning propensities, the haystack
and the manure-heap, nominated by “The Hack” and
“Pick,” are as rapid as New York society in com-
parison with a smouldering swamp. During a severe
earth tremble in the S.E. of South Aus. and Western
Victoria about a decade ago, many of the swamps
in the desolate border locality had their innards so
disturbed that they retired from the water-holding
business. The next bush fire that happened along
burned the bottoms out of them. This, consisting
of damp decayed vegetation, that had accumulated
since the great wet day of Noah subsided from the
hilltops, smouldered for months, and in one case for
three years; but I wouldn’t be exact to a day, as
the old thing may be alight yet. Am also rather
hazy as to the precise locality—Vic. or S.A. —as the
imaginary boundary-line in those parts used to be
about as shifty as a frightened horse. [The Bulle-
tin begs to mention here two long-distance fires
which seem in danger of being overlooked. One is
a mine at Broken Hill, which has been burning for
many years. The other is the coal deposit at
Wingen (N.S.W.), which has apparently smouldered
since Deuteronomy.]

“Jimmy Crow”: The blue-tongue lizard
that bit “Wandewoi”'(B. 3/8/’ll) is not venomous.
The only weapon of defence of, the blue-tongue
(“bogi,” “bob-tailed gohanna,” or “sleepy lizard”)
is a jaw that closes like a rat-trap, and hangs on like
a cast-iron bull pup. This was probably the cause
of “Wandewoi” losing her thumb nail. “Bogi” is
useful in killing, snakes,- and there is no risk in it
for him, because the wriggler’s fangs will not pene-
trate the scaly armor of the plaintiff. He grabs for
just behind the snake’s head, and once he gets a
grip the defendant can take his choice of making his
will or ordering his. coffin. He won’t have time to
do both.

“Condamine”: If the ants that trouble
“K.V.8.” (B. 7/9/’ll) are “meats,” let him (1)
boil a piece of raw meat in a solution of equal quan-
tity of arsenic and washing-soda, and hang it near
the nest; (2) pour a quantity of carbon, as used in
rabbit exterminating, over the nest and apply a
match. The intruders will pass out, as though the
Lord’s hand had smitten them.
LITTLE HOPE.
LADY : "Tell me, poor man, is your blindness permanent?"
POOR MAN : “It looks like it, mum. Times is that bad, I don't see no chance of retiring."
“Mr. Ocky”: I sympathise with “Metro-
politan Cat Proprietor” in his trouble with the cat
that won’t catch rats. When Tabby first entered
my domicile he was a good, honest, solvent cat, but
now he is absolutely unmanageable, and has intro-
duced and established here half a dozen of his most
intimate friends. Besides doing all the objectionable
things mentioned by “M.C.P.,” they scramble through
the open window of my bedroom every four minutes
throughout the night (except Sunday, when they have
a prayer meeting on the roof), and in the morning
it takes the housekeeper about an hour to replace the
property I have thrown at them. They won’t leave.
Laid some strychnine for the wretches, and, as a con-
sequence, my prize dog is now under the soil, whilst
the cats are still increasing and multiplying after
their kind. As-soon as the good dog died a mongrel
rose from nowhere, and now he won’t go either. He
has regular exercise after the cats, which as often as
not seek safety on the mantel-shelf; and the blighted
mongrel bites my leg when I seek to forcibly put
him outi The cockatoo has eaten the door of the
wardrobe and the leg of the round table; the talking
parrot will not talk beyond swearing when the minis-
ter arrives ; the pony must have oats’with his chaff,
and drink with his mouth a-t the tap instead of from
the bucket, and the goat— —-. Excuse me. There’s
Strawberry bumping at the front door for another
Jonathan apple.
“Gip”: There must be a different fashion
in foxes along “Condomine’s.” way than in the
Monaro (N.S.W.), when he can talk (B. 7/9/’ll)
about the most effective method of poisoifing them.
Up here, at the beginning of the season, when you
lay baits, you’re almost certain to get a catch; but
try that ground again the following week, and not a
bait will be taken. No one can poison a ; fox here
now. Men have drawn a three mile trail, and laid
vainly every conceivable bait —caul fat, lambs’ -
tongues, sheep’s hearts, livers, tripe, bullock’s kidney,
congealed blood and other wild fowl —without suc-
cess. Not so long ago I laid a suet bait near a dead
sheep. That night I got a fox. Dropped another
tempting bait on the same spot, and two evenings
later saw a fox amble up to the.sheep, sniff about,
and pass on. I followed as far as the sheep, and
found that the bait had been turned over and left
for the unwise. Poisoners would grow rich on
Monaro if there were any' way of getting' foxes in
numbers.

“Rypo” : Was asked to “a party” at the
MacNab’s the other week. It was stipulated that
we should bring our own refreshments. Towards the
close of the evening our hostess took up a collection
to defray the hire of the piano. McNab simultane-
ously commandeered a small sum per head for , wear
and tear to the carpet and furniture. After the
party it transpired that enough eatab'-s, etc., were
left to last the family a week. It also leaked out
that the piano was there on a time-payment basis,
and that the receipts of the evening were sufficient
to pay the first three instalments on' it. Am still
debating as to whether the above is an instance of
economy, hospitality, financial genius or highway
robbery.
“Pick” : Now and then our esteemed fel-
low-citizen, the rabbit, without any apparent reason
win entirely forsake a locality long infested by him.
Between Lake Tobowrie and Durras Mountain, on the
South Coast, near Ulladulla (N.S.W.), a few years
ago, the four-legged anathema swarmed on the low-
lands, and completely honeycombed the sandhills
and flats with his warrens. The few settlers allowed
the enemy undisputed possession. But, about 18
months ago, when the season was unusually good
and the grass plentiful, the rabbits, to a bunny,
suddenly packed their Saratoga trunks, took second-
class single tickets, and left for parts unknown. I
have observed a like exodus from the Bodalla estate
and from a portion of Bungarby, on the McLaughlin
River, and I think it very probable that a similar
trek from North Gippsland has led to the recent row
among: Abo. writers.

“9-3-11”: So far, nobody has nominated
red-heart, a timber which frequents the coastal scrubs
of Queensland, for the hardest wood championship
of Australia —which shows how little some people
really know. The first time I tried to carve red-
heart, I thought I had run against a steel tree, care-
fully painted to resemble the real goods, especially
when I saw the sparks fly. I reckon I’ve been up
against them all, but that wood—-if it is wood, and
not a money-lender’s soul —is the nearest approach
to steel I have ever seen, read or heard of. Any
scrub-cutter round Gvmpie way will back what I
say—unless he would rather commit perjury.
THE CASE OF HENRY WORRALL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Sir, —I hasten to thank you for the breadth and
humanity manifested in the article on the case of
Mrs. Hop Gooev, which appears in to-day’s Argus.
... If, by industry, courage and sobriety, a man
like Mr. Gooey can win the respect of hundreds of
the people of Geelong, is this the way to express our
national appreciation of such qualities ?... What
can the great and crescent empires of the East
think of the kindergarten politicians of Australia?
... God pity us. Henry Worrall,, Methodist Par-
sonages, Brunswick. — Extracts from an infuriated
epistle in the Argus.
Hop Poon Gooey is a simple-minded Chow,
And Brother Henry Worrall is an earnest Metho-
dee;
And, down beside the Pivot, many pious people bow
To this Yellow Man who figures in the centre of the
row
Which is raging in the Argus with extreme
velocitee!
Hop Poon Gooey, well-concealed behind his smile,
Hides a sack or two of cunning and a barrow-load
of guile,
So he wandered off to China and acquired a
familee.
Then he started raising infants ’midst the cabbage at
Geelong,
While Australia said to Fisher: “Ain’t this gettin’
rather strong?—
Here’s an industry that’s booming, and we don’t
protect the Pong”-
Which aroused the Tory Argus in the cause of
pietee 1
Hop Poon Gooey has a lot of earnest friends —
Oh, how this persecution pains the local Pharisee!
So the honest Tory paper hollers loudly and pretends
To be filled with grief for Gooey, who (at intervals)
attends
To the growing of the carrot and the useful cab-
bagee!
Hop Poon Gooey, as I think I said before,
Though a simple-minded heathen knows a trick or
twenty-four,
And his yellow pagan cobbers rub their hands with
pagan glee;
For when parsondom gets going, then the politician
soon
Finds it suitable to practice at the same partic’lar
tune,
So perhaps the Door may open for each cabbage-
growing Poon,
As Canaan to the Hebrew did (see Deuteronomee).
Hop Poon Gooey goes to chapel at Geelong—•
He sings the hymns of Sankey, for an earnest man
is he;
And this is the modern gospel preached in private
to the Pong:
“Be a Clistian, give ’em tluppence, play each Sun-
day loud and stlong—
If you want to go to China and obtain a familee!”
Hop Poon Gooey, on behalf of brother Chows,
Tries his scheme experimental with the aid of those
who Wowse —
And every Pong is watching with extreme anxie-
tee;
For if Hop Pong Gooey grows more infants at Gee-
long—■
If the Argus hollers loudly till the brides from
China throng,
Then the honeymoon will flourish, and the parsons at
Hong Kong
"Will watch their converts vanishing—all heading
southerlee.
Bop Poon Gooey and his brideless brother Chows
Simply put their trust in Worrall and those parsons
who espouse
The doctrines and the dirges of his drab philoso-
phee.
So it seems the Case of Worrall.is the thing that
matters here—'
He’s the Tory’s faithful ally ’neath the holy man’s
veneer,
As his hastening .towards the Argus clearly indi-
cates to me (
Therefore, White'Australian stalwarts; would dp well
to cogitate
On the dangers Of the Pulpit and the’perils of the
Plate,
When the same is waved by champions of the
Yellow Agonee!
For the politician panders to the parson and the
crew,
Who grow hoarse in praise of Gooey, and the Tory’s
anguish, too,
Is a sign for men to ponder as they dodge the
Chinese pew
Which adorns the tabernacle of the maddened
Methodee!

Kidman’s Man” : The recent meeting of
the Bovril Company, in London, had Australian inter-
est. The company possesses a huge cattle station,
Victoria Downs, one of the farthest removed from
civilisation. It is from 1500 to 2000 miles distant
of Sydney, Brisbane or Adelaide. One hundred
thousand cattle are reported to be upon it. Anyone
who has travelled knows it is blankly impossible to
number the cattle on such a place. There is what
is called the book estimate, but it is difficult to make
London shareholders understand why every horn and
hoof is not ticketed off. Some have been complain-
ing that the estimate is exaggerated. Kidman holds
15,250 shares in this company. Rason, the chair-
man, read a letter from him, as the best
Australian authority, declaring his belief that
the cattle numbered 100,000. There are about
8000 on the routes for the cities. About 13,000 will
altogether be disposed of during the year. Kidman,
while holding that prospects are bright, hints the
risks of such fearful travel. He says the beef ought
to go in tins.
* * * *
“Kodak” : Hobway was a mild man sober
and a wild man tight. When he was tight, he
smashed things, like a full-powered cyclone; when
he was sober, he repaired with exquisite skill the
damage he had done, and made what new furniture
was necessary. In 15 years he had practically re-
built and newly-furnished his home eight times.
Mrs. Hobway used to retreat placidly with the
children to her brother’s place, 10 miles away, when
a bad fit got him, confident that he would make
good the wreck by the end of the bout, and probably
add some new fittings as a bonus. One night she
arrived with the family, and quietly remarking that
“Joe was at it again,” took off her hat and started
to help get tea. That night Hobway', left alone,
saddled his horse and galloped into town to finish
his spree there. During his absence a terrific wind-
storm tore down the mountains, blew down the trees,
burst the home and strewed it all over the selection.
Three days later, Mrs. Hobway, accompanied by the
family, drove up in the dray to the fence, and won-
deringly viewed the dispersed ruins. A look of
pained surprise crept into the gentle woman’s eyes,
and she turned to her eldest girl. “Alice, child,”
she remarked sadly, “I’m afraid your poor father’s
getting worse. He never went to this length before.”
