“Pick”: Is “Mallarapoonie” (B. 10/8/T1)
quite sure that the north-west and Carpentaria
black stays to eat the remains of his dogs after the
poisoning raids described? If so, the niggers there
have a less wholesome fear of the occult than in
most other parts of Australia. I have frequently
known sheep-owners or ’possum-trappers to remove
blacks from a favorite camping-ground by poisoning
their dogs. In every such instance Binghi’s first
concern was to get as far and as quickly away as
possible from the “debbil-debbil” and the dead.
“Left Hand” : Bushmen hoard their mem-
ories of murder trials and such-like horrors, and
treasure them above everything else. I have heard
many a “case” dragged up from the tomb of an-
tiquity and argued to rags round a camp-fire. Once
1 saw two men, who had been mates for years, fall
out through taking opposite sides in the matter of
a murder trial 17 years old. Another time an old
bush pal of mine, who had married and settled
down, wouldn’t speak to his wife for five days be-
cause he swore that Deeming had red hair and side-
whiskers, and she said his hair was black. One
night a bloke and a cove rolled up at my camp, and
after tea they and Joe got discussing a big divorce
case that had happened 10 years previously. After
a lot of argument, Joe said quietly: “I was a wit-
ness in that case, boys. She was a bad lot.” “She
was that,” replied one of the men. “Did you know
her?” Joe asked. The man grinned. “I was the
‘other party,’ ” he said. Then Joe fell on the ‘other
party’s’ neck as if it was the throat of a long-lost
brother, and the two went over the old ground again
and again until morning arrived. They were still
at it when I went to work.
“9-3-11”: How’s this for child-slavery?
Know a seven-year-old boy on the Tweed River
(N.S.W.), who is compelled to milk nine cows
every morning of his life. After milking, feeding
sundry calves and doing divers other things, he has
his breakfast, and then proceeds two miles to school.
Most of the distance he has to run, in order to
avoid trouble for being late. Returning from school
at full sj>eed, he has to round up the cows again.
Indeed, the only time he can steal from his cold-
blooded exploiters is the lunch-hour at school. This
is not an isolated case ; there are hundreds of others
in the Tweed and Richmond districts. In Murwil-
lumbah there is a man—-anyhow, he has the same
shape as a man —who proudly boasts that he takes
all that is possible out of his sons. I’m glad that
the Royal Commission, which is to inquire into this
brutal sweating of Australian children, was ap-
pointed by the Labor Government. At least, some-
body told me it had been appointed, although I
must confess that I haven’t seen it wandering round
anywhere. Perhaps it fell down dead after the first
day’s evidence.
“Gip”: Talking about the effects of
poisoned tucker, a Tubbutt man’s dog (Tubbutt is
a back-o’-the-north-wind sort of place on the Vic-
torian side where dingoes still abound) —as I was
saying, the dog threw in its soul after tackling a
strychnine bait. Next morning the owner went to
give an old pal decent burial, but he found the
dog’s bones, and lying beside them an extra size in
dingoes. But strychnine won’t kill a well-con-
ditioned pig. If you want to make waste pork of
him pass him the phosphorus. Crows fatten on
strychnine; hawks and magpies die with the first
beakful. Again, a phosphorus-poisoned rabbit ap-
parently has no ill-effects upon a dog, while a
strychnine-poisoned bunny will sometimes kill him
in the air while taking his first jump.

“Yalgun”: Would advise the coachy (B.
31/8/’ll) to move into a pub at once, as a horse’s
leg will set. There is a horse on the road between
Bonalbo and Casino, working in a carrier’s team,
that has completely recovered from a broken thigh.
Also, I know a Narrabri stockman, whose horse
broke its front leg near the fetlock. Having no suit-
able weapon wherewith to slay his property, he
pulled the saddle off, and left it. Two years after
he encountered the derelict with a well-set, if crooked,
leg.
“Gum-Sucker”: Re “Pick's” yarn (B.
31/8/’ll) of the sheepfold which burned for 18
months. I was up Gippsland (Vic.) way in 1908,
when an old sawdust heap caught fire. Called on the
conflagration six months ago, and found it still
burning. There is little to choose between old saw-
dust and Hell for the Aged Smoulder Stakes.

The argument as to whether poisonous
snakes climb trees and other upended vegetables, is
hereby finally and honorably settled. Each side
agrees unanimously that the other knows nothing
about the matter, and rather than appear dis-
courteous, The Bulletin agrees with both parties.
If any contributor wishes to appeal against this de-
cision, let him appear in person on Tuesday, which
is the day the Religious Editor sharpens the teeth
of the office bloodhound.
“Expert” supplies some shed statistics:
Jim Powers (killed in S. Africa) shore 315 at Bareena
or Rockwood many years ago, and Jack Howe (now
a pub-keeper) 321 at Alice Downs, making an Aus-
tralian record. These are the two best old-time
tallies. Teddy Fern shore 233 big merino wethers
at Manuka (Queensland). He is still going strong
at Blackall. The day Fern shore 233, Ruhini, the
Maori, shore 333 in Maoriland. His subjects were
border Leicester wethers, as big as bullocks, but
crutched right over the tails, and with no wool at
all on the heads or points. Dan Cooper, at Winton,
in 1910, shore 316, and Ted Dean 283 at Alice
Downs in 1908. The latter, with a stop-watch on
him, shore 12 lambs in 10 minutes. Harry Living-
stone, in ’O4 or ’O5, cut 253 at Maxwellton. He is
now travelling in the interests of a shearing-machine
co. up Charleville way. The big left-hander Seary,
at Charlotte Plains, three years ago, unclothed 274;
while Zimmerle, the same day, performed the same
office, alongside Seary, for 244. That Seary has lost
none of his speed is shown by his Blackall scour
tally of 260 ewes on August 29 this year. Lately
chatted with Livingstone, Dean and Les Seary. The two
former will not shear either in shed or matches unless
they are allowed to use one brand of machine, but
long Les Seary is open to use any make. I hap-
pened to have a few machines other than what were
being used on the board, so handed him one, and he
shore all day with it, not losing a stroke. How does
the late championship match compare with these
tallies, which were done as an every-day occurrence
in the heat and sweat of Central Queensland, with
all the rest of the week to be worked?
“Pete”: Said to be the only one of its
kind in Maoriland is a yellow-flowering rata tree,
which grows beside the Mokau River (North Island),
about seven miles up, amongst beautiful but little-
known scenery. The red rata, both the tree and vine
varieties, is very common all over the Dominion, but,
so far, two specimens only of yellow rata have been
known. Both were on the Mokau River bank, and
one has now disappeared. The botanical name is
Metrosiderous florida aurata, and the flowers are
highly prized by botanists. March and April are the
months of bloom.
ON THE SOUTH COWST.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In connection with the compulsory training of
cadets, the dairy farmers on the South Coast are
being considerably inconvenienced on account of boys
being compelled to drill in the afternoons. These
drills take place at the time when milking opera-
tions are in full swing, and as most of the farmers’
boys are engaged in this work, they find it hard to
spare them. — Sydney Telegraph.
It was the peaceful eventide;
Brown’s milkers swished " their tails,
While Brown’s three youngsters sat beside
And filled the gleaming pails.
The thin, white spurts of precious juice
Struck smart and hard like shot;
Brown filled the cowyard with abuse
And mopped his forehead hot.
“Git over there!” He thrust his head
Against the milker’s flank,
And swore again.. “Gor’ strike me dead !
You are a blanky blank!”
To left and right the steady hail
Of cow-juice did not cease ;
The snow-white foam rose in each pail;
Save Brown, all were at peace.
“Gor’ strike me dead, what with yer drills—
(I’ll d —n well murder you!)
An’ Wages Boards and other frills
I dunno what to do 1
“Why can’t they leave us be, I say?”
The toiling youngsters bent
Towards their cows and milked away
Upon their work intent.
“Git over there!” The angry sun
Glared through the cowyard haze.
‘‘Teachin’ you kids to use a gun!
An army !... Spare me days !
“You kids don’t want to learn no drill!
You stay at ’ome with me ;
There ain’t no enemy ter kill,
As fur as I can see.
“And if there is —well, work comes first!
We gotter pay our way.
If cows ain’t milked this game’ll burst,
That’s all I gotter say!”
Each youngster, silent as his brute,
Worked on without a word ;
They had no time to talk or shoot
With that impatient herd.
They had no time to watch the sea
That washed the cliffs grass-crowned;
They milked and listened wretchedly,
Their eyes upon the ground.
The raiding cruiser shoreward sped,
And hung upon a swell;
A gun put out its tongue bright red,
And spat a 12-inch shell.
The cowyard burst —the cows burst too;
The milk was mixed with blood ;
The boss alone was left to chew
The bitter shimose cud.
N.S.W.

PERSONAL
ITEMS
“Bails,” writing in short barks, gives a
send-off to a lately-deceased barrister
Goldsmith, or Goldy, is mourned in Melbourne.
Q.C., but his fame is as the most dashing bat of the
East Melbourne Cricket Club in its early days.
This club, with its famous blue and white stripes,
arose from the veriest trickle. A boys’ club, founded
by A. E. Clarke, Moody, the Murphys and a few
other Scotch College fellows, under the name of the
Abbotsford, playing near Dight’s mills, by the Yarra,
Collingwood. They moved to Richmond paddock,
outside the Melbourne Cricket Ground, and got the
East Melbourne ground bv some mysterious fluke.
It had been refused by Government to the influential
Civil Service Club. The East Melbourne club sprang
up as distinctly colty, with such as Goldsmith,
Wilkie, Goggin, Hastings, Riggall, Eggleston, Allee
(not Allan), Horan, Boyle, O’Mullane and E.
Elliott, against the sober-siders of the Melbourne
club, comparative veterans like Cosstick, Gid Elliott,
Greaves, Coulstock, Hammersley, Wray, Wills, Gib-
son, Wardill and Marshall. ' See A. E. Clarke’s
“History of the East Melbourne Club.” The success
of this club made the M.C.C. far more generous to
colts, the new concern having arisen through its
Toryism. Among M.C.C. noted colts were Jacomb,
J. T. T. Smith, and Tait.

The kirk in Sydney has been sore put upon
by the action of the Stanmore fold in “call-
ing” a youthful immigrant to be its spiritual
leader. Stanmore is one of the best-paid of
the Haggisterian kirks of N.S.W., and sev-
eral dour parsons, who have been chasing
souls in general, and bawbees in particular,
in the back-blocks, feel that the will of Provi-
dence is unco’ deeficult to put up wi’ at times.
The vagrant gospel-miller, who has not
hitherto been in charge of a church any-
where, happened to deliver one sermon in
the Stanmore chapel which hit the deacons,
elders and entire congregation fair in the
conscience, and, despite opposition from the
fathers of the Assembly, they ran him up to
the rostrum, and nailed him to the Good
Wor-r-r-k.
Attorney-General Hughes has been or-
dered to take three months’ rest. Fortu-
nately, he wasn’t ordered to take the waters
at Yassberra. The new Governor-General
hasn’t had a look at these waters yet.
There are three women among the 35 can-
didates who want to run in the Labor in-
terest at the next Senate election in N. S.
Wales —Mrs. Dwyer, President of the
Women’s Organising Committee; Mrs.
McNamara, of the King P.L.L.; and Mrs.
Maincke, of the Darlinghurst League.
R. G. F. Weber, father of Melbourne’s
beautiful wrestler, died at Brighton a few
days ago. He came to Australia in the ’so’s,
and settled in Brighton when it was a forest.
In his younger days ho was a powerful
“local preacher,” and developed his muscle
by expounding the AVord.
Fame! Some enquiring mind has written
to T.P.’s Weekly for information as to the
verse beginning, “Life is mostly froth and
bubble.” Two people sent in the complete
verse, one ascribing it to Gordon, the other
to Ella Wheeler Wilcox! T.P. (28/7/T1)
just prints both names, without trying to de-
cide between them.
Peter Allen, the licensed jester of the S.
Aus. Assembly, had a useful and unusual
present from his constituents, the other day
a 2J-h.p. motor-cycle, to replace his old
push bike. His district (AVallaroo) con-
tains a lot of sand and scrub, with hardly
any railways, and he claims to have pedalled
up and down it for 8000 miles during one
three-years Parliament.
Lachlan McTaggart has lived on Wooltana
station, two-thirds of the way from Adelaide
to Iniiamincka, where Burke and Wills died,
for 54 years. The sheep he depastures
there are descendants of the flock which his
father drove up from Mount Gambier (tak-
ing three months on the road) in 1857. He
is the only man in the district —which is far
in the interior—who has held his land ever
since it was taken from the blacks.
The N.S.W. political pot will bubble
afresh at AVest Maitland, where there will
have to be an election to fill John Gillies’s
old shoes. Gillies, who had been in the
House since 1891, died rather suddenly.
David Brutus Storey reckoned him in with
his Independents ; but, as a matter of fact,
he was just as liable to punch Brutus as to
biff Caesar. He had voted both for and
against every Ministry that held office
during the past 20 years. He had been con-
nected with the pretty powerful Maitland
Mercury most of his life —latterly as part-
owner and manager—and the tolerably
bright member who can get the tolerably
bright local paper to mention his services
three times a week has a strong pull over
the rest of the earth.
The late James Rutherford was the
nearest thing to a Rockefeller or Fairy
Grandfather that Bathurst (N.S.W.) ever
knew. The old nobility
of Bathurst have the vir-
tue of economy deve-
veloped to an abnormal
degree. Speaking gener-
ally, the more affluent
the Bathurst aristocrat
is the dustier and more
insanitary-looking is his
attire, and the less dis-
posed he is to squander
his savings on anyone
but himself. It had been
the convention for years,
whenever any of Bath-
urst’s public institutions
or movements, or what
not, showed signs of
wilting, for the officials
or promoters to gallop
distractedly to Ruth-
erford; and they
mostly left the pre-
sence with a cheque.
It was one of the few
directions in which
the Bathurst Anti-
Socialists showed a
measure of consist-
ency ; they rarely
sought State aid, un-
til they had failed in
an assault on Ruther-
ford.
Since the present
S.A. session began
Acting-Speaker Jack-
son (Labor) has at-
tended to the duties
of Speaker Coles, who
is ill, in addition to
filling his own job as
Chairman of Commit-
tees, and has done all
this work without a
break, and without
any relief. Although
he is the youngest
member in the House,
he is looking an in-
tensely fagged poli-
tician, and he will be the gladdest man in
the House when his double-barrelled engage-
ment ends. Jackson is desperately conscien-
tious, and doesn’t allow a single mption
(even one of adjournment) to be put with-
out being formally “seconded,” a departure
from the established practice for over a quar-
ter of a century.
Of the original 32 members of the Mel-
bourne Stock Exchange, established in 1861,
but one now remains—J. S. Butters. The
early-day members usually met for business
“under the verandah.”
Things are improving; the appointment of
Canon Long to wear the gaiters at Bathurst
(N.S.W.) makes the fourth Bishop to come
out of Trinity College, Melbourne. Green,
Armstrong and Stretch were this new high
priest’s predecessors.
About a cat who watched a king:—
Detective A. Murdock, who has just retired from
the N.S.W. force, came to Sydney in 1884, and had
scarcely been measured for his' buttons before he
got an introduction to “de push.” He was on duty
in Sussex-street when the historic engagement hap-
pened between the Gipps-street and Bathurst-street
blokes. There were some mighty bad marksmen
among the combatants, or else Murdock’s luck was
in, for five ineffective shots were fired at him while
lie was commandeering one of the opposing chief-
tains. The bullets lodged in the door of the old
Derbyshire Arms Hotel. Also, he partook of some
hot stuff at the famous Bondi free fight. In fact,
the stuff he partook of was generally hot. It was
scorching one night when a gas explosion at his
home fired a friend through the window, and Mur-
dock, burnt and shaken, staggered upstairs and
looked to his small children. Then he staggered
down again and cabbed to the hospital to inquire
about his blown-out friend ; but it was an uncon-
scious Murdock the attendants carried out of the
cab, and it was a very sick and scarified Murdock
the doctors and nurses watched in the weeks follow-
ing. He helped Roche and Rochaix to shoo the
anarchists away from the present King and Queen
during their Australian tour, and perhaps that' was
the coolest job Murdock ever tackled.
IF THEY DON'T HURRY.
It was stated in the Federal Parliament last week
that some of the youths who were measured for their
uniforms many months ago, had not yet received
them. — Item.
Message Boy (year 1960): “Here y’ are
Mistah, your uniform. The tailor’s only
Just finished it .”
OLD MAN (Australian born): “Ah, yes, I can
mind when I was measured for it. I believe
it was just a year or two before the Japs
came.”
Passed in at Tunbridge Wells (Eng.) re-
cently, William James Tyrone Power (eldest
son of Tyrone Power, one of the great
comedians of his day), soldier, magistrate,
diplomatist and author. He served in
China, Maoriland (1846-47), through the
Kaffir war (1851 to ’53) and Canada. At
the end of the Kaffir war, in 1853, he was
appointed a commissioner for the settlement
of frontier claims, and thereafter held im-
portant offices until 1876, when he was
made Agent-General at London for Maori-
land. He was 92 when the hour-glass ran
out.
Percy Sholto Douglas, ninth Marquis of
Queensberry, has sailed from Liverpool for
the United States with the intention of be-
coming an American citizen. “There’s no
room for me here,” he said to the reporters.
‘We are played out. The day of the lord in
England is over, and I am going where the
man and not the title counts.” He is dis-
gusted with a lot or things, one of them
being that “the other day the Liberals made
a wealthy Jew, who is in a sanatorium, a
peer of England.” The Marquis adds that
the blessed nobility to which he belongs is
entirely destitute of constructive states-
manship.”

“Red Tape, junr.”: G. E. Brodie, or G.
E. Beresford Brodie, sometime Director of
Government Asylums, Chief Audit Inspector
and filler of other billets, is still the problem
of the N. S. Wales public service. Last
year the P.S. Board made him acting-
accountant in the Lands Department, a
job said to be worth only £550, though
Brodie’s old salary of £750 was not, and
apparently could not be, reduced. Since
then the permanent accountant has retired,
and G.E.8.8. is again gazetted as the stop-
gap. A couple of our chaps, who had their
eyes glued on the billet, have thus been
blocked on their way to promotion, and
when I take them up in the elevator each
morning I don’t need to switch on the cur-
rent. Their language would make a balloon
soar.
There met. at Adelaide Show, two men
(natives of the State) whose families have
done well for South Australia. These were
Patrick Kenny (of Colton on the West
Coast) and ex-Works Minister Laurence
U Loughlin. Kenny is one of four brothers
(all born in the State) who have 49 chil-
dren among them, while O’Loughlin and his
brother-in-law have 20 sons and daughters,
lhat is 69 children among six fathers.
Nearly all the second generation are either
farming or keeping sheep in S.A.
By “Cricketer” :
Tk e .l a te Louis Goldsmith was one of the best
Victorian batsmen of the ’7o’s, and a champion
long field. It was one of the regrets of his life, that
m playing against W. G. Grace’s team in 1873, he
missed W.G. in the long field. In ’7O he played
against Sydney University, one of the Uni. team
being Edmund Barton. This was the start of a
friendship which ended only with Goldsmith’s death.
Old-timers tell of the occasion when, in a game be-
tween East Melbourne and Melbourne, in ’7l, Ford,
of Melbourne, hit a ball out of the ground. Boun-
daries being then unknown. Goldsmith scaled the
fence, fell, and was knocked senseless. As he was
out of sight of the players, no one knew what had
happened until eight runs had been scored, and then
they went to look for Goldsmith. A compromise was
arrived at, giving Ford six runs for a lost ball.
Tommy Horan, and others of that period, still argue
on the legality of this decision.
“Religious Editor” : The fact that £71,000
worth of Bourke-street, Melbourne, was
sold, the other day, at the rate of £1075 per
loot, makes me wonder whether Paradise is
only another Melbourne or Sydney with the
did congestion disease in a severer form.
Ihe Bible’s references to golden streets may
be merely the picturesque Hebraic method of
saying that property values there are in-
sanely high, owing to the immense accumu-
lation of souls from this and the countless
other worlds which revolve without any ap-
parent reason in the vast and lofty engine-
room of Space. I can’t imagine any Cohen
walking regularly throughout Eternity to his
celestial pawnshop over an 18-carat pave-
ment. My theory is that the land there is
quoted at about £987,000,000 per foot, and
that when the chilled and penniless soul of
the land monopolist from another sphere
blows up to the Gates, and reads the gold-
leaf signboards of the Better Land and
Estate Agents, it crumbles utterly and drifts
slowly downward past the incandescent stars
as an insignificant pinch of dust.
MR. C.H. GOODE, Philanthropist, and
South Australia’s oldest Rag Merchant.
Norseman,” from Lautoka, Fiji, pictures
the pleasant things that happen when the
nigger is introduced “to aid in the develop-
ment of the country” :
Compared with Victoria, Fiji is a doll’s house
alongside a Tyson property; yet the last sitting of
the Supreme Court here was polluted with nine
coolies’ offences, embracing rape, murder, bodily
harm, forging and uttering, and bribery. At Taviuni
you can see two big cuts in the mosquito net on a
man’s bed. The cuts landed where a head was sup-
posed to lie. Luckily the head had shifted itself.
In this country heads get into the habit of doing
that. They have to. At Rewa, an Indian has been
enticing black damsels away from their homes. The
black ghoul practically Jack-the-Rippered them.
With many salaams he eventually admitted that he
ran a human rape and slaughter-house. And there
is another side to this awful black slavery. I have
seen black women being carried off the sugar fields,
in the noon-day heat, to give birth to their infants.
The treatment of the women is scandalous. A week-
ago an Indian slashed the fingers off his female
“freehold’s” hands, because she put her mark to a
secret agreement to become another black gentle-
man’s “leasehold.” Another Indian here has leased
his wife for £B. When you ask what the country
is getting out of this pollution, the only answer is
that it is helping to provide big dividends to a few
people who never see it, and small jobs to another
few people who mostly wish that they had never
seen it.
SOLACE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mr. A. A. Billson (Minister of Education) motored
to Sunbury yesterday, to lay the memorial stone of
the new State school building. A silver trowel,
suitably inscribed, was presented to the Minister. —
Melbourne Argus.
When soon our Minister is placed
Upon a high, dark shelf in haste,
Since the electors are unkind,
By frequent promises unbribed,
Some consolation he may find
In trowels, suitably inscribed.
When kids and teachers are forgot,
And prancing parents trouble not,
He will remember, to the last,
The few kind pushers who imbibed
Big bealcers to his health, and passed
Him trowels, suitably inscribed.
Will he forget ’em—those that came
And piled on him all sorts of blame,
And those that worked him all they knew,
And those that swore and those that gibed?
Will he forget ’em, for a few
Poor trowels, suitably inscribed?
VICTORIA.

The State Bank of South Australia, estab-
lished in 1896 :

1911 .. 6662 44,848
This State Bank borrows on mortgage bonds at
from 3* to 4 p.c., and lends at the uniform rate of
4* p.c. The advances are for from five to 42 years.
According to the Act of 1895, they were to be made
“to farmers and other producers, to Local Authori-
ties, and in aid of other industries, at reasonable
rates, on convenient terms, and upon proper securi-
ties” ; but by an amending Act, the advantages were
extended to “all persons possessed of the necessary
security.” Farmers have, however, borrowed the
bulk of the £1,786,763 so far advanced. Repay-
ment is made by instalments. On agricultural land,
borrowers who take the maximum term of 42 years,
pay 16s. 6d. p.c. p.a., making, with interest, £5
6s. 6d. p.c. p.a.—much less than, on the average,
farmers had to pay in interest alone a few years
ago. The benefit conferred by this State bank upon
farmers is not, therefore, to be measured by the
amount of the bank’s own advances; hundreds of
Anti-Socialistic backbones that never go inside its
doors have had their interest bills reduced to the
extent of hundreds of pounds through this Socialistic
concern’s operations.
As money is available through the repayment of
loans, the bank’s trustees are obliged to repurchase
mortgage bonds, and then issue fresh bonds to raise
the money for new advances. This shows the opera-
tions for some years past:—
New Old Bonds Increase of Total
Bonds Repur- Bonds Out- Out-
Sold. chased. standing, standing.

The advances made have totalled £1,786,763,
of which £966,944 has been repaid, leaving
£819,819 outstanding, or £34,409 in excess of
the amount borrowed on bonds. This excess repre-
sents part of the reserve fund; the balance is in
premises. The reserves are not at all formidable;
but they are mounting up steadily, and, if the pre-
sent low rate charged for advances is not made still
lower, they should henceforth increase more rapidly,
because the expenses are gradually coming down in
proportion to the total outstanding. A few years
ago, when the bank borrowed at £3 15s. p.c., and
spent 15s. p.c. in administration, there was no
margin at all, seeing that advances were made at
£4 10s. p.c. ; in fact, no profit at all would have
been shown in those days had various small fees
not been charged for services. Nowadays the exes,
have been reduced to 12s. 4d. p.c. of the mortgages,
and as the total of advances increases, the figure
should come still lower, and the fund for contin-
gencies should become more substantial. The next
table shows the expense rate: —
Expenses p.c.
Advances Working of Mortgages.
March, Ourstanding. Expenses. s. d.

S. Aus. has been on the up-grade for six or seven
years now, hence, naturally, the bank hasn’t had
securities thrown on its hands. Yet, even so, that
the arrears of interest over-due six months and more,
should amount to only £lO6, is a surprising per-
formance. For it must be remembered that this
isn’t an ordinary bank. When the half-year ends, and
the interest falls due to the ordinary bank, you don’t
take alone any cash as a rule ; the bank simply adds
the interest on to the overdraft, rules a line, and
starts with a slightly bloated entry. But when in-
terest falls due to the State Bank, hard cash is
called for. As always happens when people work on
C.O.D. principles, both parties then know exactly
where they are; and though losses are still possible,
the chances of the parties drifting or being dragged
out of their depth, are enormously reduced.

The half-year’s earnings were at the rate of £9
11s. 9d. p.c. p.a. on capital and reserves combined,
and the magnitude of those figures suggests: (1)
that this bank has an extremely profitable connection,
and (2) that there are few, if any, over-valued assets
or dead accounts, which is another way of saying
that the reserves are the real thing. Certainly big
profits might spring from another source —the use of
a big proportion of public money. Thus a bank
which has the use of £lO of the public’s money for
every £1 of its own, ought to be making more profit
than one which has the use of only £5. But big profits
earned in that way mean big risks, because no matter
what may happen to the securities, the depositors,
etc., will expect to get back their money intact.
The Commercial of Tasmania, however, stands very
well in this respect, for it makes its big profit in
spite of the fact that for every £ 1 of its own
money, it has rather less than £4 10s. of the pub-
lic’s in the business.
The principal movements in the past 12 months
are shown next:—
Liabilities.
Aug., 1910. Aug., 1911.

Advances .. .. 1,414,756 1,355,936 Dec. 58,820
Premises .. .. 51,124 49,614 Dec. 1,510
Bigger deposits, smaller advances —that ought to
mean reduced profits, along with reduced risks. The
total amount owing to the public is £1,710,643;
and the very satisfactory proportion of 39 p.c. of that
is held in liquid forms. Altogether, the figures call
for bouquets rather than bricks, though the investor
isn’t likely to be attracted by the fact that there
are only three directors, one of whom is managing
director, and that the accounts are apparently never
submitted to the inspection of an auditor appointed
by the shareholders.

This co.’s luck has been mixed during the year. The
Yongala was lost with all hands, the Colac and the
little Kepler were wrecked on the Westralian coast,
and the Flinders was burned so badly that it was
turned into a coal hulk. One result of all this is
that a bite was taken out of the Insurance Fund. It
is an amazingly profitable concern, which, in spite of
these disasters, can still pay a 10 p.c. dividend on
stock that has been heavily watered. Yet it wasn’t
always so affluent. Only a year before Federation
arrived the £lO shares could be got for less than
par. The capital was then £202,870, and the shares
were held in a few hands. Probably the owners of
those few h.auds knew a thing or two, and had been
steadily buying; anyway, in 1900-1, it leaked out
that things were tar better than they had seemed,
and shares sprang up to £25. Inter-State shipping
increased enormously; trade in and with Westralia
flourished ; and, instead of fighting one another for
the plums, the shipping cos. fixed matters nicely
among themselves. The future depends largely upon
that honorable understanding; and whether it will
be maintained, or whether the Commonwealth law
will rip it up, this paper doesn’t know. So far,
when somebody has squeaked about abnormal freights
—from North Queensland, for instance —and called
the honorable understanding a thief, somebody else
has chipped in with the remark that the ring has
resulted in far better boats and a more regular ser-
vice ; and that is so plainly true that the first squeak
has been drowned.
How well or badly a shipping co. is doing isn’t
usually apparent from its published accounts. Few
tricks are easier than for such a co. to hide its
profits by writing down its assets —in other words,
by paying for new ships out of profits. Yet the
money that is paid for new ships isn’t all profit, be-
cause those new ships may be more or less replacing
old ships that have been turned into hulks and
lighters ; so even if you know what money has been
put into new shins, you don’t know everything that
is worth knowing. It is possible, though, to get
some results by digging. Take this, for instance: —
, 1906. 1911.
Assets, other than steamers,
freeholds, wharves, and
shares in other cos. ..£174,134 £234,319
Liabilities 39,226 74,724
Surplus . . . . £134,908 £159,595
The surplus of more or less liquid assets has thus
increased by £24,687 in five years. Meantime,
though no capital has been got in from shareholders,
the tonnage of the co.’s fleet has been increased, in
spite of recent losses, from 53,689 tons to 61,637
tons, and large payments have been made on account
of four other steamers, aggregating 25,600 tons.
Also, various hulks and lighters have been added,
and it is well-known that large investments have
been made in coal and other properties. From all
this it is quite certain that the co., besides paying
its dividend, has been making very large provision
for depreciation and contingencies. There is a cer-
tain vagueness about that, no doubt; but, anyhow,
it seems a safe conclusion that, on the past record,
the 10 p.c. div. is very sure unless some extraor-
dinary run of bad luck strikes the fleet, or the bottom
drops out of the pleasant little arrangement in which
the cos. now null together.

Sydney has four ferry cos. Two of them are in
clover all the time; the other two find a patch of
the vegetable only now and then. This is one of the
other two. It has no excursion traffic worth men-
tioning ; and nobody goes where it plies unless he
has to, and then he usually goes by tram. Some
few years ago the directors thought they might mend
things by buying picnic grounds on the Harbor
foreshores; and though some of this land has since
been sold, “Freehold properties, wharves, etc.,” still
stand at £34,739, and apparently the return from
this asset is very wretched indeed. Probably it
takes most of the net earnings from this source to
pay interest on the £15,000 or so of mortgage and
other debt on the property. The recent history of
the co., as told in the table printed above, is bad
enough; yet the truth may be even worse. All
depends on whether enough is written off to provide
for depreciation of the co.’s ferries. Something is
alleged to be written off; but the amount isn’t hinted
at. All that can be arrived at is this: Three years
ago “ship properties” stood at £45,762; they now
stand at £45,613 —practically the same. But, mean-
time, the Lady Chelmsford has been added, and four
ancient arks have been disposed of. Therefore, in
the three years, the fleet has been written down
to the extent of the difference between what was paid
for the Lady Chelmsford, and what was realised for
the four arks. There doesn’t appear to be anything
very drastic in that writing down. It probably
means that the co. is putting aside enough for de-
preciation to buy a new boat every five years or so.
Still, this is all more or less guesswork. Assuming
that the directors are writing off just enough, then in
the past three years, the co. has actually paid away
more than it has earned, in spite of the fact that in
two half-years there was no dividend at all, and in
another only 5 p.c. This doesn’t inspire much con-
fidence in the co.’s financial management. No doubt
the Board has a hard row to hoe; and the fob seems
to call for a better hoer than the company has got.
The City Electric Light Co. (Brisbane) :

July, 191 u.. 8,600 12 ~ 6,, 4549 34,499
Jan., 1911.. 10,537 12* „ 6„ 4998 40,038
July, 1911.. 10,284 12*,, 6 „ 5222 45,100
The true reserves are less than shown in the last
line, and so probably are the true profits for the half-
year. The true reserves are less because “Purchase
account” figures as an asset worth £35,258, and all
or part of this is goodwill. The nucleus of the
business belonged to the Brisbane Electric Supply
Co., which sold out to this co. six or seven years
back, and this item dates from that time. And the
true profits are probably less than £10,284, because
£3lBO of them has been added to a renewal, replace-
ment and contingencies reserve, and some or all of
this may represent the half-year’s wear and tear;
though it has been the co.’s practice so far to pay for
current renewals out of current revenue. Neverthe-
less, a time may come when extraordinary renewals
may be called for, and then this fund may be
drawn upon. Making very liberal deductions to cover
this and other contingencies, the profit is very hand-
some, and the obese dividend on ordinary shares is
very comfortably earned.
As mentioned above, a Purchase account —presum-
ably goodwill—figures among the assets for £35,258.
Now, the co.’s right to live is based on various
Orders-in-Council, conferring titles which expire in
1940. In that year the municipalities may take
over the co.’s undertaking by paying a fair price
for the plant, etc., used in the business. Goodwill
is not to be paid for. Therefore, it becomes neces-
sary to wipe out the paper asset ( £35,258) by 1940;
and an appropriation of £lOOO a year is being made
for the purpose. Consenuently that amount also may
fairly be deducted from the profits shown above. If the
municipalities do not exercise their right in 1940,
they may do so 10 years later, or at the end of
any subsequent 10 years. Until they do so, and pay
the then value, the co. is entitled to go ahead;
and it evidently reckons that there is more ahead
than there is behind, seeing that, in the past three
years, it has put £47,508 into mains, plant, and
buildings. Of this sum £22,936 has been provided
by shareholders (the capital is now £96,227) ; the
balance is undivided profit. The co.’s financial posi-
tion is very easy: its cash and book debts exceed all
its liabilities.
Winchcombe, Carson, Ltd., formed in
Sydney in July, 1910, to take over the business of
Winchcombe, Carson and Co., Ltd., pastoral property
and products salesmen, as from April 1 of that year':

The present co. paid £41,983 for the goodwill, but
the old co. transferred a reserve fund of £10,254,
thus reducing the goodwill item to the £31,729
with which the foregoing table makes a start.
The net value of the business, including the
goodwill, was put at £105,755, and 105,755 £1
shares were issued to the shareholders in the old co.
in full payment. The public was offered and greedily
took another 100,000 shares—-largely, no doubt, on the
strength of the prospectus statement that “the net
profits of the business during the five years ended
Dec. 31 averaged £20,774 p.a This rate has
been considerably exceeded during the current year.”
It is probably the co.’s bad luck that in its first 15
months, and notwithstanding all the new capital put
into the business, it has netted less than the old co.
used to average in 12 months. No doubt the past
year hasn’t been quite so profitable for the wool-
broker as some recent ones have been, owing to the
shrinkage in values, and, consequently, in commis-
sions ; also a good deal of the capital is being put
into new premises, etc., and isn’t yet productive.
Still, giving all that in, the results are disappoint-
ing, especially when compared with those that were
previously recorded. A stock and station and pas-
toral finance co. which, in these times of prosperity,
mops up 85 p.c. of its profits in paying a 6 p.c. divi-
dend, could certainly do with a larger helping of
luck, before times of the other sort hit it.

But in considering the profits an eye must be kept
on the increase of capital; in 1900 the total was
£lOO,OOO, and it is now £198,760. Still, on the
whole, the past four years have given reasonably satis-
factory results. Nothing, certainly, to get at all
excited about, because there has been very little to
lay by after the strictly moderate 7 p.c. has been
paid on ordinary shares. The 6* p.c. on pref. shares
looks better; but when a pref. dividend of that size
has to be offered to attract capital, it doesn’t say
much for the fiscal laws of the country. Offer 5 p.c.
for pref. capital in a bank, and even though it was
of less repute than Kaiapoi is as a manufacturer of
woollen goods, it would no doubt get the money.
Yet surely, especially in a wool-producing country,
the law ought to show that it has as much regard
for a woollen manufacturing co. as it has for a bank.
If really effective Protection were the settled policy
of the country, there would, moreover, be less need to
go to shareholders for the whole of the trading capi-
tal of a concern of this" kind; the banks would
supply a great deal more of it than they do to-day,
and on more reasonable, terms. As it is, they view
commercial paper with a sniff of suspicion, and rely
mainly on land-mortgage business, which isn’t bank-
•ug at all. Things being as they are, and banks’
support being uncertain, this co. has certainly
strengthened its position by increasing its capital.
Yet, even with its new capital, it still has to carry
a considerable liability, mainly because increased
business means a bigger stock, and more book debts.
This indicates various movements: —
1900. 1909. 1910. 1911.
Capital .. .. £lOO,OOO £139,375 £150,715 £198,760
Liabilities .. 93,483 108,323 89,327 52,947
Properties and
Plant .. .. 70,070 117,311 100,103 98,166

Stock .. .. 118,388 135,000 139,847 149,342
One has to take a great deal on trust in a case of
this kind, because the value of the stock must be a
matter of estimate only. Moreover, it doubtless
includes much raw material which is liable to con-
siderable and sudden fluctuations. To that extent
the business is speculative, and profits are almost
certain to fluctuate —for which reason the wise man
won’t pay much regard to the figures of any single
year, or even of any two or three years.

Though Albert Borchard signs the accounts as audi-
tor, and though in most cases that is good enough
guarantee for anything, The Bulletin would like
~L S co -’ s present statement better if it were put
differently. The trouble is that the Profit and Loss
account shows “net profit on trading, after payment
of. . . .rent, salaries, dividend on pref. shares, sta-
• il er ~ v ’” etc. Why should dividend be included
with rent and stationery ? It wouldn’t matter so much
if it were possible to ascertain the amount of this
dividend, for then the total could be added on to the
profits; but that isn’t possible, because this capital
has been gradually dropping in during the year. As
a result, the directors’ report presumably mis-states
the facts: “Your directors recommend payment of a
div. at the rate of 10 p.c. p.a. on the paid-up capital
of the co., which will absorb £2745.” Apparently that
is only on the paid-up ordinary capital. The total
paid capital is now £71,831, in 20,000 pref. shares
paid to 10s. (with £6847 paid in advance), 50,000
ordinaries fully paid to £l, and 10,000 ordinaries
paid mostly to 10s. The prefs. have been issued
during the half-year; and ss. has been called on the
last issue of ordinaries. This new capital has re-
sulted in the reduction of the co.’s liabilities from
£58,425 to £44,763, including £31,950 of a plaster
on the city freehold bought a while ago from the
Cooper estate. Capital, reserves and liabilities are
represented by: Freeholds and leaseholds, stock and
plant, £58,485; book- debts, £74,684. Book debts
include the amounts owing on time-payment bar-
gains. The reserves apparently include provision
against losses on these transactions. The published
accounts themselves nut the matter this way:—
Profit (as per Unexplained
P. & L. Additions to
Account). Reserve Fund.
June,1909.. .. £4137 .. ?
Dec., 1909 .. .. 5822 .. £2120
June, 1910 .. .. 5585 .. 1448
Dec., 1910 .. .. 7339 .. 3261
June, 1911 .. .. 6580 .. 3592
Given normal conditions, the losses on time-payment
bargains are surprisingly small. The shop that sells
a thing straight-out on credit is pretty certain to lose
the thing if it can’t collect payment. If the worst
comes to the worst, it stands in with other creditors
for (say) Is. o*d. in the £. On the other hand,
if the time-payment man’s customer fails, the goods
can be carried back to the shop ; if he fails early in
the proceedings, the goods are then practically new;
and the longer he pays the less likely he is to fail.
With a sharp slump in national prosperity, throwing
large numbers out of work, such a co. as this would
no doubt be up against trouble; but even then it
would be much better off than the concern which
gives credit without any security at all.

Money, which ought to be by this time almost en-
tirely unknown in Australia, and especially in N. S.
Wales, is more plentiful than ever it was; and the
political prophets wiio explained that it would for a
certainty flee to parts unknown, are raking it in
with both hands, and investing it in the country just
as though the country was not “doomed.” New
suburbs are being opened, and new roads made, and
“Father of One,” who wrote the bitter letters to the
papers about the impending ruin, orders another
load of gravel for the sidewalk, just as though things
were going to be all right. And the affluent person
who wouldn’t trust the Labor party to run a pie-
stall, let alone a country, is pressing a few millions
upon that party; and the party, unfortunately, is
taking the millions, and putting them, or some of
them, into gravel and road metal. Thus, one way
and another, this co. is having a good time ; though
the good time had a cloud thrown over it the other
day, when the Government announced that it had
got rather tired of providing quarry-owners with 10
p.c. divs., and was going to be its own quarryman
in the future. Probably that will affect this co. to
some slight extent. The turn of the tide of pros-
perity will, however, affect it more. There are some
things that man buys sparingly when times are bad,
and gravel and road metal are two of them; so we
haven’t to go back more than about six years to find
this co. “passing” a dividend altogether. Since
then it has certainly put itself on a firmer footing.
Five years ago it owed £3328 in excess of the value
of its assets other than quarries and fixed improve-
ments; now it has £8703 of those assets in excess
of liabilities. That means an improvement of
£12,031 —not all the result of trading, certainlv,
because £6BOO of new capital has been got in. Still
practically all that capital was put into quarrv
property and appliances. The five years’ record is
excellent; but, as already indicated, there is liable to
be a very serious difference between the years of
general prosperity, and the other years when the
community is whipping the cat.

This co. owns the premises where the Fattest mer-
chants meet and tell one another that the country
is going to the dogs. Its own share capital is
£30,000, and it owes £20,437 on debentures; all
this and its reserves it has put into premises, which
cost £70,000, and are no doubt worth a great deal
more —the country having been “ruined” repeatedly
since the buildings were bought. The co., though
composed of men who pursue the profits with great
energy, is content with a modest thing itself; for
the shareholders realise that it brings them indirect
as well as direct advantages. Still, the security is
so good that even 6 p.c. isn’t to be sneezed at. "

The Pinnacles, one of the Barrier’s early-
day rivals of Broken Hill, is likely to have another
trial. There is a big body of ore in the mine, but it
is so refractory that half a dozen cos. have emptied
their pockets over it. A Broken Hill syndicate,
which knows just about all that is known about
silver-lead metallurgy, thinks that flotation can
answer the riddle; and it is whipping up the capital
to take the mine on a 10 years’ tribute, and put up
the necessary plant. The last people who worked it
ran it in conjunction with the disastrous Aldershot
(Q.) smelting works; and after that it was sup-
posed to have fallen into the maw of some of the
Rothschildren, who held the usual little mortgage.
The Kyloe (N.S.W.) isn’t improving. No.
6 level is turning out no better than the disappoint-
ing No. 5. At the north end there is only lin. of
ore, and at the south end an irregular vein averaging
12in. is worth only about 5 p.c.

* * * *
Dividends totalling £528,931 were paid by
the Westralian gold mining cos. for the first eight
months of this year, as compared with £658,643 for
the same term in 1910. The total amount distributed
since the industry started is £21,890,334, or about
one-fifth of the total production.
* * * *
To date Meekatharra (W.A.) has yielded
something over 180.000 oz. fine gold, and it is the
only field in the West whose returns steadily in-
crease. Its backbone-producers have been Fenian
(53,7830 z.), Ingliston Extended (32,7990 z.), Mar-
mont (27,6610 z.), and Ingliston Consols (27,3720 z.).
The management of the first-named show denies the
report, which recently reached The Bulletin,, that
the bottom level is giving some concern.
* * * *
Bullfinch West (W.A.) has bought the
King of the Hills outright, on the recommendation
of its manager, and is now trying to instil hopes
into its faint-hearted shareholders. The manager
thinks the venture may be very profitable under care-
ful management; but what proved to be a good
living for the former individual owner may not be
sufficient to keep a co. from want for any length of
time. It is doubtful if it will.
Yuanmi Co. (W.A.) has suuk to 450 ft.,
and is getting ready to open out at or near that
depth. Both ends of the mine have developed well;
and 10 head of stamps are being hurriedly fixed, m
the hope that treatment may start before Christmas.
* * * *
Great Fingall (W.A.) finds it impossible
to haul sufficient ore from the deep winze workings,
below No. 13 level, to keep the mill fully occupied,
so it intends to hang up a portion of the plant until
the internal shaft-connections are made. Meantime
the gold yield will be helned by returns from a small
plant which has recently been erected to re treat the
sands around the old 60-head mill. The new shoots
lately exposed at the No. 17 and No. 18 levels, and
in the winze below the latter point, are stated to
be something out of the ordinary, and to have given
the mine a decidedly improved appearance at depth.
Payne’s Find (W.A.) is beginning to at-
tract the attention of promoters, and already pros-
pectuses are blazoning the reports of interested
parties. Nothine' so far discovered on the field war-
rants anything beyond a prospecting co., and appear-
ances are not too favorable as regards permanency.
Westralia values its production of miner-
als, other than gold, to July 31, at £2,816,641.
Last year’s output shows a small decrease compared
with that for 1909, though in two or three cases
there were substantial increases. Coal production
grew from £90,965 in 1909 to £113,699 in 1910,
and copper ingot and matte from £45,100 to
£68,657. On the other hand, tin tumbled from
£62,989 to £45,129. The promised increased out-
put of copper from Whim Well is expected to com-
pensate for the temporary stoppage of production at
Phillips River, while the completion of the railway
to the Pilbarra field, and the promise of better times
at Greenbushes, are exnected to improve the output
of tin.
Wodgina, in the nor’-west (W.A.), has
livened up again, and very promising finds of al-
luvial and lode tin have been made within a mile or
two of the old workings.
* * *
Mount Morgan is still suffering from in-
sufficient blowing power. Four furnaces were at
work through part of August, and the output of
matte got ahead of the blowers. Consequently, A 7000
worth of matte produced in August did not reach the
converters that month. Things should improve when
the new blowing machinery is installed in November,
though general manager Richard won’t be quite
happy till the arrival of the turbo generator, at the
beginning of next year. # #
Gilmore (N.Q.) put through 200 tons for
21 tons tin concentrates; value undeclared. this
crushing is the result of much hard work; but, un-
fortunately, it has struck the metal market on the
fall Vulcan’s 323 tons for 20 tons oxide, worth
£1602, indicates a low-grade concentrate, worth
about £BO per ton, whereas Stannary Hills 22 tons
(from 2566 tons ore) were worth £2500, or over
£lOO per ton. Nevertheless, Stannary continues in
the dumps, because it can’t run its mill full time
and cut down costs. * # #
Still they come. Every tenth man one
meets in Central Bananaland has a coal mine up his
sleeve. Blair Athol Extended is the latest to be
added to the growing list of baby cos.—most of
which are foredoomed through the insufficiency of
their capital. ' *
“Kobbi”: Admiralty Mammoth Co.—a
small subsidiary concern under the wing of the
Bluff Colliery Co. (Q.)—has struck coal at 85ft.
from the grass-roots, and is busy getting the first in
stalment ready for the naval people. Naturally the coal
“is better than was got previously on the same
leases. #
Bundaberg (Q.), which is mostly con-
cerned with sugar strikes and severe frosts, has Uken
a hand at mining. A seam of coal has been tapped
three miles from Yandaran, which is 18 miles by
railway from the sugar centre. The seam, struck
at 50ft., is 3ft. thick, but is on a pretty steep dip.
Navigable water is only five miles off, so the show, if
it has any backbone at all, has good chances.
* * * *
The Batavia rush, in the far-north of
Queensland, hasn’t drawn a crowd; at the last count
there were only 56 whites working atthcrclaims
The biggest slug to date is 740 z., credited to White
and Foley, who have also got 470 z. and 15oz pieces.
The scratcher’s chief trouble is the high cost of liv-
ing Provisions come from Coen, 80 miles off, and
the horse-packers are making money while the nug-
gets shine. #
Brilliant Deep, Charters Towers, has had
the long-expected development in No. 4 west, ihis
level, after being driven 284 ft., entered formation
carrying leaders, and subsequently passed through a
good make of stone. A crushing of 67 tons re-
turned £336. It is not known how far this fine
reef will extend along the course of the channel.
No. 2 got into very heavy stone and caused a run
on shares; but the good thing proved fragmentary,
and defective ventilation made it difficult to worn
the reef to advantage. This make of stone is a long
wav from the Brilliant Extendedin fact, it is in a
different lease to the one that adjoins the Extended,
and whose forfeiture has been applied for by its
covetous neighbor.
* * *
Brilliant Extended, Charters Towers, pays
a sixpenny on October 13. There are 250,000 shares,
on which 9s. 6d. altogether has been paid, lhe
present output is at the rate of 1000 tons per week,
but this cannot be sustained for long unless reserves
are replenished, as the stone in sight in May was
only 50,000 tons. The co. owns a fine plant, and is
anxious to secure ground to the west, where its
levels follow the reef nearly to the Deep’s boundary,
and to the north and east, where there is a chance
of extending the Deep levels. Last half-year’s return
was 26,760 tons crushed and 23,600 tons cyanided
for £55,461. Costs were £1 17s. sd. per ton.
Since the half-year closed, in May, better form has
been shown, and the co. has declared two 6d. divi-
dends, costing £12,500. # #
The Great Fitzroy Mines, Ltd., is a three-
year-old reconstruction of the Great Fitzroy Gold
and Copper Mines, Ltd. The latter received 374,512
shares paid to 18s., and £37,451 was then paid by the
shareholders to the new co. That amount being
found insufficient, £75,488 was raised by further
share issues, making the total share capital £450,000.
Another £75,000 has been raised on debentures, and
£20,000 has been advanced by a metal co. A great
deal of the outlay has been due to bad miscalcula-
tions ; and the worst feature of the business is that,
after all, the co. can’t show a profit. For the 12
months to June 30, the working account showed a
loss of £1176. Add debenture interest, and man-
agement and general exes., and you get a loss of
£9837 for the year, without allowing anything for
depreciation; and the directors reckon £17,898 a
fair thing to write off on that account. That would
make a total loss of £27,735 for the year. The
future is still uncertain. At recent copper prices it
was found unprofitable to work the concentrating
mill; but readjustments have improved recoveries a
trifle, and sooner or later a payable practice may
be hammered out. The bulk of the known reserves
is silicious ore worth 3.07 p.c. copper and 2.53dwt.
gold (=47s. 6£d. per ton) ; but the recovery, even
with the help of the flotation process, amounted to
only 81i p.c. of the copper and 47i p.c. of the
gold • and that concentrate has, of course, to be
smelted, and the smelting is expensive (20s. 9.27 d.
per ton, including transport to furnace). Total
working costs ran last year to £1 18s. 6.13 d. per
ton. The metal is there: over 590,000 tons of. ore,
worth 465. 7d., are measured. The problem is to
increase recoveries, or decrease costs, far enough to
show a profit. * » *
A nice button of gold has been got by old-
timer Bren at Rushworth (Vic.). Rush worth had its
rush half a century back, since when it has been
living on the yields from its broad acres. Now all
is hustle again, because one of Bren’s kiddies picked
up a bit of gold-bearing quartz in the back yard.
Bren sank a couple of feet, got on to a vein of
auartz, and resumed his mining career, with a yield
of 1230 z. from two loads of dirt. The State school soon
emptied, every toddler having turned out on the line
of lode, to crack up bits of quartz or gouge for in-
dicators. Bren’s find is next door to the old South
Devon, which was worked down in floors for good
returns till water beat out the parties. The shoots
are too short to pay cos. ; but when got they make
the hatter happy. # *
The usual piffling little row has closed
down Mount Lyell mine and works A party of men
had a contract, and the local A.M.A. thought these
contractors were working more than eight hours a
day. A union official said he would find out the
strength of it, so after coming off shift, he went
back into the mine—which is against the rules
and interviewed the party. . What sort of am
view it was can best be judged by the fact that
there was a fight next day. This fight got the busp
ness much talked about;, and being , m uch talked
about, Sticht seems to have been driven to take
notice of it, and the union official was sacked for
breaking the rule prohibiting trespass. That Was
the whole cause of the trouble. The A.M.A. de-
manded its man’s reinstatement; Sticht said he
would sack himself before he would do that. Within
24 hours the original small row was hidden m a
cloud of allegations and claims and denials and
whirling language; and the Government of tne Speck
was groping in the whirlwind to try to find a jug of
water to pour on the trouble. #
John McWhae, M.L.C., has gone to show
his bald head to the little brown brother who is
being civilised in the Philippines by Brother Jona-
than. McWhae created the Philippine Dredging Go.,
a proprietary concern. All that comes out about it
is that it has a capital of £150,000, of Which the
odd number of 44,600 shares are in reserve, 1 here is
also a £30,000 issue of debentures. What is the
substance of it all, only those on the inside know.
Being a proprietary concern it has no call to pitcn
out balance-sheets or tell anything more than it
cares to. This has its drawbacks —you can t get a
free market for scrip in such a co. on any Stock Ex-
change in Australia. Still, if knobs of gold are
picked up in the Paracale River, John McWhae isnt
the man to keep the good news to himself —or the
good thing itself, for that matter. Before quitting
Collins-street he had a great send-off; and when he
gets to his tropic island he won’t want a better intro,
than his smile, though he is sure to have a parchment
of two in his bag to tell all whom it may concern
that this is our trusty and well-beloved Councillor
John, etc., etc.
* * *
The main trouble with the Collie (W.A.)
coal hitherto won has been that it has been about
4,000,000 years too young. However, there is now a
likelihood of harder, older fuel being found by
deeper sinking, and Expectation has got up on its
stilts. At the West Collie Colliery, the other day, a
second seam, 10ft. thick, of improved quality, was
unearthed in sandstone under the mam workings.
Furthermore, some recent discoveries of coal down
Donnybrook way, some 30 miles or so south-west of
the, Collie field, have called for a revision of opinion
as to how far the fuel really extends. As the interven-
ing region is dense jarrah forest, through which it is
inconvenient to drag boring apparatus, this basin has
hitherto escaped much prospecting with the drill.
But the next few months should see the carbon cry-
stals biting deeper, and the real extent of the field
ascertained. „
* * *
Douglas Mawson, who specialises in
radium and Poles, has made a report on the Radium
Extraction Co.’s property at Mount Painter —the far
northern S. Aus. show that he had already enthused
about in the papers. “With a profit of £lOO per
ton, the 250 tons now in sight at No. 6 would return
£25,000,” says the explorer. No doubt; but the
profit of £lOO per ton isn’t demonstrated too clearly.
Two things are still in doubt; the cost of extraction
and the market value of the product, and both are
of the utmost importance. #
The Mystery Flat dredge commenced
operations on the river flat, a couple of miles below
Waikaia (M.L.). It is now working at the foot of
Moffett’s Gully—about 60ft. above its original posi-
tion, and looking down on the township. It depends
for its water supply on a siphon pipe carried across
an intervening gully. The performance of the master
and crew in thus raising their craft is probably
unique. Apparently there’s nothing to stop dredging
on any mountain range, provided water can be con-
veyed there.
* * * *
By all the Tory rules of the game, capital
should by this time be so scarce in Australia, and
especially in N. S. Wales, that it ought to be diffi-
cult to finance as much as a new hot-pea barrow.
Something, however, seems to have gone utterly
wrong, as usual, with the Tory rules, for the City
of Sydney proposes to tell J. Bull that there is so
much available local capital that it intends to raise
£360,000 in Australia, and pay off three old I O U s.
Most of the old loans were raised in 1887 for street
and town hall purposes, and £2lOO a year has been
paid in to a sinking fund. “The council has de-
cided to leave this sinking fund investment undis-
turbed [it now amounts to £BB,OOO, earning 4 p.c.]
and to continue the annual payments until the
maturity of this new issue, when the sum will be
more than the amount to be redeemed.” In other
words, there is so much money awaiting investment
in “Labor-cursed” Australia, that the City Council
is asking for the whole £360,000, instead of using
the £BB,OOO in hand to pay off part of the old
debt; and it is going to allow the £BB,OOO to go
on earning 4 p.c. in order to .assist in presently
wiping out the present loan, which it hopes to get
at 3i p.c. or less. It is all a horrible reflection on
the prophets who predicted financial disaster under
Labor Government. Also, by the way, the City
Council’s sinking fund, of about | p.c. p.a., is a
horrible reflection on the State Government s sink-
ing fund, of mostlv nothing at all p.a. *
The prospectus of the Australian Coking
and By-products Co. is being issued simultaneously
in Australia and England. The co. is in 200,000
shares; 14,500 go to the vendors m part-payment for
various contracts which have been secured, 100,000
are offered to the public, and 85,500 are reserved.
The 100,000 shares are offered at 2s. 6d. on applica-
tion, 2s. 6d. on allotment, and ss. one month after
allotment; the balance in calls as and when required.
Of the £50,000 represented by the first payments,
£5500 in cash goes to the promoters lor the con-
tracts before-mentioned; £16,000 goes to the Con-
tract Loan and Trust Corporation for undertaking
the payment of the preliminary exes, except broker-
age • and the whole 100,000 shares are underwritten
for 7* p.c. in cash. The eo.’s objects are “to carry
on the business of manufacturers of and dealers in
coke, coal tar, pitch and other by-products of coal,
and it is proposed to erect a modern coking and by-
product plant at Hexham, near Newcastle (N.S.W.).
Anything between 200,000 and 300,000 tons of coke
is produced annually in N. S. Wales, but nowhere in
Australia are the valuable by-products recovered.
Professor Galloway, a noted English expert, con-
cludes his report on the undertaking as follows:
“No better subject for the erection of a first-class
coking and by-product installation has ever come
before me. The demand for coke in Australia ap-
pears to be increasing, and, as your co. will produce
a high-class compressed coke, it is bound to occupy
a foremost place in the ranks of Australian coke-
makers I feel satisfied, therefore, that, with even
only moderately good management, the enterprise
will assuredly command success.” It is assumed that
the co. would handle 95,000 tons of washed coal
p.a. ; and Professor Galloway, on that basis, figures
this way.— REC EIPTS.

The contracts include one with the A. A. Go. for the
supply of 75,000 tons of small coal p.a. for 20 years,
another with the Wickham and Bullock Island Co.
for 15 000 tons p.a., and a third with the East
Greta for 10,000 tons p.a. “No coking coal known
to me in this country or on the Continent,” says
Professor Galloway, “gives so large a percentage of
volatile by-products, and at the same time produces
so much good, hard, silvery coke.” The chief by-
product is ammoniacal liquor, Which can be sold in
Australia at 4d. per lOOoz. of 80oz. strength. This,
it is calculated, means 2s. lid. per ton of coal coked.
It is mentioned that the Ammonia Co. of Australia
has offered to contract for the whole output of am-
moniacal liquor at the 4d. per lOOoz. mentioned
above. “Your co.,” says the British expert,
“should be able to rely upon an annual trading
profit of £28,038, which would, after making
all necessary allowances for administration, deprecia-
tion, etc., be sufficient to pay dividends of more than
15 p.c. p.a. upon a share capital of £120,000.’ _ The
Australian brokers are Jones and Buzacott, of the
Sydney Stock Exchange, and E. L. and C. Baillieu,
of Melbourne.*

Every engineer in charge of factories ana mills
should give close attention to the power wasted in
transmission of power. The Hyatt Flexible Roller
Bearings save 20 per cent, power and 75 per cent,
of oil, so one of the first duties is naturally for
engineers to direct their special attention to these
bearings and to car boxes for trucks. Hyatts are
in continuous use in Cement Works, Ore Dressing
Mills, Factories, etc., in Australia. Bushings o\
flexible rollers may be applied to loose pulleys or
truck wheels, etc. Should engineers require further
details, they can apply to the Australian Agents,
W. & J. Lempriere, Melbourne; or E. W. Hurst-
house and Co., of Wellington, the agents in Ne-v
Zealand.*

Co., London), and keep large stocks at all branches.
All fittings are branded + G.F. + , and are made in
4500 varieties and shapes, many of which are quite
impossible with ordinary iron or steel, and are no
higher in price. Malleable fittings may be flattened
on an anvil, but will not fracture, and are guaran-
teed to 3001b. steam pressure. Used by N.S.W. rail-

Water Boards, Sydney Gas Co., Fitzroy Mines,
Queensland, etc., etc. Write for particulars.*

The undisputed benefit of asbestos covering for
steam-pipes and boilers has been exemplified by a re-
cent scientific treatise of the principles of steam
conveyance. Tests made have established the fact
that 100 square feet of pipe covered with this
material, at a pressure of 2001b. per square inch, and
coal at 16s. per ton, will save £7 per year, and
prevent loss of pressure equal to 13.84 B. Th. U. per
square foot per minute. Bell’s Asbestos Non-con-
ducting Composition, supplied in cwt. bags, or
applied by experienced workmen at contract rates.
Bell’s Asbestos, Ltd., 315 Kent Street, Sydney.*
The most modern, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “Wilfley.” It is continuous, and
consists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged
in a tank of slimes or material to be filtered. The
drum is covered with a filter cloth supported on
grooves on the drum, which are connected by means
of holes to the interior to allow the liquid to be
withdrawn. Through the trunnions at the ends of
the drum the vacuum pipe passes to the interior.
This pipe connects with the interior of the drum,
through which the liquid and air are withdrawn by
means of a vacuum pump. The filtered slime is re-
moved by a simple apparatus placed at or below the
level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly the
cake is removed, the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is immediately re-immersed in the pulp
ready to receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the
sucking in of air, and consequent loss of vacuum
between the time the cake is removed _ and a fresh
one is forming. The residual liquid in the slime
cake is removed by distributing water over the sur-
face of the semi-dried cake. Owing to the fact that
the filter is circular in form, it will be seen that it
is continuous in operation, and has no complication
of valves to get out of order ; furthermore, work is
being done continuously during the whole revolution
of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manu-
facturing rights of this filter.* [2

ROBUR
To nurse sick people you
must love the work—you can’t
only just like it —you must really
love it. Imagine watching hour
after hour with no sound to break
the stillness of the night, save
the breathing of a poor sick child
—Get on your nerves? I should
say it does! Indeed, there are
times when I feel I simply can’t
keep at it any longer; but I do.
It’s work that just fascinates one;
and when your heart’s in the right
place you simply can’t give it up.
Of course, I sometimes feel out of
sorts, but then I just take a cup
of tea and a rest, and I’m alright
again. Nothing seems to pull me
together like a cup of real good
tea, but it must be hot, very hot,
and "Robur” is the tea I like
best; it’s flavour is pleasing, it is
wonderfully refreshing, and being
absolutely pure tea, there’s no
fear of it doing anybody any
harm.
The Nurse.
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
~ Sydney, September 25, 1911.
My Dear Arini, —
The Pill and Porous Plaster Party has
been vigorously prayed for in all the
churches in this city, and filled with food,
at frequent intervals, in the gilded premises of
the Rich. In return, it has exuded learning
per medium of the daily press, and sprinkled
clinics and other medical meat over the
breakfast bacon of the average citizen. As
a consequence, that person now eyes with
fierce suspicion the family history and dis-
reputable antecedents of the pig, and meets
the morning milk with threatening language.
The Acting-Gov. Cullens gathered together
at a garden party, last week, about 800 doc-
tors and their feminine belongings—a serious
affair heavily ballasted with silk hats. The
medical aggregation did not take along its
best bedside manner, and it showed a deter-
mination of grass to its feet, for it deve-
loped a habit of getting rooted to small
patches of green velvet lawn while its ladies
preened their plumage amongst the flower
beds, lhe sawbones in the front row of
bone-sawing is a busy person, who resents
the attempts of his Missus to drag him into
dress parades, and the average Male Person
at the garden spree looked cheerful enough to
have been prescribing for his own last
moments.
The G.-G. Denmans, with their Giant
Warrior Staff—disguised in the silk hat of
peace and plenty and other suitable attire—
materialised at the Cranbrook party late in
the afternoon. ller tall Ex.—adorned that
day in a soft shimmer of ivory satin under a
film of blue and rainbow—overtops her lord,
and shows the same brisk attention to busi-
ness that marks the G.-G. Denman manner.
The usual half-dozen persons were trotted
up to the Presence, or carefully betook them-
sejyes to the edge of the Sacred Circle, and
lUOO taxpayers (or thereabouts) did picket
duty in turn, so as to get a first-hand im-
pression of the New Importations.
The two handsome Delilahs, Eleanora de
Cisneros and Marie Ranzenberg, were the
largest ladies present at the garden fete.
Eleanora-Delilah, a beautiful cream cloth
tower of female charm, waved the other half
of an ostrich farm on her head—the half left
over when a Seaforth Highlander has fixed
up his fighting millinery. Austrian Marie
Ranzenberg has imbibed the English lan-
guage with much success, and spreads it
over her remarks with a faint foreign flavor.
Two Delilahs,” muttered a Man of Medi-
cine, gazing at the grand opera duet of
witchcraft. “This is no place for me,” and
he fled to the shelter of the tea tent.
Mrs. Scot Skirving, one of the belles
of the occasion, was up to the last
dying moments of fashion in wonderful
draperies of cream satin, and her lovely
mane of golden hair was coiled in ropes
round her head. Mrs. P. J. Collins, another
handsome dame at the Cranbrook party, had
escaped, like Eleanora, with a handful of
what tail the ostrich had left, and tossed
long feathers of black and gold on her big
hat. Mrs. G. Lane Mullins achieved a
powder blue robe that exploded in Oriental
trimming.
Mrs. Carty Salmon, who flitted over with
her husband from the Cabbage Patch, was a
radiant young matron in black and white.
Miss Norrie matched her eyes with a gown
of periwinkle blue. Mrs. A. J. Brady wore
one of the events of tfie afternoon, a miracle
in blue and green floral silk which changed
its colors like a chameleon.
Dr. Helen Sexton, who is over from the
Cabbage Patch for the Porous Plaster Party
and Amputating Conference, dived into medi-
cine, with distinction, in the earlier days of
prescriptions in peticoats. She turned a’seri-
ous profile to the cMffons of last week, and
looked as much at home at a garden party as
a deep-sea fish in a tub of blue water.
Dr. Mary Booth, who formerly inspected
the eyes of State school children in these
parts, and now inquires similarly in Victoria,
scampered over to take a share in the Senna
Argument.
The lady who handles knives and forceps
wagged her tongue to practical purpose
where the medicoes were gathered together,
and one of Queensland’s women doctors,
Eleanor Bourne, got away last Thursday
with some plain tales of legs and arms. Dr.
Bourne put her learned finger on the same
spot that gymnast Alcide Capitaine assaulted
}“ this letter a few weeks ago—the languid,
ladylike movements which, in many girls’
schools, are branded “physical culture.”
Let boys and girls exercise together,” was
the Queensland medicine lady’s challenge to
the type of school marm who considers legs
indelicate on anything but a grand piano.
Female draperv this spring is working its
way back to Quaker colors —dove-grey, and
faint lavender. Two all greys, in front of
competition at one of the Epsom Salts assem-
blages, were owned by Mrs. Walter Clark
and Mrs. Kenna.
Mrs. An till Pockley, Mrs. Steer Bowker,
and Mrs. Armstrong, executive organiser of
the ladies’ entertainment committee, are re-
to be . snatching five minutes’ sleep
after the Medical Congress flutter, which re-
duced even meek men to strong language and
oyster suppers. The post-office clock col-
lapsed under the strain. It broke down at
r U past 8, and had a surgical operation on
its inside before it gave another tick. Invi-
tation cards for some Pill parties also fell
down exhausted a day or two before the last
disease germ had been dissected in the Medi-
cal School, and had to be supported by the
telephone and other desperate remedies.
The Town Hall, which was laid out in
palms and bunting, and other dressy drapery,
for the British Medical Association’s even-
ing party, broke out in a few acres of scar-
let poppies, two nights later, at the revel
given by Dr. Antill Pockley. Invitations
lor this ball -"ere heavily underlined: “This
card kusT be presented.” Arriving late
from the splendor of “Carmen,” and safely
unloading my pasteboard on the Town Hall
doorstep, I met the ordinary tragedy of life,
the person who has lost the only thing that
at one brutal crisis matters two brass but-
tons. Sometimes it’s a train, or a tram
ticket, or a girl, or a latchkey. This time
it .was the card that had to be laid on the
official palm like a fly in a desert. The
ensuing spectacle—a distressful person in
swallowtails, searching every crevice of his
attire from his cuffs to his boots, while his
lady looked three volumes of home-chat —
suggested the general uncertainty of mun-
dane matters.
President Pockley’s ball was the best-
looking affair of the Medical Shriek. Pop-
pies crawled over every available selection
in the Town Hall, with a flicker of starry
clematis amongst the red riot. The decora-
tive scarlet blooms made a flame of color
that sprawled across the atmosphere till it
reached the pale pink petticoats that draped
the white electric arcs in the City Fathers’
roof. When the great procession trailed out
of the ballroom, two by two, its head was
somewhere amongst the daffodils in the base-
ment, while its tail was whisking out of the
ballroom.
