The Gov.-Gen., with Mrs. Pockley, was
ahead of her Ex. That lady was garbed
in brocade, in a faint tone like the pink
skin of a sun-kissed apricot, and wore her
fair hair in a nest of curls under a
blaze of diamonds. This splendid ap-
pearance was taken down to the flower-
trimmed supper by President Pockley.
Lady Barttelot, a fairy beauty in blue;
the acting-Gov. Cullens; Gentlemen Helps;
the Naval Rollestons; Medical Congress
upper crusts; and a municipal fringe, made
a Noah’s Ark procession—two by two, after
their official kind—which was one of the
pageants of the great Bolus Gathering. Dr.
Sinclair Finlay’s large and handsome lady,
in heliotrope and diamonds, had her decora-
tive ninon top-dressed in one of the new
devices —narrow bands of silk, laid out in
tiny rosettes.
A good Australian man sends me some
Yankee fashion notes from N’York or there-
abouts :
There are no ’orny-’anded sons of toil in the
United States. They all wear gloves, even the
Dagos. (Every European with a touch of color
in him is a Dago ; and the Dago population is in-
creasing at the rate of about a million a year, thus
swelling the clay feet of the Yankee Colossus.) The
smallest shop in the smallest town will have a
hundred varieties of gloves for the working man,
from five cents a pair up to a dollar and a-half.
Can you imagine “Billy” Hughes’s team of wharfies
fitted up in gauntleted gloves, adorned with yellow
rosettes or scarlet fringe? Here it would be ab-
normal to see them without these things. But each
man is entitled to wear what he pleases. There
seems no self-consciousness about anyone. In a
crowded city you may see a girl riding her horse
astride, dressed in a velvet costume, a revolver on
her hip, and no hat on her head—or if she has one
it is of the Boy Scout variety. On the pavements
are dozens of women without hats; young, middle-
aged. and old. With you on the tram car is a
young fellow in velvet corduroy trousers, coat, passion-
ate socks, dirty boots, hair as long as Paderewski’s,
and a hat of any shape, fantastic or otherwise—
barring the “plug.” Of the latter, I have seen’ only
one specimen on a citizen. (I except the cabmen of
San Francisco, who wear them. But they are not
citizens; they are bandits, licensed by the law to
rob any passenger they get into their unholy hands.)
The next specimen of man has pants 18in. wide at
the leg-top, turned up 4in. at the narrow bottom,
the turned-up part stitched so that it won’t fall
down. Whatever else he may have on, his hair is
usually long enough to make a prize merino proud,
and his boots are dirty. Hotels don’t clean your
boots unless you specially order it to be done, and
then it costs 10 cents a time. Servants here won’t
clean boots, so the morning tram is full of dirty
boots and unshaven beards. The cities are full of “boot
shine parlors,” and mere man, if he finds time dur-
ing the day, gets his boots cleaned in the city. As
is well known, every good American man never has
much time, hence the men of America are to be
noted for dirty boots and two-day beards. I am
talking of the great mass, not of the 5 or 6 per cent,
who keep themselves tidy. The women are much
smarter in appearance, but given to wearing wild
and woolly hats and very short skirts. As they have
well-shaped boots, and are mostly built like Charles
Lever’s Mullingar heifer, the tout ensemble isn’t what
a man would seriously object to.
The Shakspearian ball in aid of St. Vin-
cent’s Hospital, one of the gilt-edged events
of Race Week, occurs in the Town Hall on
October 3, and will be heavily spangled with
stars, garters, coronets and other baubles.
The G.-G. Denmans are going to bestow
their presence on the Cause, and the Scarlet
Giants of the G.-G.’s staff will also be on
view. Most of the ladies and gentlemen

nor) is pushing “Much Ado About Nothing”
into place.
The Army Medical Corps rally in Vic-
toria Barracks, on Friday afternoon, was
gently rained on without serious fatalities,
though much chiffon had to be caged up in
the drill hall, the Garrison Hospital grounds
(the place in which the party had intended
to be garden) being too sloppy for outdoor
exercise. The Denmans explored the gun-
ners’ sick room hostel, and cheerfully swal-
lowed tea in an atmosphere of splints, band-
ages, etc.; catching their own private ambu-
lance, later on, without any cotton wool
round their feet.
Miss Emily Meston is going to show her
brush work —chiefly portrait studies —on Oc-
tober 3to 5. Various hostesses will take a
hand in diluting Art with hot tea, and the
kettle is going to boil over at the sign of
Pathg Frhres, 51-53 Elizabeth-street, each
day, at 4 p.m.
Artist Rupert Bunny’s lovelv ladies are
hanging in their frames, on the walls in
Lawson and Little’s showrooms. These
beauties in oils reveal the feminine creature
in most of her moods, but Bunny buries the
feline claw, and he never allows great grief
to scratch crowsfeet on the prettv faces of
his subjects. Bunny’s favorite model is his
wife, whom he believes to be the most ador-
able person he has ever met.
Mrs. Bunny, who is French, lives on can-
vas as the lovely lady in the foreground of
the picture, “A Summer Morning,” bought on
the first day of the show for the Sydney
National Gallery. The trustees stamped up
to “A Summer Morning,” looked at the girls,
the kittens, the green shutter, the size,
weight and general quality of the merchan-
dise, and did a deal on the spot, exchanging
two smaller Bunnys recently acquired in
Melbourne for the green and white glory of
“A Summer Morning.”
Mrs. Theodore Watson, on a holiday trip
from Melbourne, and Mrs. Whiting and her
artistic daughter wrung the Bunny hand
when the trustees said, “Done,” or some-
thing of the kind. R. A. Society artists lined
up to “shake,” and the Bunny manner, which
is one of the youngest things still left in a
rather blase world, effervesced.
“No, really I’m not a Frenchman —what?
Heard I was naturalised? No! I live
chiefly in Paris; our home is there, but I
preferred to remain a British subject, though
the French Government could have given
me work had I become naturalised, which,
otherwise, only a French artist can do.
Bunny is a trying name—at least I thought
so when I was a schoolboy in Victoria, and
afterwards I almost thought of changing it.
However, I decided to remain Bunny to the
end. When I left Australia I went to Paris
with my father (my mother seemed to think
I had some artistic ability), and studied
under Jean Paul Laurens. Longstaff was in
Paris about that time. By the way, I went
up to the R. A. Society’s picture show, the
other day, and was much struck with Long-
staff’s work there —the Portrait of a Lady
(Mrs. R. Broomfield). It’s so sparkling,
and the treatment of the drapery is so clever
that it comes very close to Sargent.”
Artist Bunny has been 26 years away from
his native patch, feels about 16, doesn’t in-
tend to grow any older, is enchanted with
Sydney (seen for the first time last week),
and is the proud possessor of a wife who is
“a magnificent art critic and a good cook.”
The modern male novelist, like the late
Emil Reich, puts Woman under a micro-
scope, and cheerfully sticks pins into her as
an entirely new specimen of the tribe. Thus
Paul Neumann in “The Lone Heights” : “An
unmarried girl is always in fear of Mrs.
Grundy, and, when she marries, becomes
Mrs. Grundy.” Or, “A girl cannot be too
pretty, and she can’t have too good a figure,
but she can be too clever.” W. J. Locke gets
his muscle up in “The Glory of Clementina
Wing” by inserting a hatpin. “It’s all very
well to give a man a vote; he is a trans-
parent animal, and you know the way the
dunderhead is going to use it; but the incal-
culable and pyrotechnic way in which woman
will use it will make humanity blink.”
The Ben Davies-Esta d’Argo concert sea-
son opened fire in the Town Hall the other
night, behind a barricade of blossoms. This
was the vegetation handed up to the re-
turned wanderer —the Hetty Holroyd of 10
years ago—who has battalions of friends in
this city. The lady brings back the same
smile, the same powerful and flexible voice—
beautiful in the quality of its middle regis-
ter —and the same thin patch wearing
through the top notes, which float down
the years as a concert memory of the Hetty
Holroyd of long ago. Esta has developed
into a ballad singer, of considerable charm,
and her warble in Haydn’s lilt, “My Mother
Bids Me Bind My Hair” was a delightful
performance.
Ben Davies, the star tenor of several
thousand candle-power light, is large and
beefy in appearance, and owns a vast voice
that can only be approached with bended nib
as “an organ.” He puts in the soft stops
with sudden and amazing dexterity, is as
agile as a cat in climbing up and down the
voice ladder, and gets a flute-like finish on
to his top-storey tones. These notes excite
all the biggest sticks in the audience to bang
the floor as an encouragement to Benjamin
to hurry up aloft again. Handel’s Recita-
tive, “Deeper and Deeper Still.” with the
exquisite air, “Waft Her, Angels,” was the
strong meat of the concert. When tenor
Davies does get upstairs on his velvet sky-
scraper tones, he closes his eyes, presumably
because he’s one of the persons who can’t
look over the edge of high places without
feeling giddy.
Edward Goll, the pleasing pianist of the
Davies-d’Argo tour, is rather like an en-
larged portrait of Kubelik, with an extra
of°his collar aCk W ° ol falling over the back
Eleanora de Cisneros, as Carmen, is an-
other excuse for the alleged Sorrows of
oatan, the gentleman with the pitchfork
being unable to settle down in sulphur in
company with, such beautiful witchcraft.
JJon Jose-Zeni is bowled over by Gipsy Car-
men (with prolonged applause from every
part of the theatre) early in the opera, and
plunges into the simple life with a gun and
appropriate drapery, amid a magnificent
sta.ge setting of brigands and mountains
with Angelinas fiddlers to keep things mov-
ing. Eleanora-Carmen reveals the true in-
wardness of the Spanish silk fringed shawl
with a garden of silk roses blooming on its
centre, in the glorious drape she wears over
a subtle suggestion of underskirts that flash
the sheen of dull rose. This snare of Satan
puts Don Jose’s last struggle in the dust-
heap of good resolutions.
Melba was in the managerial box on the
first night of “Carmen,” not looking well
after her recent ear anguish.
Journalist John Arthur Barry, of the
Evening News editorial columns, passed
over the horizon of this planet, the other
day, a friend and comrade not easily spared
by those who knew him best. Mariner Barry
put the swish of green seas, the hiss of the
hurricane and the flapping sails of a be-
calmed wind-jammer into his sea stories,
with the ready pen of the man who’d
handled these wild beasts at close quarters.
The Apple Orchard’s rill, that rushes down
from the snow hat of the hills, has now lost
its reputation, and the summer tourist, who
laps up portion of a waterspray to slake his
thirst —the nearest tavern being out of
coo-ee —will continue this form of drink at
his own risk. Tasmania’s Chief Inspector of
Health, Dr. J. S. Purdy, threw a brick at
wandering rills, at the Medical Corroboree,
and accused them of harboring goitre germs
in their limpid shallows. So Tasmanian
trippers will either boil their waterfall, or
swallow their whisky direct from the flask.
Yours, heretofore,
VANDORIAN.

A merry group of chorus girls from one of the city theatres
is enjoying a dip in the briny on a Sunday morning regardless
of the fact that near by stands, scowling, an Anglican par-
son who looks upon the performance as sinful to a degree—
but keeps looking, nevertheless. For the ladies have been
amply endowed by Nature, and are free of their charms; and,
whilst keeping an eye on them, the poor parson literally
“Falls in,” and is rescued by the belle of the bunch. After-
wards she takes him in hand, and makes a man of him,
and shows him a bit of life. How she does it is well worth
finding out, and the inquiring reader will be thoroughly satis-
fied.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, September 25, 1911.
My Dear Yarrie,—
The son of Viscount Gormanston, once
Governor of Tasmania, is, all going well, to
marry a daughter of Lady Butler —the Lady
Butler who painted “The Roll Call.” Jenico’s
family has been in the viscount business
ever since 1475, and has hung on to its
11,000 acres of Ireland for most of the time.
Melbourne Society is rather annoyed at
the G.-G.’s programme of frivols. Accord-
ing to the list, only one hop is to be given
in Melbourne, while Sydney is allotted two.
Some dames are consoling themselves and
friends by reminding them that the ballroom
in the palace by the Yarra holds twice as
many people as the one in the mock castle
by the Harbor, and that the two Sydney
dances merely represent an A to L and L to
/.solution of the crush difficulty.
The numerous Fuller household is still
cooped up in the Malvern home, no more
elbow room having been acquired. It was said,
soon after the beginning of the reign, that
an extra house was to be rented for the
children and aides. Then his Ex. was re-
ported to have said something about canvas
houses in the garden. It sounded idyllic;
but so far nobody seems to have rushed the
fresh air, for no shelters are visible to the
naked eye. Fuller once remarked that he
and his lady had every intention of “seeing
through the whole five years in Australia.”
The brave sentiment was fired off with the
air of a hardy sport, who was roughing it in
the jungle and didn’t give a hang for per-
sonal discomforts and privations.
Mrs. Edward Miller, who has taken Lady
Carmichael’s place as president of the Guild
of Irish Crochet Makers, is also acting as
secretary in the absence of Mrs. Austin.
The departed Governoress, who was herself
an expert with the needle, fanned the en-
thusiasm of promising lacemakers with en-
couraging letters, and was always ready to
swap stitches with anyone. She invited
countrywomen to send along examples of
their work, and opened a market for the
filmy merchandise so attractive to all fe-
males. The Guild has some beautiful ex-
amples of Irish crochet, which will be lent
to good workers on application. Skilled ad-
vice and help is also on tap for those who
are striving after improvement. The organ-
isation is rapidly extending, and the com-
mittee is revising the rules and letting out
the official tucks, so that it will have room
to grow. The lace industry, intelligently
controlled, will enable many women to add
materially to their incomes. In the long
winter evenings much time and energy is
squandered on pretty but valueless fancy
work. The same amount of time and energy
devoted to lace-making would return a nice
profit. A show and sale of Irish crochet (no
other brand need apply) is projected for
next Easter. The exact date will be fixed
later on; but it is not too early to publish
the preliminary announcement, if a satisfac-
torv number of recruits is to be raked up.
The address of the president and acting sec-
retary is Glynn, Kooyong-road, Toorak. The
show, it may be explained, will consist prin-
cipally of loaned examples of Irish crochet;
the sale is purely for the work of Guild
members.
Florrie Finn, daughter of the Melbourne
Punch family, who has been wandering
abroad for the last 18 months, is now headed
for Australia in the Orsova. Besides doing
the frosty Coronation, she has been in a
sandstorm in a desert, and a snowstorm at
Interlaken ; also, she has dipped in the Dead
Sea, strolled round the back ways of Pales-
tine, and struggled to the top of the highest
Pyramid, with the help of only two pale
yellow asistants. (Most people, lam given
to understand, require at least four. Even
Jane, the heroine of “The Rosary,” had a
quartette to tow her up.) The energetic
girl walked many miles through Bull’s dis-
tressful land, and inspected many moated
granges. She intends to pass her experi-
ences on to the public in a book which will
be illustrated by herself.
With the Federal House at work again,
Melbourne streets are gladdened by the old,
well-loved cartoon models. The moustache
of Spruce Myth is observed to be curled
jauntily, and his buttoned boots have a
dazzling shine. The wearer >'* chem com-
plains that he finds cork soles are a necessity
in changeable Melbourne; whereas, on his
weekly return to sunnier Sydney, he is able
to cast them cheerfully aside along with his
macintosh, umbrella and goloshes. It is
almost impossible to look really Spruce when
in wet-weather armor, though one may look
Myth any time.
One of the members of the recent Medical
Congress turned his trip into a honeymoon.
Dr. Harry Stevens married the daughter of
Dr. Coles, of Carlton, early this month, and
fled to Sydney well in advance of his purely
business-iike brethren.
Miss Amy Young is to be married, on
October 12, to Dr. Percy Gore Brett, a
talented and moneyed young man. The cere-
mony happens in Cairns Memorial Church,
and the subsequent refreshments will be
served at Menzies’. The bride-elect is the
last of Tom Young’s bevy of pretty daughters
to dive into matrimony, and the Wimmera
clan is coming down in force from Sunny-
side to assist in the splash.
Rupert Clarke, one hears, is not for long
in Australia this trip. Having exchanged
his active interest in the _ Clarke-Meynell
firm for a passive partnership in the Com-
bine, the restless baronet is not troubling
much about stage matters on this side. The
speculation was good sport while it lasted,
and it brought him a profit 0f.£60,000. But
the Combine has no speculative charms to
speak of. It is just a safe investment. Some
time in February, or March, perhaps, Rupert
will return to the crowded latitudes and
make his headquarters in London perhaps.
Mrs. Charles D’Ebro’s daughter is giving
a smart dance this week; not at the parental
Praedo, but at a Chapel-street hall.
Lord Dick Nevill arrived at the beginning
of the week, and started to have a general
look round the Melbourne establishment, be-
fore going on to Sydney to join the G.-G.
The Willie Felsteads’ fine young Austra-
lian daughter is returning by the Orsova,
after a four years’ “finishing course on the
Continent, presumably with the usual load ot
languages. From a scholastic point of view,
a great disadvantage of this continent is that
we all speak the same tongue, and can under-
stand one another perfectly without gesticu-
lating.
A fine old exclusive house, which stands
in its own garden, well back from Flinders
and Russell streets, is placarded for sale.
It was for years Dr. Rowan’s private hospi-
tal ; after that it became a paying guestery ;
now it is plain boarding-house. Soon it
may be a hash tower. The high,, terraced
wall hides a wilderness garden full of splendid
old trees —a quiet, green, bird-haunted spot
almost in the heart of the city. The boarders
in the old house will probably see the valu-
able garden sold before their windows, even
if the house itself is not auctioned over their
heads, and the birds will have their freehold
allotments in the trees confiscated by human
builders in brick and stone.
The Most Respectable coteries seem to
make it a rule to “take up” Shakspearian
mimes, and look carefully over the heads of
other deserving mummers whose playwrights
are not so dead as Bill of Avon. After the
University had beamed through its spectacles
at the Trvings, the sleek tabbies of the Alex-
andra Club extended a velvet paw, and in-
vited the visiting mimes to dine in the beau-
tifully-papered feeding room, with Mrs.
Emmerton and Mrs. James Murphy as
hostesses. Mrs. Ophelia Irving was made
an honorary member, and given the privi-
lege of lapping m'lk—mixed with tea and
sugar—whenever she had the time to spare.
Sassiety is confidently prophesying a double
engagement in an elevated house. The
wealthy sister of an engaged girl and the
brother of the fianed are being observed with
interest by kindly gossips.
Melbourne’s toniest association of She-
kind was divided into sections recently by
the autocattic action of its tricky rulers.
There are now two parties—the Ins and the
Outs —and if the leader of the Opposition
were to move a vote of want oi confidence
in the Government the voting would be close.
The trouble was born of exclusive snob-
bery, of course. Members of the club have,
the right to engage the big; room —one day a
week—for private entertainment, and these
hiring fees help considerably to pay the
exes, of the swaggah establishment. The
usual notice of an ordinary booking for a
particular date attracted no particular atten-
tion at the time, but subsequently the fact
leaked out, or gushed out, that the commit-
tee had wanted the big room for a reception
to the G.-G. lady, and that only some of
the members had been invited to partake of
the grovel. Whereupon the ignored ones re-
membered that the rules of the club require
every member to get notice when a Person-
age is officially invited. Indignation runs
not high, but deep. “A club, indeed !” says
Mrs. Somebody; “/ call it a snub.”
The Menzies’ Ball, held on Saturday, will
roll a fat bundle of good Commonwealth
notes into the Children’s Hospital treasury
chest. The Chief Justice’s lady acted as
impromptu hostess; for Norman Menzies
had gone down suddenly with influenza, and
his devoted Dorothy Vane wife gave herself
up to nursing him. The Menzies are in
hard luck, with Mrs. Dowager Menzies also
on the sick list. The children danced per-
fectly, offering a contrast to any grown-up
ball, where the dancers are few and the
sprawlers are many. Mrs. Green’s star
pupils gave some special capers. A “Cobra”
girl got the poster prize, and a boy Indian
lassoed another trophy. Esther Barton had
her fair hair plastered back into a periwig,
and looked a dandy Cavalier. The Grand
Hotel Holdens sent along a spick and span
representative; size about and labelled
“Bond-street.” She infatuated a Monsieur
Beaucaire in powdered wig and brocade.
The children were serious people, mostly.
They booked their dances with as much care
and coquetry as any child of 30 or there-
about. Almost the whole of the hotel was
thrown open, and everything was well done.
The living rooms—including even the big
dining-room—were cleared and decorated in
soft-colored flowers and lights.
The Fullers, of State Government House,
would have sent the whole nursery contin-
gent of six to the Menzies’ juvenile fancy
ball, if the frivol had been held in the after-
noon. Unfortunately, the start had been
fixed for 8, and, as the sub-Excellencies have
“Early to Bed”' tacked on the nursery wall,
they bad to regretfully decline. As soon as
the news got round, several mammas in high
life hurriedly cancelled the engagements of
their offspring, on the plea of night air. The
enraged offspring sprang about and howled
dismally, saying, “But you know we went to
Mrs. Blank’s, and to Dodo Money wool’s at
night time!” It was useless. The de-
frauded infants were up against vice-regal
precedent, and Ma blankly refused in every
case to take a reasonable view of things.
Some people are capable of imitating any-
thing.
The Geelong Grammar School has decided
to import a parson headmaster from Oxford,
to fill one of the fattest billets on the educa-
tional market. This painful process of drag-
ging brains from the other side of the world
is liable to give Foglanders the impression
that Miss Australia is a wild, tousled female
with milk cans in each hand, and an un-
rivalled store of natural ignorance. Quite
a lot of people on the other side would listen
with sympathetic horror, if you informed
them that the good G,-G. spent his spare
evenings teaching the hardy pioneers in Col-
lins-street to read and write. The periodical
cry for a University professor or a plain
schoolmaster only goes up to London from
the wild outposts of civilisation — and Aus-
tralia ; so Australia is classed as one of the
wild outposts, and the brave soul who an-
swers the call is wept over at the docks, and
told not to let the kangaroos eat his hair in
the drought. Amongst the 32 “cast” candi-
dates were well-equipped local men of experi-
ence ; but they were all counted out. In
some walks of life it appears to be a consid-
erable disadvantage to be an Australian.
Rita, the eldest hope of the Reggie
Weigalls, gave a smart evening recently at
the ancestral mansion in Elsternwick. Sister
Dora, a blue-eyed brunette with a strong-
sense of humor, made her debut quietly at
the private function. It must have been
the sense of humor that prevented her from
taking the social plunge at a mayoral hop
in company with the usual flock of white-
robed strangers whose photographs are
printed the following week in rows by the
weeklies. During the intervals in the pro-
fessional music, Beryl Fanning lifted up
that flute-like voice which Melba says con-
tains a future. All the same, the Fanning
chick is not likely to leave the nest in order
to go futuring on the stage.
Amy Castles’s sudden throat attack seems
to have suddenly lost its serious aspect.
There was talk of diphtheria, and the date of
Amy’s first farewell concert was postponed
indefinitely. Then the 11th of October was
dimly suggested. But by last Monday the
diphtheria theory had fallen through, and
October 7 was said to be decided on. The
Taits, wdio suffered from shock to their busi-
ness arrangements whilst the songstress was
diphtherialising, got the consolation of a cable
from brother Nevin last Saturday announc-
ing that the seats for Clara Butt’s concert
in Capetown were selling at a premium.
Fanny Dango Mackay, who lately acquired
a little son of her very own, also possesses a
stepson, and a 17-year-old stepdaughter who
is one of the belles of the Glassy Harem.
The Advertising Art Exhibition should be
in full bloom in the Town Hall by the time
The Bulletin has brought its latest issue
to bear on Melbourne’s understanding. Four
o’clock this (Thursday) afternoon is the
time fixed for unlocking the floodgates of
John Madden’s oratory— i.e., the new-born
show’ receives its baptism of John’s honeyed
words at the witching hour when tea-cups
yawn and slaves give up their bread. But
the public w’ill be admitted at three o’clock,
in accordance with the ancient custom of
disclosing an exhibition to view an hour be-
fore the official opener arrives, so that he
may have an audience awaiting him. For
these three days (ending on Saturday night)
everybody worth mentioning in the local
world will prate of posters and be interested
in all pictorial forms of advertising art. For
which relief the Lord make us truly thank-
ful ! There are so many other forms of ad-
vertising art that are not a bit picturesque,
A train which had been clattering and
roaring merrily across country towards the
Medical Congress, now dead and gone, came
to a stop at nowhere in particular with a
heavy bump and a screaming of brakes. The
horde of doctors on board stuck their heads
out of the windows, and were accosted
by a peevish engineer who inquired in
a hopeless tone, “Isn’t there any man here
with an engineer’s certificate? I don't want
a doctor.” Unhappily, there was not
amongst the passengers a person qualified
and able to render the badly-needed first aid
to the engine. So the engineer did his best
single-handed, and after a wild-flower pick-
ing interval by the passengers, “All aboard”
was shouted, and the steam caravan jolted
sulkily on its journey, arriving some hours
late. That engineer would have cheerfully
exchanged a dozen or two dozen first-class
physicians for a man who was competent to
put a blister on a boiler or amputate a
funnel.
Tasmania is sticking hatpins into its
Tourist Bureau, and urging it to wake up
and tell the holiday-maker what a restful
paradise the place is. The Tasmanian no-
bility and gentry have always seen to it that
the streams running through their pro-
perties were well stocked with trout; and as
fish don’t take any heed of fences, and as
tributaries are numerous, the unlanded
angler has plenty of inducement to bait a
hook. Moreover, the streams are mostly
limpid and shallow, and are edged with shade
trees, so that he can fish comfortably and
his wife avoid catching even one freckle.. A
circular trip covering 150 miles is being
arranged that should land the tourist iff
large quantities.
A Bulletin par. is said to have been the
means of reuniting two loving souls. The
story runs this way: A young London medico
hankered secretly for a girl of his acquaint-
ance, but, considering that his brass plate
wasn’t bringing in enough to marry on, he
refrained from proposing. The girl, in the
meantime, was wooed and won by an Austra-
lian. She came out here to visit the fiance’s
relations, and the medicine man was driven
to desperation and hard work. Months went
by, and the engagement suffered a fracture,
which was duly recorded here. A cLpy of
the issue containing the news was. sent to
Fiji, the headquarters of the uncle industry,
and despatched thence to someone in Edin-
burgh. The Scotchman, having a stamp to
spare, sent the well-creased paper to the
man himself in London. He read of the dis-
engagement, threw his silk hat right oyer
the house, and wrote off at Once offering him-
self and his renovated income to the girl.
She sobbed on his shoulder by post, and
started to pack up. Latest reports mention
orange blossom and confetti. Now I am
waiting for a tiny slice of wedding cake.
An October bride, who is now engaged
planning her domestic) scheme, should not
have much trouble on the lower deck, if
dainty frocks and surroundings count for
anything with Marianne. The members of
the house staff in her establishment, are to
be supplied with rose-pink frocks, white caps
and frilly aprons, and should look like de-
lectable strawberry ices. Of course, if the
right sort of maid doesn’t transpire, she will
merely get a beetroot salad effect. The back-
ground of the color scheme is to be green
and white; the kitchen and surrounding
rooms are to be decorated that way, and gen-
erally made to look like the interior of a
spick and span yacht. A matron who had
her orange blossoming a few years ago
turned her kitchen into an artist’s dream
with Dutch blue and white tiles and Dutch
blue china. The servants had a sitting-room
furnished with easy chairs, covered with blue
chintz, and Missus, who is one of the beauty
matrons of Toorak, turned up her sleeves
every morning and helped in the general
tidying-up. Notwithstanding all this, she
only manages to get girls by paying their
cab fare home on three nights a week. Her
husband has just come to the Conclusion that
it will be cheaper in the long run to buy a
motor, and use the travelling gas engine him-
self when Marianne doesn’t require it.
From my Adelaide correspondent: —
On Tuesday afternoon Mrs. Bonython asked the
members of the local Alliance Frangaise to have tea
and admire her garden. The Bonythons inhabit a
big house top Ding Montefiore Hill. It (the house)
was originally built by that Dixon who is now Den-
nison, and who keeps racehorses, newspapers and
other bitter beasts.
The Port Races increase steadily in popularity,
and the crowd which journeyed to the Cheltenham
course last Saturday was such that the provided ac-
commodation was badly overtaxed. It’s not much of
a woman’s meeting, though some of the keener
spirits find (heir way there, and rejoice in it as a
sporting ■ event pure and simple—not a semi-garden
party. Calamus kept up the reputation of the total-
isator by giving his backers £25 11s. for £1 in the
Spring Handicap. In the Flying Handicap, “Dick”
Hawker’s Sacrfi came to grief, and owing to the non-
appearance of the S.P.C.A.’s horse ambulance, there
was some delay in getting the animal to town. It
wasn’t the fault of the S.P.C.A. folk. They’d offered
the Club the use of their ambulance, sbut had been
rebuffed.
v Which reminds me that there has been some fiery
discussion anent Sir Grenville’s smash at the last
Morphettville meeting. Seeing that the S.P.C.A.
makes horses its rather special care, and has pur-
chased an equine stretcher to make things easier
for them, people marvelled when Sir Grenville, with
a leg smashed, was led past the stand and on into
the remote distance before being put out of pain.
It; now transpires that the fault wasn’t the Society’s.
Their vehicle and their inspector were close by
the smash, ready for action, but the racing club’s
vet. took it on himself to lead the animal for a half-
mile stroll —directly away from the waiting ambu-
lance. One hears that something was said about the
impossibility of destroying the creature in front of
the stand, “because of the ladies.’’ Any woman with
a spark of decent feeling would rather see a horse
destroyed than paraded in obvious agony. So that
excuse is worn out.
Hunters had a busy day on Saturday—a before-
breakfast run (with a small field) at Urrbrae in
the morning, and jumping at the Show in the after-
noon. At the latter function Mr. O’Dea’s Top Note
secured first place in both the Open Hunters and the
High Jump, with Mr. F. S. Tom’s new purchase, The
Whip, second in the Hunters. Now that the bi-
yearly rural orgy is over, we’ve returned to some-
thing like normal conditions, and progress in the
streets can be made at the rate of more than a mile
an hour. It’s a fine thing (in some ways) to be
a prosperous State, but it’s distinctly inconvenient
when your cocky backbone elects to hold a mass
meeting in town.
“The Arcadians” have returned. Miss Gilliam and
Miss Linden are among them, so broken hearts should
be as plentiful as leaves on Vallambrosa, or dead
marines at a mayoral banquet.
Mrs. Halcomb (whose husband sees that the law
of precedence is duly observed in the Legislative
Council—he’s its clerk) has been rejoicing in a visit
from two English- sisters. To prove it she gave a
bridge and garden party in their honor.
Preparations for the church pageant continue—
more or less smoothly. Mrs. Albion Tolley is the
secretary. The authorities have abandoned their
original idea of the theatre in favor of the exhibi-
tion building, which takes some filling. The “book”
is the work of Miss ltebeuham, and is on the lines of
the old miracle plays, covering a period of from 550
to 680, so it should be picturesque.
The local Wattle Day League held its first annual
public meeting on Monday, and the report presented
shows healthy activity. Vice-President Sowden sub-
mitted a common-sense suggestion, to federalise the
affair and make it an Australian Wattle Day League,
instead of letting each State go its own way, or
stand still, as it chooses. The idea caught on, and
the N.S.W. and Victorian Leagues are to be ap-
proached.
Recently returned from England is Ronald Angas,
the C. H. Angas’s eldest son. He’s been spending
some years in English ’Varsity life, and is now
booked to take his share in the management of the
estate.
T’other night a parson expressed himself freely
on the subject of feminine head-gear, and stigma-
tised it as an “invention of the Devil to keep men
from seeing the preacher.” -He also remarked that
he had admonished his own wife to get a “decent
kind of hat.” The rev. will probably make things
worse by this ill-advised step. The jelly-bag con-
cerns, which are the alternatives to the Merry Widow
brand of lid, provide priceless shelters for slumber
during dull sermons.
On Sunday the South -African soldiers’ memorial
service was held at the parade ground, and the
statue at Government House gates was hung with
wreaths. There was a very fair muster of troops,
and both the Governor and Lady Bosanquet attended.
Died the other day at Walkerville, an old
identity in the person oi Mrs. Levi. The suburb has
been the home of the Levis for ages.
From the Royal College of Music, London, come
good progress reports concerning Miss Kleinsohmidt,
who was dubbed La Serena, made into a syndicate
and despatched Englandwards for the betterment of
her voice. The syndicate is happy, and says she’ll
pay good dividends. A big voice and an equally
big capacity for work should certainly take her far.
Premier Verran wants to pull down the old (50
years old) and ugly stone wall which surrounds the
vice-regal mansion on North Terrace. The Governor,
however, objects. So there will be nothing doing in
the eyesore-removing line for the present. But be-
fore our next figurehead transpires the deed will cer-
tainly be accomplished.
The pile of bricks and mortar, which is to
be the new University-Conservatorium, is
being gradually trowelled into shape. Melba
and the late Thomas Bent conjointly patted
the foundation-stone some considerable time
back. The edifice has still some ragged edges;
but encouraging signs of hod-carriers and
bricklayers were discernible amidst the fall-
ing moisture.

IN Miss Ethel Irving’s performance of the principal part of “Lady
Frederick” in London, the critics were unanimous that here was an actress
of extraordinary resource and character, and the clever play, whose success
had carried it from theatre to theatre during its lengthened run, could scarcely
have achieved such distinction had it not been for the perfectly lovable and
convincing picture drawn by this inimitable artiste.
The scene in which Lady Frederick’s lover is initiated into the mysteries
of complexion “veneering” is a delicious caricature of how the average English-
woman practises her daily beauty culture, and its presentation by Miss Irving
must ever remain a classic.
But in private life Miss Irving knows better. She would abhor the idea
of taking care of her skin in any but the most natural sensible manner. In
the letter here reproduced she attests the merits of Valaze preparation in the
most flattering terms. She pronounces them “perfectly wonderful for the
skin.”
And many of her sisters in the profession
share her opinion, as witness Miss Ellaline
Terriss. She says: “She finds Yalaze a most
valuable preparation, and will most certainly
recommend it.”
Miss Marie Studholme states: “That she
has found Yalaze a very agreeable and valuable
preparation, and can certainly recommend it.”
Miss Lily Brayton, acknowledging the un-
questioned superiority of the Yalaze prepara-
tions, says: “Every woman should use Yalaze
to preserve her complexion.”
Miss Alice Crawford, the beautiful Bendigo
girl, who has been winning increasing fame
with each theatrical season in London, says:
“Your Russian Skin Food, Yalaze, does pre-
cisely what the skin requires. It preserves its
tone, whiteness and clearness as no other pre-
paration has done.”
Thus are the Yalaze preparations spoken
of among the most competent judges.

the American method of producing a slim,
trim figure is meeting with astonishing suc-

wonderful impression there, is the Marmola
Prescription Tablet method of reducing fat.
Anything that will reduce the excess flesh a
pound a day, without injury to the stomach,
the causing of wrinkles, the help of exercis-
ing or dieting, or intei’ference with one’s
meals, is a mighty important and useful ad-
dition to civilisation’s necessities. Just euch
a catalogue of good results, however, follow’
the use of these pleasant, harmless and
economical little fat reducers. We say
economical, because Marmola Prescription
Tablets (made in accordance with the fa-
mous prescription), can be obtained of any
chemist for 3/6 (which is a decidedly
economical price, considering the number of
tablets each bottle contains), or sent post
free on receipt of price by the Marmola Co.,
7 Barrack Street, Sydney, N.S.W.

BAD FOR THE SKIN
♦
Rubbing on Greasy Ointments*
How to Properly Treat
Skin Troubles*
It is not a good thing for people with a
tendency to have pimples and a blotchy com-
plexion to smear themselves with greasy
ointments and such things. In fact, they
couldn’t do anything worse, because the
grease only clogs the pores of the skin,
making the complaint worse. When there is
an irritating rash a soothing boracic wash
may help to allay the pain or itching ; but,
of course, it doesn’t cure. Skin complaints
arise from an impure condition of the blood,
and will persist until the blood is purified.
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have cured many
cases of eczema and skin disorders because
they make rich, new blood that drives out
the impurities, and imparts a glow of health.
Mr. Robert Davies, of 43 Rathmines-street,
Northcote, Melbourne, tells how these pills
cured him of a terrible attack of Eczema.
“I had a bad attack of Eczema,” said Mr.
Davies. “My poor body was quite raw. I
could not bear anyone to come near me. I
was under the doctor for 12 months, and he
couldn’t cure me. My skin used to burn and
itch something frightful. I was on fire with
it. No one knows how I suffered, only my
wife and myself. I spent pounds and pounds
in medicine, and none of them did me much
good. The trouble used to appear on the
thighs, and on the calves, and most particu-
larly about the ankles. My clothes irritated
the parts dreadfully. It was impossible to
keep from scratching; my skin used to scale
and come off like scurf. I was suffering like
this for about a year when I read that Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills would cure my com-
plaint, and so I bought some and took them
as directed. After awhile the irritation be-
gan to decrease, the skin got less scurfy, and
more healthy looking. I felt better than
ever I did. To-day I am perfectly cured,
and my skin is as clear as it was when I
was born. I can eat and work; in fact, I
am a new man.”
Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills are 2s. 9d. a box,
6 boxes 15s. 3d., of all dealers, or from The
Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. of Australasia
Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINS
MR.
WADE,
of N.S.W.
Assembly—
his present
aspect.
An Echuca-ite writes :
Palmer, who misrepresents Echuca in the House of
Reps., has suddenly raised his voice against Yassberra.
Furthermore, he has just asked the Reps, to pass a
Federal Capital Referendum Bill, before any more
good Australian money is wasted on the Desert City.
Considering that Palmer was one of the anti-Austra-
lians who voted and worked against the Monaro site,
his new attitude occasions surprise. The explanation is
that one William Orr, who opposed Palmer for
Echuca at last election, would have beaten him in
a single-handed go, and intends to contest the seat
again. Orr is, and always has been, strong on a
Federal Capital Referendum, and Echuca is strongly
suspected, these days, of being the same. In fact,
Palmer is stealing Orr’s thunder as a measure of
self-protection.
Chirrup of joy from the S. M. Herald's
Bradford (Eng.) correspondent: —
Leeds does a big over-seas trade in ready-made
garments, and we are glad to note that to South
Africa especially larger quantities of light, but well-
made fabrics are being shipped, while Australia is
appreciating well-made flannels and light fancy
worsteds more than it ever did. In fact, we are in
a position to say that but for the growing trade in
textiles between Australasia, South Africa, Canada,
and the Argentine, not to mention the Far East, the
whole industry of this district would not have en-
joyed the prosperity that we have seen.
Which is a shriek of derision in tlie face of
Australia—the fool country which produces
most of the wo< 1 that, when mixed with
rags, is the basis of the “well-made flan-
nels,” etc., that Bradford is whooping about,
and hasn’t sense enough to keep its product
at home and make its own flannels.
AFTER THE STORM. “ OH, IVHAT A
TIME WE HAD!”
The sort of thing that the Wade push
wanted ex-Minister for Lands Nielsen to
do! Eight years ago the N.S.W. “Liberal”
Government sold the fee simple of some
State land at Cronulla (N.S.W.) for £l5 an
acre. Naturally, in accordance with “Lib-
eral” policy, the money was thrown into con-
solidated revenue and spent. Recently the
same land was sold at £3600 per acre.
“B.” : How these towns hate one another!
A Perth jury gave R. D. McKenzie £lOOO
libel damages against the Kalgoorlie Miner,
and now the Miner asks for a new trial at
Geraldton, the inference being that a Perth
jury can’t be trusted to give a Goldfields
paper a fair show. And the case of Mines
Minister Gregory against the same paper is
to be tried at Albany—presumably on the
same old inference.
Frank Henty: Last week I got this from
the Scripture Instruction Campaign Council
of Victoria: —
Dear Sir, —-We notice that you are announced as
a candidate for the general elections. We are for-
warding literature in respect to the Scripture Cam-
paign Council and its objects, from which .you will
see we are definitely organising in favor of a Refer-
endum Bill on this question, which provides for the
majority having the right to rule itself on this
matter of securing in Victoria what works so well
in four adjoining Australian States. We shall be
glad to be informed as to your views on this ques-
tion, to guide our friends throughout your electorate.
The widely-advertised educational policy of
the Labor party of Victoria is that educa-
tion—primary, secondary, technical and uni-
versity—shall be free and secular. Yet the
Scripture Instruction crowd does not scruple
to ask a pledged Labor candidate to sell his
honor for the support of the sectarians. And
it is on this rotten foundation that the advo-
cates of Scripture teaching in State schools
are attempting to build up the characters of
little Willie and Jane Australia!
News items within the past few days:—
The Adelaide S.S. Co. announces that three new
8000-ton vessels have been ordered in Great Britain.
The co. is expecting another new vessel in three
months.
The Howard Smith Co. has notified that it has
ordered a new steamer of 8000 tons. It is rumored
that the co. is about to order two or three additional
cargo steamers.
The Melb. S.S. Co. received by this week’s mail
the description of a vessel of 5000 tons register.
The U.S.S. Co. has purchased a large vessel in
European waters. Other steamers are building, or
in contemplation.
Capital is apparently making frantic pre-
parations to fulfil its threat to flee the
country, and is buying swift vessels to
leave in.
“8.J.H.0.” : Even the very mild compul-
sory military training business is proving a
blessing in several directions. Since a num-
ber of boys have had a taste of the real
thing they have wearied of the Boy Lout
foolishness. At Geelong, recently, the local
Skites disbanded, and it was announced
that the Barnum Powell show had been
killed by the advent of the real article.
N. S. Wales is finding out that, though
J. Bull Cohen loved us as his child, he bit
pieces out of us as though we were his
lamb. For every £lOO the State borrowed
from him between 1905 and 1910 his
charges ran from £3 4s. 3d. to £3 14s. lOd.;
and the State didn’t know what a ravenous
appetite that showed until last year it found
out that the entire cost of raising money in
Sydney was only 2s. lfd. per cent. Thus,
dear, kind Cohen of the J. Bull variety had
been charging his child as much to raise
£lOO as the child finds it can itself raise
£3600 for.
Tommy Bent's best-remembered public
utterance was dug up and artistically em-
ployed in the Tasmanian Legislature, the
other day, by the tremendous Joshua Thomas
Hoskins Whitsitt. He is reported to have
said that the Australian Labor Ministry is
“not fit to carry offal to a bear.” Which,
when you come to think of it, is true, and
the same might be said of Rembrandt,
George Washington, Garibaldi and the
Apostle Paul. But why carry offal to bears
in this large general way, seeing that most
of the tribe prefer fruit and vegetables?
Why not carry the offal to Whitsitt, seeing
that he raised the subject? Then if he didn’t
happen to require any more offal just then
he could pass it on to the bear, provided
that beast didn’t flee with its hair standing
on end when it saw him coming. And if the
bear did retreat in disorder AVhitsitt would
at least have the society of the offal to keep
him from feeling lonesome, and the offal
would have the society of Whitsitt, and the
Labor Government would be satisfied, and
the bear would probably be satisfied, and it
is difficult to see that anybody would have
just cause of complaint.
“The merchants of Sydney are all in a
state of bankruptcy,” wrote Henry Parkes
in a letter to his sister dated September 15,
1841 —just 70 years ago. Things have
changed since then. In the same
letter he tells that a young woman,
who had just arrived in an emi-
grant ship, was ordered “to sit for
one hour in the stocks!” because
she had no home to go to, and was
found on Queen’s Wharf by a
policeman. That was how the olden
Tory crowd encouraged immigra-
tion.
Most Melbourne people have only just dis-
covered that the little suburban Brighton
Gas Co. charges Is. less for cooking-gas and
something less for lighting-gas than the huge
Melbourne octopus squeezes out of the
metropolis. The fact is the best of evidence
that the Melbourne City Council’s electric-
lighting business is not doing the work it
was intended to do —or, anyhow, ought to do.
The Hobart Gas Co. neatly forestalled elec-
tric competition by establishing a make-
believe electric service of its own; and the
fat and sleepy Melbourne City Council is
playing into the hands of the Melbourne Gas
Co. just as effectively as if the electric-light
department were also a branch of the Gas
Co.’s business.
Tlie distinguishing characteristics of “Lib-
eralism” are the grand old British freedom
of thought and action which it allows its
adherents, and its quiet, strong, law-abiding
spirit. Oh, certainly! F’rinstance, accord-
ing to S. M. Herald (23/9/T1) :
The Liberal League at Stuart Town (which is
situated in the Robertson electorate, for which Mr.
Willis was defeated at the last election) passed the
following resolution at last meeting:—
“The Liberal League at Stuart Town is greatly
surprised at Mr. Willis’s action in accepting the
Speakership. He was always treated like a gentle-
man when member for this electorate. If he dares
to show his face in Stuart Town he shall be hanged
like a dog.”
The Liberal feature of the above is that
Willis is to be merely hanged—not boiled.

The S. M. Herald (18/9/T1) thought it
had discovered a curiosity:—
It is curious that the present (N.S.W.) Government,
which, while in Opposition, clamantly preached the
doctrine of non-borrowing, should thus early in its
career have discovered that one of its main planks
was unworkable, and discredited.
As usual, the Herald hasn’t quite got the
hang of it. The Labor party never tried its
own plank to see whether it was workable
or not. And even the S. M. Herald ought
to know that a ship isn’.t necessarily un-
seaworthy because it hasn’t yet made a
voyage.
That eminent alligator-sitter, Johnson, of
N.S.W., nearly swallowed himself in his
anger, the other night, in the House of
Reps, when Webster is alleged to have said
that “the ex-Premier of N.S.W. was paid a
sum annually for bolstering up the Em-
ployers’ Federation.” Bellowed Johnson:
“There is nothing more despicable than for
a member to make a personal attack on a
person outside.” “Are you referring to Mr.
Waddell’s attack on Judge Higgins?” asked
Page, sweetly. Then Johnson sat down and
glared at Page till his eye got tired.

S. M. Herald (23/9/11) begins to grope
after the light:—
The existing N.S.W. House threatens the whole
value of Parliament to the community. It should
be ended. The first two sessions of this Parliament
are an almost conclusive argument for unification.
A Legislature that can do no better than ours with
the concerns of the State had better be exchanged
for Federal control.
The latest trouble in the United Kingdom
is the strike of Irish railway men. Why
thev should go on strike passes comprehen-
sion. The wages work out at an average of
19s. Bfd. per week, and after making allow-
ance for the better-paid men—engine-
drivers, station-masters and the like—there
must still be 10s. or 12s. a week for the rank
and file. And when a man is wallowing in
the affluence of 10s. or 12s. a week what
more does he want?
BOOTS AND BREAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
These people are continually being tempted to
spend all their spare intellectual energy upon “the
raucous orators of the market-place,” and upon the
one-sided publications of the Labor-Socialist move-
ment. Both orators and publications appeal to
bitter class-hatred, and offer mere materialistic
gains as the supreme end of man’s existence. — Mel-
bourne Argus.
When we arise upon the stump to preach our gospel
of good times,
Stout screams a silly tale of slump, and slates us
for our nameless crimes;
When we appeal to Stout to help his helpless sick,
his broken poor,
We hear a sudden, painful yelp of trouble coming
swift and sure —
A prophecy of blight and rot, and Johnny-get-your-
gun...
What sort of slump is that, if not a gross, material-
istic one?
In these materialistic days the future seems unusual
bright,
When Labor tries, in various ways, to treat himself
(and brother) right,
When Labor strives for bread and cheese, for bright-
red, juicy, butcher’s meat,
For sound, assorted groceries, and chairs, and bed-
steads. Life is sweet
When shines the kindly sun; when each of us is
fairly clothed and fed,
And Labor doth not cease, to preach its gospel of
good boots and bread.
Good boots and tucker fail to touch the right, the
most uplifting spot?
They are the primal cause of much that makes the
toiler’s rotten lot?
It may be so, indeed —and then it may be other-
wise, of course;
It may be bad for people when the plans of Labor
they endorse; •
But still it’s joy, when Fatness bawls his silly
bawls of blast and wreck,
To see how Labor manna falls and smites him rudely
in the neck!
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.

REGULARITY OF THE BOWELS.
THE FIRST LINE OF DEFENCE
AGAINST ILL-HEALTH.
When the bowels are clogged the waste
matter decays and ferments and enters the
Blood, and is carried to all parts of the
body, producing Headaches, Biliousness,
Sleeplessness, Heartburn, Loss of Appetite.
Indigestion, Neuralgia, Rheumatism, and
various other ailments disturbing the Heart
and Nervous System; and if continued is
liable to cause inflammation of the Bowels,
Liver and Kidneys.
To maintain a healthy system the bowels
should operate at least once every 24 hours.
This is one of Nature’s wise provisions which
is too often ignored, and the result is untold
suffering. Women and children are the great-
est offenders, but why such should be the
case is a problem to be solved. .

SPORTING
NOTIONS
The Athletic Carnival, at Sydney Sports
Ground last Saturday was the biggest affair of its
kind held in Sydney for more years than one cares
to remember. It was run under the auspices of the
N.S.AV. Rugby League. Over 6000 people paid for
admission —a phenomenal attendance for an athletic
gathering in the Ma metropolis. The meeting
sported two great attractions —the meeting between
the Australian flier Donaldson and Yankee Holway,
which attracted the real sports, and the appearance
of some 16 ladies in a magnificent leg-show, which
drew the other sort. The run between Holway and
Donaldson was an intensely interesting affair. On
July 8 this year, Holway ran away from Arthur
Postle and A. A. Taylor over 130yds. at Sydney
Agricultural Ground, in, according to the clock,
12 7-10 sec. Donaldson had broken down in training,
and could not take part, and he objected to Holway
grabbing any title till he had met him, Donaldson,
and defeated him. The dispute raged long and
loud ; but on Saturday it was dragged to an end by
Donaldson streaking over the course (130yds. 4in.)
in 12sec. If this was correct it is a world’s record,
the previous best being that of W. Johnson, of Eng-
land, who covered the distance in 12 l-Bsec. The
Australian demonstrated how easy it was for long
legs and a frame to outpace the cart-horse-
built variety of ped. A good start saw the Yankee
bound away, and for 30yds. there was little between
them; then the long striding Donaldson began to
come along with a 500-motor-h.p. pace, and, racing
away from Holway at 70yds., ran straight as an
arrow, and won by some 15ft. The crowd howled
with delight. It was a good tussle for half the
journey, but thereafter the Australian never looked
anything but an easy winner. The three timekeepers
agreed that Donaldson had flown over the distance
in 12sec.-—equal to 10yds. under evens.
Of the 16 acceptors for the ladies’ champion-
ship, three failed to don the running kirtle or
knickers, and the remaining 13 received a wild
ovation from the crowd. But the race was a com-
paratively tame affair, for the result was never in
doubt. Miss Moore, who recently gathered in the
amateur championship, easily packed the “ £ 5 open
order” in her peggy-bag, with Miss Meredith as
runner-up. Among the starters were seven with the
prefix “Mrs.,” and their “form” tended to the belief
that they had left their best efforts at home while
careering after the children.
Talking about running, A. B. Postle made
things hum at Pittsworth (Q.) last week, when,
after gathering in the 7 syds. Sheffield Handicap
from the 2yds. mark, in 7 l-ssec., he demoralised
the 70yds. record. Three timekeepers agreed that
the distance was stamped over in 7sec., which is a
mighty fine sprint.
Saturday’s Victorian League football final
was an episode of disappointment and a tragedy of
lost opportunity. Given a fine day, and Essendon
and ’AVood at the top of their form, and there should
have been an exhibition of fast, sparkling football.
But light showers throughout the first half made
ball and ground greasy, and the anxiety of the
players made their fingers likewise. For half the
game the 43,905 suectators urged the “Magpies” to
“play their game,” but the scrambling continued.
Essendon was little better, except that it could mark
a bit, yet its lead at the interval should have been
30 points instead of 16. The last half was ’AVood’s.
By sheer persistency it pulled up to within one
point of ’Don. Then the latter made a last dash, and
scored two goals. The game seemed over, as there
were but five more minutes to play, and the crowd
began to stream out. But ’AVood wasn’t yet finished.
AVithin a minute it had passed the ball down
between the posts. It repeated the manoeuvre,
but only a behind resulted, and the hands were
marking the hour. Then,' right on time, a ’AVood
forward marked within easy distance, and carefully
“placed” the ball. A goal meant a draw and an-
other chance. But the kick went wide, and the bell
rang out the premiership of ’Don, which won by five
goals 11 behinds to four goals and 11 behinds. It
was a sloppy, untidy, but withal, courteous game,
ruined by anxiety and lack of confidence. ’Don won
by better marking and surer handling.
Essendon, Victoria’s new premier club, hadn’t
arrived at the top since 1901, when the same Colling-
wood was second; but from 1891 till ’94, it held
the fort against all-comers, and ran through one
whole season unbeaten. This year’s triumph is a
feather in the cap of that champion coach, Johnny
AVorrail, who had a good team to work on, and kept
it well strung to the finish. The “gates” of this
year’s three “big” matches were £1624, £1398,
and £1584, a total of £4606, nearly £5OO above
last year’s takings for the four “finals.” Against
this sum the Association, for its four games, raked
in £1435, which is a decisive answer to those who
claim that Ass. ball is better worth watching than
the League variety. Also, the gentry who claim that
the “gate” is everything, may note that had Essen-
don lost on Saturday, it still had an even chance,
and the League should have been £l5OO or so better
off. Yet Essendon didn’t lose.
The Ass. Essendon —the real Essendon,
for the League club of that name doesn’t belong to
that suburb at all —was simultaneously deciding its
challenge final against Brunswick, which had beaten
it by a pair of points the week before. Like Colling-
wood in the League, Essendon took the risk of play-
ing a damaged man, though, as he kicked two goals,
he couldn’t have been quite dead. As in the League,
the cooler and more seasoned team made better use of
what few chances it got, and won—-Essendon, four
goals 10 behinds to ’AVick’s three goals eight b-'-
hinds. The play was mannerly, but lacked the pre-
cision of games that counted for much less. This is
Essendon’s first Ass. premiership, though it has been
third the last two seasons, and its arrival is chiefly
due to the play and energetic leadership of one man,
whom St. Kilda, of the League, fired out last season.
AVe will now, dearly beloved, adjourn to the
cricket pitch, where the “flannelled fool” awaits us,
and the seaside, where the shark loafs easily around,
watching for a nice plump leg for breakfast.

Soccer is the only branch of , football
which hasn’t been stowed away in Sydney. Gran-
ville’s defeat of Sydney, on Saturday, by four goals
to one, makes a final match with Balmain necessary
to determine the holders of the Rawson Cup—that, is,
if the protest bv Granville against another club is
upheld by the Association.
N.S.W. Rugbv League put up its shutters
and retired for its summer sleep heavier in the
pocket than ever. In fact, the pocket bulged so
much that the League, feeling good, handed the
£9OO taken at the “double final” gates to charity,
and thereby earned considerable blessing and credit.
The fearsome expansion of the League brand of
lioof-ball is crowding the opposition so badly that
next season’s struggle for playing grounds has begun
already, and the underground engineers are in con-
stant work.
The old Hawkesbury (N.S.W.) club sup-
plied the harvest, for the bookmakers on Saturday,
and gave the prads a final non-committal gallop be-
fore the big Spring meeting. The Hawkesbury
Guineas comprised the big event, and, though only
five three-year-olds were saddled up for the £250
prize, the race was well contested, and Pontano won
with but a head to spare from Popinjay and Alured.
The Hawkesbury Spring Handicap—which was a prize
valued in davs a-rear —went to Britain, with Sand-
bath and Flaxen ahead of six others. Three of the
winners of the day—Lager, Borambil and Britain
are in the Epsom Handicap on the coming Saturday,
and their form makes the tangle worse from the
punters’ viewpoint. The bookmakers had a profit-
able afternoon, as only one winner was weighed
down with "old to the position of favorite.
If the Epsom falls to a Victorian candi-
date, the animal will probably be Broadsword. The
son of Clanranald is understood to be going for all
he’s worth, and he’s worth a whole crowd of ordinary
horses when in a good humor. Broadsword can
carry the weight (9st. 41b.), anyhow; 101 b. more
than he carried in the Doncaster iast April will not
make him go faster, but it will not stop him, and,
in the meantime, he has probably improved. Lady
Medallist looks a better chance on their Maoriland
form, but Broadsword is judged more on his pro-
gressive racing since he settled in Australia.
* * * *
These recent failures of Blairgour would
suggest that the somewhat in-bred son of Wallace
and Wakeful cannot go a mile if he were not already
credited with an easy win over a longer course. A
year ago Blairgour, starting a hot favorite, got away
with the Memsie Stakes at Caulfield, which is a
nine-furlongs event. It was not a big performance,
but it made his subsequent collapse in the Caulfield
Guineas look all wrong. Again in the autumn, the
animal stopped unaccountably in the Newmarket,
after his end-to-end victory in the Futurity Stakes
(seven furlongs) had paralysed the betting market.
His two notable failures last season, when he was
apparently “fit,” contrasted with his three notable
successes, left the question of Blairgour’s stamina
open to doubt. His “form” this season, thusfar,
leaves it just where it was. Just the sort of queer
customer to score a surprise in a 12-furlongs race.
The return to the old order of things with
the Sydney and Melbourne racing fixtures this spring,
will revive the old possibilities of Randwick “form”
being indorsed at Caulfield and re-indorsed at Flem-
ington—if it hasn’t gone stale between the end of
September and the beginning of November. Last
year the Randwick meeting overlapped the opening
day at Caulfield, and the three nlaced ones in Caul-
field Cup, run on October 15, had all been wanted
for the Metropolitan 12 days previously. Two of
them —Apple Pie and Britain —had been rushed over
to Melbourne after runninsr at Randwick only a week
before. This year the Metropolitan is dated October
2, and the Caulfield Cup comes on October 21, or 19
days later. There was the same interval between the
two handicaps when Poseidon ran second for the one
and captured the other. The brilliant three-year-old
maintained his “form” from the Sydney Tatt.’s
meeting, on September 8, to the Melbourne Cup, on
November 6, but a Poseidon, like a Carbine, happens
only occasionally. As a rule, the nags ripe enough
for big performances at Randwick are going stale
before the close of Melbourne Cup meeting. Flav-
inius, last year, was a case in point, and punters
should note the fact that three-year-olds, anyhow,
are likely to remain in “nick” longer than old
horses.
Grafnax (Grafton-Xantippe), Australia’s
champion steeplechaser, winner of over £3500 in
stakes alone, jumped into eternity last Thursday
without hitting the top rail. In the Australian
Steeplechase, last mouth, he came to grief at a
hurdle, and fractured one of his stifles. Necrosis,
and then enteritis, set in, and vet. AY. J. Miller
could do nothing. The prad’s first victory was as a
five-year-old, when he won the Eumolo Cup (flat
racing) at Cootamundra (N.S.AY.). Then he was
entered for hurdles, and won a jumpers’ flat race at
Rosehill in 1908, and the Maiden Steeplechase at
the 1909 Grand National meeting. In 1910 he ran
first in both A.J.C. steeples, and in the Vie. G.N.
Steeple the same year, with list. 121 b. up. Last
season he won the Australian Steeplechase at Caul-
field, with 12st. 121 b. up, and did it in 7min. 54fsec.,
but at the G.N. meeting at Flemington he only ran
third. He started favorite in the Australian Steeple
last month with 13st. 21b. in the saddle, and fell
with the above-mentioned fatal results. He was
owned by J. P. Cox, of Mora station, and trained
by McGrath, of Kensington.
On South Aus. racecourses the innovation
of an eighth event is creeping in. For some time
past seven races have been more usual than six. The
many racegoers who don’t profess to know the points
of a horse and don’t go near the saddling-paddock,
find three-quarters of an hour between events too
long a time to wait. The club doesn’t mind giving
an extra £l5O stake, because, if £2OOO more goes
through the totalisator over it, the club’s 74 per
cent, commission pays the stake. And, as owners
can see their weights for a sovereign, and accept
for another, they like it too. It is the totalisator
that does it all.
Bookie Sol Green has but small respect for
brother Galilees, and in the past two weeks is
alleged to have supported Comedy King to win a
small fortune in the Melbourne Cup. The imported
prad now rules at a short price. The move has
brought about a scare in the illicit short-price places
where the gambling law is side-stepped; and as
Green is credited with putting the commission
simultaneously in the othpr States, the yelling of
Irish Moses spreads throughout Australia. It may
be rough from a bookmaking point of view, but it
is excellent sport.
Of the 127 acceptors in t&e Goulburn-to-
Sydney Dunlop cycle race, exactly 100 faced the
starter ; no less than three of the scratch men failed
to put in an appearance. Of the actual starters
from scratch two were from Victoria, one from
AVestralia, and two from Maoriland. The Ma State
hadn’t a single representative on the line of difficulty.
Of the five one was placed, D. Kirkham, of Victoria,
being second. A. Pianta. of AVestralia, came 20th.
The actual winner was AV. Rennie, of Victoria —that
State supplying the first three men home. Kirk-
ham, who ran second, put up the fastest time over
the difficult 131-mile course, and created a new
record (6hr. 19min. 31sec.). The previous best
was Tom Larcombe’s 6hr. 35min. The Dunlop Co.
is justly proud of the fact that the six first men
home were mounted on its tyres. The roads seem
to have been fairly good throughout, any rain that
had fallen merelv serving to lay the dust. Falls and
punctures, though numerous, were not severe. About
the worst damage done was when Honevman, of
Sydney, broke his handle bar, but he patched it up
somehow and ran into fifth place. It was a good
race, but the result does not indicate that the Ma
State has any great hope of winning, the great AVarr-
nambool. That looks like a certainty for A’ictoria
this 3 r ear.

Last tveek was a time of record annihila-
tion. On Saturday, sprinter Jack Donaldson put up
a world’s best by fleeing on foot over 130yds. in
12sec., at Sydney; and cyclist Kirkham clipped
15min. 57sec. off the previous best effort for the
131-mile stretch between Goulburn and Ashfield. On
Friday, A. B. Postle careered over 70yds. in 7sec.,
at Pittswortli (0.), and put a new world’s record
on the slate; while at Adelaide (S.A.), on the same
day, a misguided weight-lifter hoisted 2371 b. above
his head in a clean hoist, and thereby made short-
weiffht of all previous efforts for a 10-stone person.
Australia, therefore, supplied something for the
balance of the world to worry about.
That: there is still good rowing material in
N. S. Wales, was demonstrated on the Parramatta
by a couple of raw youths last Saturday, at the
regatta of the Kemp Professional Club. One very
dangerous-looking infant, named George Jack, a 16-
ycar-old, who weighs 13st., led the back markers
right up to the line, and was only beaten on the
post. This, after less than two months’ experience
in a sculling boat, and notwithstanding that he
stopped several times, and had some narrow escapes
from a capsize. He is a most promising water-pup,
and gives promise of being an even greater Dick
Arnst. The other infant phenomenon is J. Lorimer,
who, though not so promising as the other, is prom-
ising enough to go on with.
