Besides giving the punters a fair deal the
Tote befriends the Government which sanctions it.
Last year Maoriland raked in £49,000 from the Tote
Tax, and there is little doubt that, by sticking to the
bookmakers, N. S. Wales and Victoria are each
throwing away something like £lOO,OOO per annum.
And that heap of money isn’t to be sniffed at even
by such bloated States as N.S.W. and Victoria.
The fact that Grade Cricket will com-
mence in Sydney on Saturday, is an official an-
nouncement that summer is here. The senior clubs
will number 13, and the Second and Third grades
will have 19 each. Which means that every grade
is to be plagued with the infernal nuisance of a
bye - .
Judging by the preliminary roll on the
field the old-time N.S.W. cricket cracks are going to
make a splash this season. Trumper rattled up 112,
not out, in a total of 228 against Marrickville, and
Macartney made 90 of the 152 scored against St.
George. “Mary Ann” distinguished himself with the
ball, and smashed up six Marrickville wickets for 43
runs. There is a dark suspicion that if M.A.N. will
only fall off the high horse on which he perched last
year, he will be a useful man to have about the
premises when Warner’s team is trying to break in
and burgle the “ashes.” #
The N.S.W. Cricket Association has de-
cided that the captain in all big matches shall be
chosen by the selection committee. That committee
consists of Trumper, Bardsley and Hume; so, pre-
sumably, Trumper will not be deposed, in spite of
the complaints as to his lack of genius in choosing
his bowling talent last season. Trumper, with his
judgment sharpened by his season’s experience, isn’t
likely to disgrace the position, but, all the same,
N.S.W. is a cold place for the cricketing colt. He
has to wait a long time for that room which is
always said to exist at the top.
N.S.W. Cricket Association shows sound
common-sense in the engagement of Alec Ban-
nerman as coach.” Alec’s good work contributed
not a little to the N.S.W. success against the
opposing States and Springboks, and, with an Eng-
lish team coming this season, and an Australian
team going to Britain next season, there is more
scope for his efforts than ever.
After all, South Australia isn’t going to
lose left-hand trundler W. J. Whitty, who has been
recuperating in N.S.W. S. Aus. needs the cham-
pion left-hander more than Ma: and the return of
the wanderer will make much difference to the Crow-
Eaters in the representative matches.
Victorian Cricket Association will pre-
sent the takings of the Vic. v. England match, on
February 2 and following days, as a benefit to Jack.
Blackham, Australia’s great wicket-keeper in days
gone by. It is a graceful act. Considering the
iron wall that the wicket-keeper is expected to repre-
sent, no player deserves and requires a benefit more
than that battered individual.
Trade brochures are not usually interest-
ing, but the writer became so engrossed in the
Dunlop Co.’s “All About Cycling” that he
read on till he had left his station 20
miles behind. The pamphlet told how to
fix up almost every trouble that could possibly occur
to a cyclist, but it said nothing about how a man
walking on two sticks was to get back over 20
miles, so there was nothing for it but to pay, money
to Mr. Johnson and take the next train. Anvone
desiring to get back on his enemv should carefully
provide him with a copv of “All About Cycling”
three stations from his destination.
Sam McVea, colored pug, is also a motorist
and a sailor. He spent a good part of his life at
sea, and the last time he was in Australia was with
the sailing ship Melindo. On the trip out in the
Argyllshire he took his turn at the wheel every day,
and steered the boat into Adelaide harbor, and also
through Svdney Heads. He says he is anxious to
meet Johnson, and, “if I don’t beat him in the ring,
I’ll beat him on my motor.” He has two cars, one
a racer, and Lill’ Arthur is bringing out three. The
race has already been arranged, and McVea is going
to enter some events here to get into form. Sam
has also had a trip in an aeroplane, but thinks that
one was enough. .Johnson can have that sort of
flying all to himself. #
Ern Waddy, the Adelaide “hope,” who
knocked out McDonald in seven rounds and Devitt
(who had the experience of 60 fights thick upon him)
in eight, is a bit of a marvel. He bakes bread all
night, and does no training to speak of. For some
reason he is always the unpopular party in a scrap
—the crowds take a sudden and vehement dis-
like to him, and he usually ignores the crowds in
a way that drives them mad. Only once has he talked
back, and that was when McDonald was hammering
awav at him. Then he did the Johnson act: “All
right, you blankers.” he said; “I’ll show you
what I can do with him !” And he did, to such a
degree that an expert at the back of the three bob
seats said, “My Gard, Mack’ll be over here soon.”
Curiously enough, as far back as July
and the early weeks of August, the Yankee sporting
press prophesied that there would be a.n outcry
against this Johnson-Wells fight in England. The
Yankee papers, however, calculated that it would
be from the fight followers and not from the Metho-
dists, on the ground that the fight was a one-sided
affair, in which a white man was being pitted against
a black with whom he had no chance. The yell
from the pious clan was unexpected. As a matter of
fact, the Mclnstoush was offered control of the scrap
somewhere about the end of June, and turned it
down in his own inimitable style. All the world of
London wondered —but the Magnificent knew when
to come in out of the wet. As a matter of fact, it
is rather unfortunate that the Bombardier has been
pitted against the Golden-Mouthed One. For the
Bombardier is coming on very well, and on appear-
ances onlv requires to smash round among men of
his own class a bit more to blossom out into a real
success. Pitting him against Johnson at this stage
is nipping him in the bud, and the sports it.
Sam Austin, one of the authorities on the
“game” in the great and glorious “Yewnited Stites,”
admits to a profound belief in one Tony Ross. Tony
is an Italian, who distinguished himself by putting
up a whirlwind fight against the big black. Joe
Jeannette, who probably stands third on the list of
heavyweights, the gentlemen above him being John-
son himself and Langford. At long-range fighting
Tonv was outpointed bv the veteran, but in the in-
roughing he gave his duskv conqueror a lively time.
He has a tremendous capacity for punishment, and
regarded as thistledown blows that would have sent
to sleep most of the prominent occupants of _ the
square circle. Langford, who outpointed him in a
six-round go, declared him the toughest morsel he
had tried to chew. Jeannette, after the scrap, said
Ross had hurt him a good deal, and had shown that,
with a little more experience, he would have “chawed
me right up.” Jeannette had his bruises thick
upon him when he made that admission, and next
day promptly denied a lot of things, but it seems
that while his bashes were fresh he spoke with an
open soul. But even afterwards he admitted that
he landed a number of blows which he considered
the knock-out kind, and found them of no value.
Yet, somehow, this writer has no great faith in a
bruiser whose stock-in-trade is inability to feel. It’s
ability to make the other cove feel that really counts
in bruising.
The pug is evidently no mug—in Eng-
land, anyhow ; and the agitation against the .Johnson-
Wells fight revealed the fact that pugs have a trades
union of their own. The union protests against the
agitation, and in doing so hits the parsonical crowd
fair on the midriff, with a claim that professional
boxing develops the moral qualities in a way that
study for a course of divinity does not. The boxer,
to be successful, has to live wholesomely, for if he
does not eschew vices, some more moral pug chews
him right up the first time he shows his nose inside
a ring. All the same, the discovery that the bruisers
have a union is liable to cause the audience to hold
its tongue in its che_k while it watches the gory
scrap. Nobody will really believe that two trades
unionists will do their best to kill each other for the
amusement of a mostly blacklev crowd.
* * * *
Followers of the noble art of woodening
one’s fellow-man for gold and the odd change who
missed the scrap between Australia’s lightweight
champion, Hughie Mehegan, and the Yanko-Italio
boxer, Frank Picato, on Saturday night, missed the
cleanest, fairest and one of the fastest exhibitions
yet staged in the big Mclnstadium in Sydney. It is
a good job that the Shakers and Dry Baptists weren’t
there, or their howls of protest would have been
closed for the rest of the season, at least, and
Mclntosh would have been minus his best and
cheapest advertising medium. One instance will
serve to show the spirit that animated the boxers
throughout the 20 rounds. In the tenth session
Picato struck his opponent low; of course, without
intention. Mehegan lowered his arms, bent up with
pain, and Picato likewise stopped, no doubt ex-
pecting an appeal for a foul. After a moment’s rest,
however, the Australian straightened up. “Box on,”
was all he said, and whirled his weapons. Picato,
equally sportsmanlike, put up his hands, but did no
more than defend himself until his opponent had re-
covered from the unfortunate blow. Picato surprised
the majority of those who were present with the
clever showing he made against Mehegan. It is true
that he was about 101 b. heavier than the latter, but
just prior to the fight it was known that he was not
in such splendid health. The Australian was fit to
fight for the ransom of a rich uncle; his blows were
timed with extra precision, and his smother was
almost as protective as a rabbit-proof fence. Never-
theless, he seems to be developing a tendency to
overdo the smother tactics, and on Saturday received
some whangs on the forehead that, coming from a
man with more mule power in his upper hoof, would
have led to possible recriminations at the coroner’s
quarters. Up till the 12th round there was not f a
great deal to choose between the men, Mehegan’s
cleverness just keeping him a bit ahead in the
points. The 13th continuation of the shindy wgs
Picato’s, and it seemed then that he had suddenly
developed an unsuspected mine of confidence, and
was going to pay dividends. Mehegan, however,
had been saving himself, and a few rounds later
went in to give his man the rounds of the kitchen,
in order to make sure of victory. The last lap saw
him spring at the enemy like a wild cat with a buU
ant on his conscience, and when the gong exploded
the issue was beyond doubt —Mehegan the winner on
points. All the same, the Italian-Yankee isn’t done
with yet by a considerable distance.
Throughout last week golfers smote and
swore over the Rose Bay (Sydney) links, in deciding
the baubles attending their sidelight of sport. The
Australian championship final fell to J. D. Howden
(Vic.), from C. Felstead (Vic.) —in fact, the John
Weary State supplied the last four to lend a hand
in the struggle. But N.S.W. golfers reversed mat-
ters in the inter-State matches, wherein Victoria was
annihilated by six matches to one, and the monopoly
which has existed for six years thereby checked. The
ladies also held up Ma’s mantelpiece, narrowly win-
ning their dustup by two holes in a match.
The announcement that a 14-year-old
girl swung clubs in Muswellbrook (N.S.W.) School
of Arts from 10 a.m. till midnight is worthy of
notice —much notice of a hostile nature. First, some
intelligent medical man should put his professional
reticence aside, and sound that girl’s heart, and tell
the misguided parents the exact truth. The chances
are she will grow up—if she lives —-a querulous,
weak, useless woman. Then McGowen’s Government
should hastily pass an Act dealing out six months’
hard “jug” to anyone who aids or abets such exhi-
bitions, for if these long-endurance swings _ become
popular among children, the results will be disastrous
and numerous. This child’s exhibition, may have
been perfectly legal, but it was a crime against
Nature, all the same. And Nature always exacts a
penalty without the option. There is no option in
that court.

Died, last week, John A. Barry, writer of
many sea yarns, though his best-known
“Steve Brown’s Bunyip” was pitched on
shore. After 12 years’ seafaring he retired
with a mate’s certificate, and went to the
Palmer diggings, in North Queensland ; then
he had some years of droving, digging and
boundary riding, and some more years on the
coasters, until finally, about ’96, he settled
down on the staff of the Sydney Evening
News.
Only a short while back the “Liberal”
lady went from door to door, stampeding
the women voters into voting Anti-Sosh,
with terrible descriptions of how tyrannical
unions robbed the working man, and of course
his “poor” wife and children. But the
boom in unionism has brought thousands of
men and women within the scope of various
awards; and now. when the “Liberal” lady
gets on to the union curse, she is hit with
something like this: “Well, before Tom
joined the union he only got 365. a week,
and now he gets 455.” ; or there’s a cold re-
mark that the union raised Johnny’s wages
55., and Kitty’s wages 7s. It is a clever
canvasser that can hold out against the hard,
tough facts concerning Johnny’s extra five
shillings and Kitty’s seven.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of a hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.
C. Newlings: (1) The Bulletin’s ligures
did represent British exports and imports, and when
you say they are exports only, you are simply absurd.
(2) The Bulletin pointed, on the authority of the
English Statesman’s Year Book, to a decline in the
digging out of iron ore, in blast furnaces, pig-iron,
coal, cottons, woollens, linens, shipping in actual
service, shipbuilding, railway revenue, etc. Your
remarks about an increase in the one item of motor-
cars has no bearing on these general figures. (3)
The gross value of manufactures in Australia in 1909
was £107,409,733, and the gross value of all other
industries about £134,000,000, so your figures are
miles out. You make up your case by comparing
the net output of factories with the gross output of
other industries. (4) The manufacturer pays better
wages for short hours than the farmer does for long
ones. He is compelled to do it by Wages Boards
and Arbitration Courts, while the farmer is allowed
to sweat without limit, on the ground that his in-
dustry can’t afford to pay decent wages. If he isn't
protected much lie makes it up in cheap labor. And,
in addition, the N.S.W. railways (which work on
the same basis as other Australian railways) give
him gorgeous assistance. Mis produce is carried at
an average of one-third of Id. per ton per mile,
while manufactured goods pay from Id. to 4id. (5)
Considering the irenzy with which you describe ail
manufacturers as “trusts ’ and “combines" and
“monopolists,” you had better go naked and eat
your food raw and sleep out. What do you mean
by patronising these “trusts" and “combines” and
“monopolists”? (6) Your statement that every pro
tected European country is in revolt is bunkum.
There is one big row in Spain and another in Eng-
land, and some few strikes elsewhere. (7) This
paper never said that Britain hadn’t the biggest
Empire on earth. By the way, that Empire is mostly
Protectionist. (8) Britain hasn’t “practically
a monopoly of the trade of her Dominions.” She
hasn’t quite half of it. (9) Britain is in most years
the world’s greatest shipbuilder, though, in 1908, the
United States is said to have been a fraction ahead
for once. (10) Britain, great as she is, doesn’t own
“more than half” the world’s shipping. (11) The
other discrepancies would take too much space
L.L.: (1) Your suggestion for making estates
unlimited in size, but requiring the owner to em-
ploy a certain amount of labor, would only cause the
big landlord (if he didn’t really need so much labor)
to give nominal employment, at 6d. a week and
sleeping accommodation, to old-age pensioners. (2)
The idea that no “machine” should be sold at over
100 per cent, on the cost of production is all right or
all wrong, according to what you mean by it. It is
quite possible to spend £lOOO or £lO,OOO on experi-
ments before the machine gets bn the market; then
to spend £lO,OOO on appliances for the manufacture
of the machine ; and then, inside a year, to find the
whole thing turned into a bad debt because some-
body else evolves a better machine out of the original
idea. The actual inventor of the machine, if it is a
good machine, generally deserves 100 or 200 per cent.
The middleman who exploits the inventor seldom de-
serves 10 per cent Alec. L.: Verses just missed

A SMART CAPTURE.
The Woman woke with a start
There was someone in the room
And listened for a minute
All was silent as the Tomb
But when she rose from her Bed
To press the alarm
A hand grinped her throat
And another her Arm
She was strangled in silence
And the Murderer bold
Lit his lantern and searched
Round the room for Gold.
But a smart detective
Saw the flickering light
And captured the Villain
Who did the Deed that night.

THE BRIDE’S GOOD-BYE.
The bride has "one from her native home
Full many a mile away;
She has flown from her birthplace far to roam
Never again to stay.
The echoing peals of her youthful voice
And the merry, merry laugh
Shall cease the old heart to rejoice
And I write the epitaph.
’Tis an epitaph but the tomb is vast ;
’Tis the home of her childhood’s days
And the scenes shall cling and bring up the past
Bringing back all the old time ways. /
The bare old arms of the settler gums
(No longer rejoicing in life)
Stand quaint and still like a forest of tombs
These all make the parting loath.
You rollicking soul! Back to your undertakery!

NIGHT BY ITCHING
And Burning. Sore Places on Baby’s
Cheeks, Hips, Spine and in Bend
of Arms and Knees. Could Hardly
Walk. Was 111 for Want of Rest.
Got Cuticura Remedies and Now has
Not the Least Sign of Eczema.
*‘My son when two years old had eczema
very badly. He had great sore places on the
KL
edges of his bones, the
lobe of each ear had a
great split, the sores ex-
tending over the jawbone
on to the cheek, and on
the cheek bones against
the eyes were some more
sores, making him look
ghastly. He had a large
.sore on each hip and all
up his spine, and in the
bend of his arms and knees.
The latter were so bad that
the child could hardly
walk. Whenever the child
scratched himself, some
watery matter would come from the places
like great drops of perspiration He was tor-
tured day and night with the awful burning
and itching sensations. I had tried several
remedies, and then I went to the doctor
but the stuff I had from him made not the
slightest impression. The child was getting
quite ill for the want of proper rest.
“I gave up going to the doctor, as a friend,
a nurse, gave me some Cuticura Ointment to
try and see if it would relieve my boy. I used
it for a week, and by that time the places
were looking decidedly less angry, and were
not nearly so irritable. I got some Cuticura
Ointment and Soap, and persevered with
them. He was in such a state I could not
make a tin of Ointment last ten days. I used
to smear the places over two or three times a
day. The eczema kept coming back, but
as soon as it showed 1 used Cuticura Oint-
ment. The boy is now thirteen, and not the
least sign of eczema.” (Signed) Mrs. A.
Oliver, The Gardens, Egerton House, Eger-
ton, near Ashford, Kent, Eng., May 11, 1910.
Cuticura Remedies are Bold everywhere. Send to R.
Towns & Co.. Sydney. NSW., for a liberal trial box
Of Cuticura Ointment and 32-page book on the skin.

The Inky Way.
Sydney Evening News runs a weekly page of
(mostly) London chatter, or blither, as a sort of
counter-blast to Emily Soldene’s effort in the loathly
contemp. The trouble is that the News stuff has a
habit of looking as if it were manufactured on this
side. F’rinstance: —
“Wilted Londoners are stealing silently out of the
great metropolis. The Society doll is tired. Dolly
must rest till next year. Cowes, Scotland, Cairo,
Strathpeffer, Margate, and Switzerland are, opening
their nets for butterflies.”
Cowes . . . Cairo . . . Margate l Just so, the London
inkslinger writes knowingly concerning the “luxuri-
ous colonial homes of Toorak, Kose Bay and Wool-
loomooloo."
* * * *
G. B. Stronach, late editor of Sydney Sunday
Sun, has gone north to the chair of the Brisbane
Daily Mail. It is said that the Mail has now got
boodle behind it, so, with Brains and Boodle, there
ought to be a good time ahead. Stronach has had a
fair tour along the Inky Way, having come in here
30-odd years ago. He joined the Sydney Even-
ing News, and soon rose to be sub-editor; then
Alfred Bennett made him editor of the Town and
Country. Lured to Westralia in the ’9o’s, he edited
the Perth Morning Herald, but resigned -when he
found that he was not to have a free hand. •• His
judgment proved correct; a few years later the
Herald died. Coming back to Sydney, J. M. San-
ders made him associate-editor of the Sunday Sun;
and when he became editor he lifted the paper to
a great circulation. Incidentally, he is- an M.A. and
Triple Prizeman of Edinburgh—but he never speaks
of little things like that. •
James Rutherford, just deceased, practically started
the Bathurst (N.S.W.) National Advocate. A
sturdy Protectionist was Rutherford, and in the
early days the only Bathurst daily was a Freetrade
sheet, which had - a marked disinclination to give
tariff reform orators a show. So the Advocate was
instituted, and began doing good work forthwith.
When George Reid announced, six years ago, that
the fiscal issue had been drowned, and that the
Federal battle lay between Socialism and Anti-
Socialism, the Advocate flung clods, the size of
cathedrals, at Reid, taking the view that the Sosh
business was a fraud. Rutherford couldn’t see it.
Age having somewhat impaired his intellect, he was
fain to take the stout impostor from E. Sydney
seriously. He stood the anti-Reid fulminations of
the paper for a while, and then resigned his direc-
torship. Later, he severed all connection with the
Advocate, and reviled it in the market place and
elsewhere.
* * * *
Adelaide Herald, of 12/9/’ll, achieved one of
those misprints which set the sub. wondering whether
or not the comp, did it on purpose. It advertised
that somebody’s mortal remains would be removed
for interment at such a time and place, and asked
“fiends” to accept the intimation!
* * * *
“P.X.”: Some day the Inky Wayfarers of Aus-
tralia will meet at a soda fountain or elsewhere,
and discuss how far a journalistic hot-gospeller is
entitled to go ink-wayfaring for the honor and
glory of the thing. Thus, it has just been announced
that “Hugh Mahon, M.H.R., has accepted the posi-
tion of hon. editor” of a new Westralian Labor
paper —a very praiseworthy thing, probably, from
one point of view, but pretty maddening from the
1 viewpoint of the man who gets his bread and butter
by editing newspapers. He, poor devil, is apt to
regard Mahon as much the same sort of person as
the clerk who helps to lift the Fat man’s wool into a
ship when the Fat man says he can’t afford to pay
the wages that the professional wharf-lumper re-
quires. The Labor people who own, or should own,
these papers, could afford to pay their editors
just as well as the Fat person alluded to. Both of
them suffer from the same disease—a dreadful dis-
inclination to part up.
* * * *
W. J. Chidley’s Melbourne-published pamphlet,
“The Answer,” in which the author attempted with
astonishing frankness to turn all accepted ideas of
sex-relation upside down, has reached London, and
M.A.P. gives it a special par., headed “A Vile
Book.” The editor says: “It is certainly one of the
most infamously wicked examples of sex-perversion
that has ever been printed. I at once submitted the
book to the National Council of Public Morals, and
I understand that it has been referred to the police.”
THE END OF THE ROAD.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Attend, ye eager youths who think
Of going on The Press,
Of winning fame through printers’ ink —
To this, my brief address.
Your hopes are boyish day-dreams,
What though your faith is new ?
The Press exists to slay dreams
Of children such as you.
There’s a drear and dismal corner that you’ll find
upon the track,
And you’ll turn it as a mourner —when you get to be
a hack 1
For there is no turning back,
On the pressman’s treadmill-track;
Ask the linesmen —they will tell you, what it means
to be a hack 1
Your best and brightest craftsmanship
The Press will take and use;
For, once it gets you in its grip,
This thing that men call News,
Of uttermost endeavor
’Twill bleed you, heart and brain,
And call you on for ever
To do it yet again.
Though you be a star reporter, winning cross-heads
big and black,
Day by day' your time grows shorter, till you find
yourself a hack.
With your methods of attack
Ineffectual and slack,
While the cubs you knew are subbing—glad to prac-
tise on the hack!
The cracks that take the gallery
May mock the fate in store;
The roundsman with a scoop may see
No future to deplore;
“Exclusive stuff’’ may brighten
The departmental men —
Yet grim forebodings frighten
The boldest, now and then;
Though they swear upon the Bible they will quit the
inky track,
Ere they’re outed by a libel, each may live to be a
hack.
Yes, it’s better than the sack;
Though the Chief of Staff looks black
When the book is overloaded, it’s a Godsend to the
hack!
The Press is no palladium
To those that labor there;
For rarer is than radium
An editorial chair ;
Their copy they may burnish
With quirk and crank and quip—
But shall their efforts furnish
A leader-writership ?
Therefore I, a pressman, pray you, while you yet
are cubs, turn back,
Ere the paper turn and slay you, or convert you to a
hack —
To a moaning maniac
On the dread diurnal rack;
For the triumph of the system is the tribute of the
hack 1
VICTORIA.
DELE.


THE SNOWY BREEZES OF SUMMER
IS THE UNIQUE OFFERED TO VISITORS
Jleuivt Wusfefisli.s
Climate .
Throughout the hottest summer great snow-drifts linger
on the vast shoulders of Mount Kosciusko —Australia’s
great mountain.
The snow-kissed breezes from the giant hills make
Kosciusko a wonderful sanatorium. The salubrity of its
summer climate is unexcelled —mild, bright, and bracing.
For residents of the warm parts of Aus-
tralia, the Kosciusko trip is a boon, afford-
ing a healthful change of air and scene.
The Trout Streams.
The trouting waters of the Kosciusko
uplands and foothills are said to equal the
finest Scottish streams.
The Hotel Kosciusko .
The Hotel Kosciusko, over 5,000 feet above sea level,
is a palace in the quiet of the mountains. Guests iind
every convenience and comfort, and an excellent cuisine.
Daily driving and riding trips are made to the summit,
to the summer snowfields. Tennis-courts and golf-links
(in preparation) are provided near the Hotel.
The Kosciusko Panoramas -
The scenery of the Kosciusko ridgt
is magnificent. The shaggy rock
chains and bottomless gorges rank
among the finest highland scenery in
the world.

BARRON GORGE.
MR* DONALD McDONALD (the well-known writer)
describes the trip as follows :
the greater part of the journey on the Queensland coast the steamer runs between the
H Great Barrier Reef and the mainland, making it one of the smoothest of the sea trips of Aus-
tralasia. The route is dotted with picturesque islands, covered with foliage strange to
southern eyes, and the boat is ever rounding headlands, streaming into fine bays and estuaries. The
famous Whitsunday Passage, between Mackay and Bowen, is extremely picturesque.”

IN BANANA LAND
“Kuscobin” : The Queensland Government
has set aside the C.D. Act in the metropoli-
tan area, so the good character which the
British Admiralty gave to Brisbane as being
a specially clean and healthy port, and miles
ahead of Sydney in a hygienic sense, will
probably be lost in very short order. It is
commonly reported that this relapse into
dirt and disease was the price paid by the
Government tor the snuffle vote at tile re-
cent by-elections.
Bananaland could improve on its method
of appointing its mining wardens. A young-
ster enters the office as licker, and gradually
ascends till he is Clerk of Petty Sessions.
Then, after some experience in granting
miners’ rights, etc., he goes forth as a full-
fledged warden, and writes the monthly
official report on the mines of his district.
This treatise is supposed to be reliable in-
formation, though, in many cases, it is
merely an account of what the owners say
about their shows, and what they say isn’t
qlways as truthful as it is enthusiastic.
Queensland’s active immigration operations
are enabling J. Bull to dispose of a portion
of his helpless population. Among the con-
tingent which arrived by a recent immigrant
ship were two small idiot children, two crip-
ples, and three boys who were at the least
weak-minded.
Died at Southport, the other day, John
Jardine, whose brothers Frank and Alex-
ander formed the first cattle station in York
Peninsula as far back as 3865. Their father
was Police Magistrate at Rockhampton, and
was appointed to found the settlement of
Somerset at the top end of Bananaland.
The two young men—one was 23 and the
other 20—resolved to establish a cattle-sta-
tion at Cape York, and set out from Rock-
hampton with a party consisting of six
white men and four blackboys. The last
300 miles of the trip had to be done on foot,
and when they got to the end of it, they had
lost nearly all their horses and more than
half of their cattle. The Peninsular blacks
gave them a let of trouble, and on one occa-
sion a pitched battle was fought. Thirty
Binghis were killed, and a good many more
were wounded. Frank and Alexander Jar-
dine are both living. The former is still at
the old Cape York station. The latter didn’t
stay there long, and subsequently joined
the Bananaland Harbors and Rivers Depart-
ment. John (deceased) was on the Cape
York station for some years, but had been
connected with a Brisbane firm lately.
E. M. Macartney, who got in for Too-
wong, the other day, largely on the Serip-
ture-in-State-schools racket, is a chip of the
old holy block. Dean Macartney, of Mel-
bourne, was his grandad, and the Dean, as a
member 6f the Public Library Committee,
moved arid carried, in the ’7o’s of the now
decomposed century, a motion that all “im-
moral” words and phrases in the works of
Shakspeare and other classic scribes should
be pasted over with goldbeater's skin. The
deed was done, too.
Letter received by Labor member Allen
from a friend in Java: —
One workman I saw in a factory recently was not
more than five years old. This mite revelled in the
Carnegie-like wage of twopence a day and free rice.
Tea-packers, mostly gills, make from Id. to 5d., and,
with free rice, live in a.Huence.
When Federal Ministers talk wildly of com-
mencing to sweep away nrotective duties, let
them think of that Javan lilliputian of five,
with his twopence a day—though even that
seems prodigal payment in a country where
the average full-grown and experienced
sugar-estate coolie gets no more than five-
pence.
THE POJEERS OF WOMAN.
BILL (painting): “ Who's kicking up all the row next door?"
DAN (also painting): “Sure, I dunno, ’Tis ayther 40 men or two women."
The Bananalnnd Government recently
avowed its intention of improving the con-
dition of the underpaid branches of the Pub-
lic Service. _ Next week the salary of Rail-
way Commissioner Evans was lifted from
£1750 to £2OOO, and one low-wage employee
had some improvement perpetrated upon
him.
Hugh Miles Milman, Government Resi-
dent at Thursday Island, with control over
Torres Strait and its islands, died at South-
port on Saturday, lie was the son of a
Devonshire baronet, the first of the Milman
barbflets being physician to George 111. The
Queensland sprig was born in 1845, entered
the navy in 1859, and was invalided in 1865.
Coming to Australia, he had some station ex-
perience on Peak Downs, and afterwards as
a. partner in Listowel Downs. He sold out
of that and joined J. R. Jardine. his brother-
in-law, in the purchase of Marlborough.
Drought and disease swamped them, and
Milman got a job as P.M., first at Aramac
and then at Cooktown ; and when old John
Douglas died he was provided with another
billet as Resident at Thursday Island. He
married Katherine Marde, a daughter of
John Jardine —and there was a baronetcy
wandering about in that family, too.
Ora Pro
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
It is disquieting to find that the practice of us'ng
the Prayer for Parliament, appointed in the Prayer
Look, has very largely heen dropped in .our
churches. We criticise Parliament freely, and vve
sometimes abuse it vehemently ; but we do not pray
for it. —Brisbane Church Chronicle.
Oh, let us pray for Parliament, whatever else we
miss,
And lift a kind petition to the Throne of Grace and
Bliss ;
For if we run this island through, from happy shore
to shore,
We cannot find a body here who want our praying
more.
Tile Tor}' crew are heaving lavish curses on the
Rads;
The Rads condemn the Tories as a set of arrant
cads ;
Each party vows the other one has reached the lowest
level,
And each believes the other’s driving matters to the
Devil.
The Land is riven by their strife from Torres to the
Leeuwin,
For each is sure the other’s aim is rapine red and
ruin ;
Each one’s accusing t’other of unscrupulous ambition,
Of trafficking in turmoil and of waltzing to sedition.
Says A.: “You’re only aiming at the spoiling of the
classes.”
Says B.: “You are but grinding down the poor and
lowly masses.”
And then says C. of A. and B. (with much of truth
about them) :
“The Country would get on as well or better far
without them.”
And he who calmly views the game with philosophic
focus,
Must wonder how it is that out of all this hocus-
pocus,
And vulgar bounce and diatribe, and dulness deep
and deadly,
The State can lift its head at all and breathe above
the medley.
So don’t forget to pray for him —the hapless poli-
tician ;
And let his name remembered be as oft as you peti-
tion ; ' -
And if, in spite of all your prayers, the Ileav’ns re-
fuse to aid him.
Remember he is just the man your suffrages have
made him.
QUEENSLAND.
F.B.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, September 23, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindah,—
Premieress Denham has been scoring again
for the Popularity Stakes. This time it
was a garden party of the Very Best People,
on Thursday, amidst her beautiful collection
of palms and other vegetables. Those who
were present agree that it was the most
successful entertainment of its kind since
the first lady entertained the serpent to
afternoon fruit in Eden ; those who were not
invited say—well, it doesn’t matter what they
say. Daughters Elsie and Winnie acted as
general managers of the commissariat, and
the star guests were Lady Mac and the
Vision, both garbed in the favorite amethyst.
On the same day the popular Mayoress of
Windsor also conducted a tea scramble for
notables. Scores of them attended and did
the tea proud.
The marriage of Hugh McWhirter Kid-
ston, this week, recalls the fact that he is
the proprietor of several medals from the
Humane Society, gained by diving into the
flooded Fitzroy (Rockhampton) and drag-
ging forth the apparently drowned. He was
a good deal cooler on that occasion than the
other day when he dived into his own pocket
for the gold ring. And he won’t get any
medals for it, either. That sort of thing is
the real heroism that passes unnoticed in
. this world of limelight.
I.ady Macgregor and the Vision lent their
moral support to the Flower Show this after-
noon but the weather’s unfriendly attitude
accounted for a poorly-followed" example.
Thp flowpr'! notable npau nnrl
orchids, were magnificent,’ and the straw-

No appointment lias yet been made to fill
in the gap caused by the departed Scarlett,
who, this morning, crept aboard the mail
train, and silently stole away. Tennis par-
ties, at Government House, have been many
and often during the week, and tile number
of society girls who have discovered a latent
enthusiasm for the game might surprise the
uninitiated and very unsophisticated. Until
the arrival of another Young Gentleman,
Captain Foxton will act as A.D.C.
The Mayoress having good cause to be
satisfied wjth the result of her “stand and
deliver propaganda for the Sick Children’s
Hospital, handed over a cheque worth about
£l6O to the president of that institution on
Tuesday last. Mrs. Cowlishaw said she was
pleased to get it, and not a living soul
doubted her. After transferring the cheque
the Didaams lady spread the glad tea and
strawberries. But interest in the hos-
pital does not seem to be thriving, and the
progressive element in the committee has a
feeling that it is trying to bore through the
stone wall of indifference with the penknife
of despair.
The Medical Congress has left us with
scarcely a doctor to share amongst us.
Health Commissioner Elkiugton, the Wil-
ton Loves, the Guilford Davidsons, and Dr.
Eleanor Bourne are in your State arguing
the point about germs and other public
nuisances.
The Brisbane Daily Mail is rapidly get-
ting wise to the trials of man. The other
day, in reviewing the many reasons' why the
mysterious death of a young man was not
due to suicide, the family journal stated
that he had no troublesI—not 1 —not even a sweet-
heart !
The boys of the Grammar School enter-
tained delighted mothers at a concert, the
other night, the principal items of which
were scenes from “The Rivals.” They cer-
tainly were wonderfully good, but what was
even better was the volley of advice given
in a free and generous manner by every
small boy in the building as to how Beverley
should make love to the fair Lydia. Mothers
looked startled at the experienced tone of
the remarks.
The Sydney bowlers have returned to
their Sydney wives with “the ashes” locked
away for safe keeping. Years ago our stout
men could beat anything at the game, but
it seems to be getting too strenuous, or they
may be getting too stout, or perhaps they
are dead, and only thin men play nowadays
—anyhow, our triumphs are not what they
were on the green that lies before the club
bar. Still, we’re good losers, which counts
for something.
The Botanic Gardens are becoming in-
creasingly popular, and children’s parties
tnere are the fad of the moment. A few
days ago Mrs. W. H. Moore made a small
grandson’s birthday the excuse to feed about
300 youngsters and a score or so of mothers;
and this afternoon Mrs. Frank Wilson, the
popular little wife of Dr. Wilson, shep-
herded another large flock of lambs. Also,
last week, Clinton, the home of the Deputy-
Governor Morgans, was overrun by a tem-
pestuous herd of children.
The “Gay Gordon” mummers and mum-
meresses took with them to Maoriland one of
our society warblers, to wit Ida Murphy
(daughter of the Hon. Peter), whose voice
has been dug up and sown and generally cul-
tivated by Steffani. Talking of voices, Alioe
Currie made a fine impression this evening.
She won her laurels, I understand, in sunny
Sydney under Mrs. Christian; none the less,
she is a Queenslander, and we like to re-
member the fact.
Yours affectionately,
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Kobbi” : Bananaland’s boom barometer :
Evans, Anderson and Phelan are the successful
tenderers for the construction of 20 goods engines of
the Cl 6 type. Price, £90,839.
The Government lias decided to throw open to
selection 32 portions, totalling 224,891 acres.
Brown and Brown, Brisbane, have been accepted
to build 100 timber waggons (£13,185) and 60
H waggons ( £8050).
Ipswich workshops are to build three Pullman
cars, valued £7500.
The only complaint I have to make is that,
out of £120,000 worth of work, the State
shops get only a paltry £7500 worth.
Petrie, M.L.A., got a chance last week to
bring on his motion in favor of “doing some-
thing”-—preferably in the way of a land
grant—for Queensland pioneers of 50 years
or over. But the House reckoned up the
land that the pioneers had already collared,
and decided that it was about up to the most
—or nearly the most —heavily mortgaged
country on earth to begin to do a bit for
posterity. When it came to a, vote, Petrie
could get only eight others to join their Ayes
to his. r
“Kobbi”: Notwithstanding Digby Den-
ham’s poetical allusion to “clear skies,” and
the sundry whoops about prosperity, Bana-
naland fears that it is up against a dry
time. Cairns (N.Q.), where it is alleged
youngsters are born with overcoats on, has
had to date 156.84 inches this year. But
only about a quarter inch fell in August,
and none, so far, (14/9/T1) in September.
The northern line, right even to Cloneurry,
shows the land on the fidget for dampness,
while cattle are already being hurried by
train to relief country. The west and south
west have been paragraphed frequently of
late re dry stretches. In Central Queens-
land, the householder, in between his jour-
neys to the nearest well, has for months been
observing the weather with the persistency
of Clement Wragge. Mount Morgan’s dams
are showing a big stretch of bank. And
Yeppoon (C.Q.), usually a very wet corner,
hasn't put up an umbrella for several
months, except when it put the thing up the
spout as superannuated goods.

The Wine of the Barley.
FOR countless generations Ale has been a favorite beverage.
It was drunk in Egypt 3000 years before the Christian
era; it was known and appreciated by the ancient Gauls;
it was the favorite liquor of the Anglo-Saxons and Danes, and
is still the premier drink of the most alert and active peoples of
the world. It was the drink of the lusty and strong, the
“doers” in the history of the past.
Cascade Ale, brewed by the Cascade Brewery Co., of Hcbart,
is akin to the ale of old England, for it is rich in the qualities that
promote health and strength. That its merits are appreciated by
those most competent to judge is proved by the rapidly-growing
sales in every part of the Commonwealth.
Sold at all first-class hotels and stores.

SYDNEY: C. D’Arcy & Co., 474 George Street.
MELBOURNE: Taylor, Ferguson & Co., 127 King Street.
BRISBANE: Burns, Philp & Co., Ltd., Brisbane.

ARE YOU
DEAF?
If you are deaf, or going deaf, you will
be glad to hear of a simple and scientific de-
vice which will restore your hearing, no mat-
ter how old you are. There is no mystery
or secrecy about it; we describe it fully and
explain just how it acts.
You use spectacles as sight magnifiers, and
in the same way you may use this sound
magnifier as an aid to weak hearing. But,
unlike spectacles, the Wilson Common-sense
Ear-Drums are invisible and .comfortable.
You can’t tell you are wearing them. No
one else can, either, because they are not
seen.
They rest the ear nerves by taking the
strain off them —the strain of trying to hear
dim sounds. What a blessed relief to have
done with that tiring strain!
Being made of the purest and softest Para
rubber, they cannot be felt, even when the
head rests on a pillow. For the same reason,
a blow on the side of the head will not hurt
the ear. In fact, they protect the ear, the
tender inner parts that are so sensitive, from
cold winds, dust, sudden and piercing sounds.
So that you may clearly understand why
these facts are true, and why the Sound Mag-
nifier restores the hearing, we give an illus-
tration of one as it. lies in the ear. You will
notice that the Sound Magnifier is shaped
like a trumpet, and so conveys even the
faintest sounds right to the natural ear-
drum. Being of the softest rubber, it cannot
fV
hurt the ear. It fits the orifice of the ear
with precision and comfort. The division
that runs across the mouth is to allow the re-
moval and insertion of the Ear-Drum, and to
brace it.
The shape of the Wilson Ear-Drum has
been chosen after years of experience as the
best adapted to carry the faintest sound right
down to the natural ear-drum.
To persons who are nearly deaf, it is not
only trying to have people shout at them,
but it hurts the ear-drum. When age comes
the ear-drum sometimes gets thickened, or
loses its tightness, and shouting does not act
properly on it. It only makes a confused
and tiring noise. But the Sound Magnifiers
concentrate the sound in the best spot for
perfect hearing.
Of course, if you have lost the hearing-
nerve completely, or were born deaf, we can-
not pretend to cure you. Only a miracle
could do that, in spite of some of the absurd
claims made to-day. But if you can hear the
faintest sound, do not despair. You will
never regret trying the Wilson Ear-Drums.
We have letters to show you from deaf people
in Australia and New Zealand who have been
cured, and you can be cured, too. You may
wear the Ear-Drums day and night, and take
them out whenever you wish. Children can
wear them (we have special sizes for children
from six to twelve). Even a child can insert
and remove them without the least danger.
The price is £1 (the fee for a doctor’s
single visit). This includes a pair of Drums
(they are only sold in pairs), a fine nickel
remover and also an inserter. After the first
pair is bough't you may purchase a single
drum at any time for Bs., but the pair you
get at first will last quite two years. Do not
ask for a trial pair. We do not send them
out on trial, and so they are never second-
hand. Use the Order Form below, and the
Outfit will be sent to you at once, with the
fullest instructions. Don’t delay, but have
the comfort of good hearing as soon as you
can.
ORDER FORM.

serter and a Remover, for which I enclose

“Fontaine”: Mostly the Australian youth
takes kindly to compulsory service; but one
of him, at least, has his own views on the
matter. A Melbourne area officer recently
got this note from one of his squad: “Dear
Sir, I beg to resign from the compulsory
military training.” The epistle, framed, now
adorns the walls of the orderly room.

CLEMENT-
BAYARD
Motor Cars
Speak for Themselves.
Extract of a letter from
the late Manager, “ Motor
Transit Company of Aus-
tralia,” Glen Inpes: “During
the time I was managing
‘The Motor Transit Co. of
Australia,’ we had plying
between Glen Innes and Inverell an 11-16 Clement-Bayard Oar, whicn we purchased as second-hand. We
did about 60,000 miles with her up to the time of my leaving (she is still going well). Nearly all the running
was done between Glen Innes and Inverell, over the famous Waterloo Range, some of the grades of which
are one in six. The Car was invariably overloaded, and was given very little care ; but in the face of the bad
usage she received at our hands she stood up wonderfully well, and the only repairs that were effected were
two ball races lor road wheels and a set of new valves.”
Call and allow us to explain the reason why our prices for Cars generally, Commercial Lorries and High-
wheel Buggies will be found to be 25 per cent, lower than the usual Sydney quotations.

A CIGAR is the BEST
SMOKE OF ALL!
But why can’t you get a
Good Cigar in the Country?
ALL over Australasia the only places you can
buy a really high-class Cigar is in the big
cities, In the country districts a good Cigar
cannot be had; because it’s only at the pubs that
you can buy Cigars at all in the out-back districts.
The publican knows that selling liquor pays him
better. He reckons that his customers only call
for a Cigar when they ve got a few drinks in and
want to dodge getting “fu’ the noo." Any old
Cigar does for that.
So he buys a few hundred at a time from
some “back-yarder"—real “Flor de Cabbage”
with big gold pictures all over the box and
mighty pretty bands round them—made from the
waste leaf and the cheapest growths, but called
by some high-falutin' name. (The making of these
Cigars is another story —I’ll tell you about it
another time.)
sdfdfdsfsf
Tbestorekeeperor tobac-
conist in country towns does
not sell good Cigars either.
If he does, he has to add to
the selling price of the Cigar,
the maker’s profit, the profit
of the warehouseman he
buys from, the profit of the
traveller who calls and sells
them to him, and his own
profit.
So that a good Cigar
that sells in the city at 6d.
he’d have to charge gd. for,
and not grow fat on it then,
because the number he would
sell would be small; and
good Cigars have to be kept
in a uniform temperature,
free from exposure to the
air, or else they’ll go off.
Those are just some of
the reasons why you can't
get a good Cigar in the
country parts of Australia,
and also why so very few
Cigars are at present smoked
in the country.
Buy a 100-box of my
" DIADEMAS,” smoke 5 or
6 of them, and, if they’re
not as good as any 6d.
Cigar you’veever smoked,
you needn’t pay a penny.
That's the way I’m in-
troducing my Cigars into
Australia. I know Cigar-
making from A to 7.. I know
these DIADEMAS Cigars of
mine are just the coolest,
smoothest and most luxu-
rious smoke any man could wish for. Cigar-
smokers of a lifetime say so; and the more you
know about a good Cigar the better you’ll like
them.
My DIADEMAS are made from the very
finest, mild, rich, mellow, J ava leaf, the filling or
inside heart of the Cigar being all straight long
Brazilian. The Cigars are hand made by skilled
workers, and every box has to pass me before it
goes out to a customer; and is guaranteed to
burn free and draw easily. The Cigar is 4 inches
long, and the picture at the side is a copy of it,
exact in size and shape.
Now, this DIADEMAS Cigar is as good as
any 6d. Cigar, sold in the cities by high-class
tobacconists, as I sell it direct to the customer —
saving the cost of wholesalers’, salesmen’s, and
retailers’ profit. I only charge you 22/6 the box
of too DIADEMAS, postage paid right to your
address.
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of too
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade. Please add exchange to country and
interstate cheques.
My Only Address is
LOUIS KAUFFMANN
34 QUEEN STREET, MELBOURNE

The Man of Little Faith.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WILLIE Torrence, the young accred-
ited clerk of Schinken and Over-
end, stockbrokers, of Draper’s
Gardens, London, stood in Throgmorton-
street, gazing for a moment after the re-
treating figure of Robert Laidley, of Paisley
—a man whose name was one of the strong-
est in the North.
“Queer mixture of business and religion
that,” said Torrence to himself, and, having
said it, immediately lost all interest in the
queer mixture, and became Schinken and
Overend’s clever, intuitive “half-book man”
again. Behind that frank countenance, lit
by very blue eyes that looked honesty, was
hidden an ambitious, supple mind. Out-
wardly, he was as frank as a schoolboy who
loved sport more than work; inwardly, his
environment had sharpened while it tainted
him. At the early age of 25 he shared big
commissions with his principals; his native
integrity, unconsciously to himself, warped
to regard all things—mines, railroads, bonds
and industries —as having but one utility,
that of the gambling counter.
The lust of the game had eaten to his
marrow, but behind the sporting instinct, and
soiling it, was the lust for quick riches. His
dreams were all of money easy gotten; on
the ’bus from Kensington in the morning;
iu the Tube at night; in his bath, and at his
meals, he day-dreamed of millions, won by a
turn of the market —the joy of a safe bear
in a stock that would sink a quarter a day,
from the moment he had sold and never call
for the payment of a difference ; the rapture
of holding all the shares in a 200,000 share
comnany, knowing that some mad millionaire
had beared 250,000 of them. Imagination
governed his arithmetic. To sell something
he had not overnight, and to wake next
morning and find he had netted £50,000; to
buy something he did not want, and imme-
diately after the purchase to find the whole
world clamoring tor it.
The dreams did not obsess him; they
made him the hungrier for sport, and the
keener when at work. He lost no oppor-
tunity. Schinken and Overend were well
pleased with him. His winnings mounted
steadily; he rarely made a mistake. For a
time he was satisfied ; then the gradual pro-
gression wearied him; tired of crawling, he
wanted to fly.
And one day came the opportunity, and
he called on Schinken and Overend for the
wings.
His information came from a reliable
quarter. Henry Nairn, a mining engineer,
who only considered the value of a mine in
appraising the value of its shares, was a fit
adviser for Torrence, who only considered
the market in appraising the value of any-
thing. Nairn and Torrence seemed a perfect
combination —the man of facts, and the man
of the market—w r hich is often utterly op-
posed to facts.
Nairn’s training and experience had made
first a scoffer and then a pessimist of him ;
he the consideration of the Swift
Snail Gold Mines with a joy that was almost
malevolent. “It’s a dead sure bear, Willie,"
he said. “I won’t put up a penny, or take
any liability, but I’m to have 15 per cent, of
the profits for my information and advice.
Is that a deal?”
“It is,” replied Torrence, his fair face
flushing with excitement, and they shook
hands.
“The mine is the Swift Snail,” said Nairn.
Torrence was frankly disappointed, and
looked it.
“Impossible,” he said. “Why, look at
raeir returns for the last year—every
numth going better. And the stocks at
laj.”
, ‘All very fine and large,” replied Nairn,
iheir crushings have been good, but they’ve
Picked out the eyes of the mine to do it.
And”—he looked around him, fearful of
being overheard, although they were alone
111 ( the little tea-room near Warnford Court
their developments are bad. Man—the
lower levels are not worth four penny-
weights.”
“But, Swift Snails,” again protested Tor-
rence, in a louder voice
“Ss-sh—Call ’em Crocodiles in future —
you never know who’s listening.” He
glanced suspiciously at the little brown lady,
who owned the tea-room, saw that she was
busy drawing threads from a tea-cloth, and
was evidently reassured by the sight.
“But,” said Torrence, in a voice full of
awe,, Huffman and Spink are behind it.
They re a terrible crowd to bump up
against.”
c Huffman and Spink keep on bulling
kw—Crocodiles, I mean—(and don’t say H.
and S.’s name any more —don’t think it
if they remain bulls long enough
they 11 be borrowing ’bus fares before six
months are out.”
‘‘But the reports were good, Nairn.”
.Book here, Willie, you know me. I know
the mine. I’ve been below, and I have a
man in the mine now. I tell you we’ve got
a , i.rtune in sight. Will you take it on, or
shall I find another man?”
« The decided belief of fortune in sight had
hred Torrence’s blood; the thought that
another man might dare more than he, and
Wl J} ove f his head, decided him.
,Im in it, Nairn,” he said.
Hood business 1 And if Schinken and
Uverend won’t join you?”
T’B do jt myself,” replied Terrence, full
ot fight and enthusiasm.
Bood man! Then it’s sure.”
There 11 be big differences to pay for a
time.
Don t you believe it. Sell ’em till the cows
come home. Make the bear determined
enough, and theydl come down with a run.”
Schinken and Overend had all a crab’s
cautiousness—and showed most bravery in
°I, a fleeing and disheartened
Torrence proposed a bear
fc>wift Snails, Mr. Schinken closed one
agg . e , d bls n ght ear, and then his head
shook in the negative. Mr. Overend closed
both eyes, smiled, and showed his estimation

Mr. Schinken waggled his ear again, and
Mr. Overgnd repeated his imitation of an
ascending and descending corkscrew.
“Have you any objection to my bearing it
on my own?” asked Torrence.
Mr. Schinken replied indulgently: “Not
der leesdt bit, Villie—young fellers vill haf
der vay. Of you vant to lose a few boundts
on a bear of fiftv or ein ’undert —veil so!
It is your own affair, but geep our book
glear of Swift Snailse.”
“a bear of fifty or a hundred!” said Tor-
rence to himself as he left them. “Fifty
thousand more likely. I’m a big bear, or
none at all. Stock stands at 15|, and is
worth probably I\. I’m in it. I see half a
million for Willie in this account.”
If be had any lingering fears that night,
they were dissipated by Nairn and pleasant
circumstances. Dinner at the Cafe Royal,
Melba in “Romeo et Juliette,” and supper
at the Carlton, made the outlook brilliant—
full of light, music, endless money, and om-
nipotent, omniscient youthfulness that should
never die.
* * * *
He opened his bear account with a Napo-
leonically-decisive daring, selling Swift
Snails by the hundred and the thousand. The
market absorbed the first three days’ sales,
and the price moved downward only a point
or two. Then a few small holders took
fright at this attack on their property—an
attack that had opened with the calm
audacity that knows it is preparing for a
long fight, but knows, also'', that it will win.
The small holders threw their shares in as
sellers. A few little bears —small, imitative
animals, who never have information or in-
itiative of their own, and do the thing they
see done by other and more important ani-
mals in the city menagerie—sold by the
fifty and the hundred ; saw the genuine sales
and the bogus sales absorbed and the market
rising; squealed, and bought in to cover
their sales—escaping with a loss of half a
crown a share. Torrence continued selling
until he had sold 15,000, and the market
rose a half. He gritted his teeth to the emer-
gency, and sold another 5000, and they were
taken before his offers were well out’of his
mouth. The stock rose against him by leaps
and bounds. He made his information pub-
lic, and the financial papers printed columns
of comments on the well-hidden truth of the
Swift Snail mines. The telegraphs began
their work, and 10,000 miles of cable pulsed
assertion and denial, making statements of
the utter worthlessness of the ore in the
lower levels of the Swift Snail, and .immedi-
ately contradicting them with reassuring re-
ports from the naturally impartial manager
of the mine. The balance of credibility was
with Torrence—yet the stock steadily rose.
Then the public lost interest, and the little
imitative bears withdrew from the arena, for
everybody knew that Huffman and Spink
were strong bulls. The bull and the bear
were left to fight it out. Everybody knew
that Torrence had gone too far to retreat,
and as every holder except Huffman and
Spink was now a seller, and the stock con-
tinued rising, and Torrence continued sell-
ing, it was patent that Huffman and Spink
had burned their boats also. A duel to the
death.
On the first account, Torrence’s liabilitv
for differences was roughly £25.000. He sold
an interest in the bear, but without liability,
for an advance against all other stocks he
held ; he mortgaged himself and future pro-
fits to Schinken and Overend; he overdrew
his bank account to a special limit; he per-
suaded his brother to mortgage his property
and his future, and he found the money.
The stock came back a little, and he
thought the tide had turned, and recom-
menced selling. But it was only Huffman
and Spink’s bluff to try his strength. He
sold another 5000 shares, and they put the
price higher than it had ever been.
“Hood Lord!” said Torrence to himself;
“only 10 days to the settlement, and they’ve
put the price up to over 17. At this rate I
have lost everything, and I'm mortgaged
ahead for £30,000. But I’m damned if I’ll
be beaten.”
Settlement day was very near —where was
he to find the difference?
The next mail answered him. It was a
letter from a friend and client —one Lane —
a registered letter and a key.
Dear Torrence, —I send you the key of my safe
deposit box. I am coming to London in a fortnight
or so. Will you please sell all the Grand Trunks,
Eries and Lackawannas at best. I’ve got a new
business offered me, that will mop up all of
£30,000. As you sell, take the stock from the safe
deposit, and pay your firm’s cheque into my account
with Smith’s.
New hope and sure fortune! Lane would
not want the money till he arrived. He need
not know when the stock was sold as long as
the money was ready when he wanted it. It
would be a little irregular, of course, to make
a temporary use of another man’s money,
but nobody would ever know, and it would
save him.
So at the next settlement Torrence paid
his differences, to the utter amazement of
Huffman and Spink.
“Tell you what, Jim.” said Spink ; “it’s no
use of Schinken and Overend saying they're
not in this. I expected Torrence to squeal
to-day, and instead, he pays up like the Bank
of England. They're behind him all right.”
Torrence's principals had alluded anxi-
ously to his Hreat Bear, and Torrence had
satisfied all parties by allowing them to
think that there was a great financial power
now standing behind him and supplying him
with unlimited money.
The mine, in the eyes of the public, was
now almost certainly a coming failure, but
the public was afraid of Huffman and Spink,
and held aloof from the struggle, and the im-
possible happened. No holder came forward
to relieve the market even at these artificial
high prices. But Torrence continued a sel-
ler, growing daily more anxious, but as full
of courage as at the beginning—nay, fuller,
because as the game became more desperate
he became cooler, knowing that if the worst
should come, it would not decrease his cour-
age, for then would come despair, and de-
spair fears nothing.
Five days before the third settlement Huff-
man and Spink became very bitter, and put
up the price to 25. They threatened to ruin
Schinken and Overend, and were much
amazed and secretly disquieted to observe
the child-like innocence of those two gentle-
men. The artless way Schinken waggled his
ear, and the maidenly simper of Mr. Over-
end as he described ascending and descending
spirals with his forefinger so oppressed them
that they snarled like cornered rats, and put
the price to 26.
At that figure Torrence sold them the last
thousand of the 45,000 that represented his
Bear. It was truly a colossal animal—it
sat upon his chest during nights of alternate
insomnia and nightmare, and in the daytime j

The Greater J. D. Williams Amusement Co.
Capital £200,000. Ui
it trotted beside him in Throgmorton-street,
and told him that by next settling day the
price of its maintenance would be £322,000.
Then a letter came from Lane asking for
sale notes, a-nd wondering why nothing had
been paid to his account, and that completed
the decay of Torrence’s youth. The misap-
propriation of Lane’s money put him outside
the pale of sympathy; he could open his
heart to none. The debt to his brother sun-
dered them; his use of Lane’s money kept
him from confiding in his mother. Nairn
was of no use in this strait. He had speci-
fically freed himself from liability in the
beginning, and even if he had not, he had no
money.
The life had gone out of Torrence—he was
horribly afraid. The marvellously youthful
color had left his face; even the youthful
contour had disappeared, making him grey,
old, haggard and wan. The long-drawn ten-
sion or the last 40 days had burred his
nerves; the anxiety and the fear of the week
to come obsessed him, so that the cant name
of the speculative seller, selling that which he
has not got, seemed to materialise itself. He
thought he heard the soft, cushiony foot-
falls of a great plantigrade on the pavement
beside him—the unmistakable sound of flesh
and fur on stone; he seemed to feel a cold
muzzle thrust into his hand, and a great,
red, lolling tongue licking his fingers.
“My God!” said Torrence. “This won’t
do. I’m going mad !”
He sought the little tea-room in Warn-
ford Court; ordered some tea, and while it
was preparing took up a paper and read it
listlessly. And the first thing his eye
chanced on was a police-court report headed
“A Bear in the Dock.” The connection made
him start, violently, lie read it again, and
saw that it was the report of a prosecution
of two Frenchmen who had blocked traffic
with a performing bear.
He threw down the paper impatiently, and
drank his- tea, and the man opposite him put
down his paper also, and said: “Mr. To-
rence!”
“How are you, Mr. Laidley?' asked Tor-.
rence, glad of any company, even of the com-
pany of the man of Paisley.
“I’m weel—but ye’re no lookin’ weel yer-
sel’ ?”
"I’m all right, but —you know—a big deal
always brings anxiety.”
“It is na gude—laddie, it is na gude,” said
Laklley, regarding him benevolently, or in-
quisitively—Torrence was not sure which.
“Will ye win througn wi’ it, laddie?”
asked Laidley, as if he were merely kindly
curious.
Glad of the relief of even a partial dis-
closure, Torrence answered almost eagerly:
“I think so —i in almost sure so. The
mine is in itself worthless —the shares are
not worth a pound.”
“But —laddie —they’re no’ ane ; they’re
nigher 30.”
“But they must come down with a run,
Mr. Laidley.”
“Aye! Aye! but will they fa’ doon in
time for ye?”
Torrence was silent—heart-sick with ap-
prehension.
“Laddie! They willna’ fa’ till ye ’re
broken. I ken Guffman an’ Spink.”
“Then I’m ruined.”
“What will save ye?”
The note of kindliness in his voice roused
Torrence to a new hope. He leaned over the
table, and said in a low voice, raspy with
emotion: —
“Oh ! Mr. Laidley—if you could help me.
Come in on the bear, and help me through
this account, and we’ll smash Guffman and
Spink.”
“What share would ye gie me?”
“A half —if that isn't enough—three-
quarters. There’s a million profit in it, if
we could hold, out to the end.”
“Laddie ! -Laddie ! ’Tis no' the business for
me. How much would clear ye?”
“Three hundred and twenty-two thousand
pounds.”
“O-o-o-o-o-h —e-e-e-e-e-e-e, man ! Dinna
speaii o’ so much at once. ’Tis disrespeckfu’
to money. A canna’ help ye, laddie.”
“Mr. Laidley, you can if you want to.
You'll not say you couldn’t get the money
on guarantee if the business was good.”
“Na—na—l willna’ say that. But the
business is na gude for me. So ye hae no
chance?”
“None! If the market doesn’t break in
three days—break to nothing—l’m broken.”
Laidley leaned across the table in his turn
and whispered: “Laddie, ha’ ye prayed?”
“Prayed !”
“Yes —prayed that the market may
break.’
Torrence laughed bitterly. “Is that your
.help? Prayed! Prayed for the market to
break. No! I’ve no time for that foolish-
ness.”
“Dinna’ ca’ it fulishness, laddie —I hae
prov’t it. Years syne I was a bull o’ Kelvin
Joint Stock Banks preferred. I bought four
thoosan’ o’ them at 19 —forbye I didna’
know that the bank had been caught heavy
in advances .to refinerries just before the
sugar smash. When I knew, the stock had
fa’en to saxteen, laddie—’twas a loss
o’ twal’ thoosan’ poonds. Laddie —I’m no
bearr. There are borrn bulls and borrn
bearrs. An’ I’m a borrn bull —an’ so are
you—laddie, I ken ye’re a borrn bull by ye’r
een. An’ ’tisna natural for a borrn bull to
be a bearr —Nature willna’ hae it. That’s
why the market for Swift Snails is ower 27.
.... Weel! I’m no’ a bearr; I canna’ bring
mysel’ to cut a loss. So I began to pray,
laddie —an’ the stock dropped to 15 —saxteen
thoosan’ poonds o’ a loss t’ me. I couldna’
cut the loss. then. I prayed, an’ I prayed—
man, I prayed powerfu’, an’ I sweated whiles
I prayed; an’ the stock lifted to 18, an’ then
dropped to 17i ; an’ I prayed it back again.
I went doon on my knees an’ wrestled wi’
the Spirit; an’ after three weeks of hearrt-
rendin’ prayer—prayer wi’oot ceasin’, laddie,
I liftit the stock to 18 an’ a hauf, an’ sold on
the turn o’ the marrket.”
“Was anybody praying against you?”
asked Torrence, his despair making him
ribald.
“Laddie —laddie—dinna’ jest wi’ sacred
things. An’ as soon as 1 sold oot an’ stoppit
prayin’, the stock cam’ doon to saxteen, an’
it hasna’ moved up since. Laddie! I canna’
help ye—but ye may help yersel’. Pray,
laddie—pray wi’oot ceasin’.”
“I’m no hand at praying, Mr. Laidley—
but if you will pray Swift Snails down to
10. I’ll give you £100,000.”
The Scotchman rose in offended dignity.
“Ye maun hae yer jest, laddie —Ah ! if ye
wull, ye’ll pay the price, o’t. ... I canna’ pray
for ye —I maun pray for my own stock.”
He held out his hand, and Torrence
clasped and held it, and the horror of his
position came to him.
“Mr. Laidley, you can help me. For Gods
sake, do help me. If I can’t pull through
this, I’m worse than ruined.”
“Worse than ruined, laddie?”
“Yes.”
Laidley came nearer, and loooked sternly
into the younger man’s eyes. '
“Ye hae dippit yer haun’ intil amther;
man's cash-box?” . ,
“Yes. For God’s sake, help me —it s quite
safe, and you’ll be half a million ahead on
the deal. Don’t say no, Mr. Laidley. lor
God’s sake —for m.y mother’s sake”— —
“Dinna’ greet aboot yer mither! Why
didna’ ye think before o’ her?” He wrenched
his coat from the despairing grasp ot the
younger man, who had caught at the lapel.
“I hae no maircy on the thief.' He
glared his hatred of all financial irregu-
larity. “Ye are na’ fit to pray.’
He walked away, his boots squeaking rec-
titude, and every wiry hair of his white,
correct Pharisaical beard bristling with in-
dignation. The door slammed behind him.
Torrence hid his face in his hands, and the
little brown woman found him so in the now
darkening tea-room, as she came to tell him
that her business hours were over and closing-
time come.
He roused himself with an effort and
took his hat; but as he walked to the door
after paying, the little brown woman said :
“It will be eighteenpence, please. The other
gentleman did not pay.”
Torrence smiled bitterly—the Pharisee
had saved ninepence by his indignation. The
ruined bear paid, and walked into Throg-
morton-street. At the Tube station he bought
a last edition, and read that Swift Snails
had closed strong at 274 buyer, and at that
he smiled again, as if nothing could hurt
him more.
He was thankful for the absence of his
mother in the country, and then surprised
that he could be thankful for anything.
There was everything in order in his own
special sitting-room in the luxurious flat. He
did not want any dinner, he explained to the
servant, who seemed surprised at his early
return, but he drank a bottle of champagne,
and felt his courage returning in response to
the genial influence of the wine.
He was almost cheerful as he opened his
letters. One of them was from Lane, de-
manding an immediate explanation; the
other a chatty letter from a cousin.
“May’s husband has sent such a queer pre-
sent from Australia. He says its proper
name is koala, but the people out there call
it the native bear. It’s such a queer-looking
thing—-it looked up at us with the wonder-
ing, steady look of a Child. We went to the
dock this afternoon and brought the bear
away.”
The bear in the dock again!
“Good Lord! I’m getting childish,” he
said aloud. lie lit a cigar and tried to
smoke, but his hand trembled ; tne power to
perform the usually simple act of pulling the
cigar had departed from him, and the cigar
went out.
He lit another match, and held it until it
burned his fingers. , Then he dropped it, but
without hurry or pain, and stared at the un-
lighted asbestos stove as if hynnotised. Hope
had absolutely gone. He preferred to have
it so ; wilfully disregarding the chances that
certainly did exist, of a recovery and
triumph at the eleventh hour; glad in his
numbed condition and unnaturally swift
acceptation of defeat to think that there was
no chance of recovery—no loophole of es-
cape ; persuading nimself that by settling-
day Huffman and Spink would put the price
to 30 —35 —anything. And Lane was on his
way to London —not the boyishly-affectionate
Lane of old with his “How goes it, old
man?” and his cheery slapping of the shoul-
der ; but a grim, revengeful Lane, hungry
for his Fries and his Lackawannas, or the
£30,000 that represented them.
“The Bear in the Dock.”
The brilliant, electric light seemed dim-
mer ; the ebony elephants from Colombo that
ornamented a cabinet were not elephants,
but bears; the pattern of the carpet, the
figures on the walls changed to grizzlies
that shambled on their soft pads towards
him—their months open in humorously hun-
gry fashion. The great plantigrade whose
soft footfalls he had hi ard beside him in
Throgmorton-street. rolled against him—he
felt the rasp-rough tongue upon his down-
hanging hand.
“Damn Huffman and Spink and Nairn!
Damn Swift Snails and Schinken and Over-
end ! Damn Lane! Damn everything.”
The Pharisee of Paisley checked his stride
near Winchester House. His hatred of all
flagrant dishonesty—all financial irregu-
larity—was gradually replaced by the con-
centration of his business instincts on the
Swift Snail’s position.
He decided quickly, turned back to Cop-
thall Avenue, and stopped at the office of
Huffman and Spink. He smiled as he saw
that the place was still lighted—he knew
that to Vtuiiman and Spink the position was
too serious for them to be in a hurry to
leave.
Five minutes’ study of them convinced
him. He sought a broker —a man of un-
questionable solidity—and he sold a bear of
2000 shares at 274.
That night he prayed, but before sleep
came to him he had time to say to himself:
“Praying is verra sairveceable, but ’tis weel
to s >ii at the top o’ the market.”
Next day there was more reason for
prayer, for Huffman and Spink put the price
up to 29, and there were but two days to
the settling. So Laidley prayed.
Then genuine sellers came into the market
by scores, and Huffman and Spink bought
still. And the day after the market broke —
not by points, but ,with a run! Huffman
and Spink were ruined. At the last moment
the source of their advances had closed
them up. The whole world wanted to sell
Swift Snails. It nad a fierce joy in the
position; the weak never forget the strong
who have made them fear.
Laidley did not close his bear. account
until months later, and when he did he was
£45,000 richer.
But on the morning of the day Huffman
and Spink broke there was a wild man
named Lane heaping curses on the memory
of a suicide; and Willie Torrence lay back
in his arm-chair as if wearied —and in his
brain a bullet from a revolver fired nine
hours before the market fell away to the
weakness of water.
“The fool!” said Nairn. “If he had
waited till afternoon.”
“He jested at sacred things,” said Laidley,
as he read of the tragedy. “He liadna’
muckle faith.” ■ .. .
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
